Correct Questions

CORRECTIn the moment when the man on the left briefly rubs his face while laughing, which side of his face does he touch with his right hand? (answer: C)
Stage 1 Summary
The planner did well by using a single focused siglip query—"man laughing and rubbing his face with right hand"—that directly described the distinctive visual action in the question rather than generic soccer or interview context. That was effective because the key evidence is a brief gesture, and the retrieved frames include the relevant interview/laughing moment where the man on the left raises his right hand to the left side of his face, which is exactly what is needed to choose C. The successful strategy was action-centric visual retrieval: target the unique pose and interaction instead of relying on object/entity queries.
Stage 2 Reinforcement
The success came primarily from the prompt's emphasis on locating visually distinctive evidence rather than answering directly. Several parts of the current prompt support this: (1) 'Locate, don't answer' pushed the planner to target the exact moment/gesture instead of reasoning over choices prematurely. (2) The siglip guidance to describe what the scene looks like—especially actions and visual states—fit this question perfectly, since the answer depends on a brief hand-to-face motion. (3) 'Focus on the most visually distinctive feature — rare details beat generic descriptions' strongly encouraged using the unusual action 'laughing and rubbing his face with right hand' instead of broader context like soccer interview or press room. (4) 'Break complex scenes into separate queries across tools' implicitly allowed the planner not to add unnecessary tren queries when the action itself was the best locator. (5) 'Use answer choices wisely' helped avoid wasteful per-option queries, because all choices referred to the same scene and differed only in fine-grained interpretation that the answerer could resolve once the right frames were found. (6) The instruction to keep siglip queries concrete and visual discouraged abstract phrasing and led to an action-centric query that matched the needed frames well.
CORRECTIn the moment after the yellow slug advances, what object is the skull-wearing creature shown holding? (answer: A)
Stage 1 Summary
The planner did well by anchoring retrieval on the key subject with the tren query 'skull-wearing creature' and then pairing it with visual similarity queries for each answer-choice object, especially the effective match 'skull-wearing creature holding short arrow.' The retrieved frames are relevant because they consistently show the skull-wearing red creature in the surrounding moments, including clear views where it is holding a small arrow-like object, which lets the answerer directly compare against the alternatives. This worked because the strategy combined entity grounding with choice-conditioned visual queries, narrowing retrieval to the right character and the specific held object.
Stage 2 Reinforcement
The successful plan follows several core prompt elements closely. First, the 'Locate, don't answer' rule pushed the planner to search for the visual evidence frame rather than infer the answer from language. Second, 'Use all information' and 'Use answer choices wisely' clearly contributed: the planner extracted the key entity from the question ('skull-wearing creature') and then converted each visually distinct option into its own search query ('holding short arrow,' 'holding round shield,' etc.). Third, the tool guidance was used correctly: tren was used for the distinctive entity anchor ('skull-wearing creature'), while siglip was used for scene/object-state descriptions involving the creature holding different items. Fourth, the instruction to focus on 'the most visually distinctive feature' helped because the skull-wearing character is an unusually identifiable subject, making retrieval robust. Fifth, the combining guidance worked well: using an anchor entity query AND a union over option-specific visual scenes narrowed results to the correct character while allowing retrieval of whichever held object actually appears. Finally, the prompt's recommendation to break complex scenes into separate queries across tools is exactly what happened here, and that decomposition made the plan effective.
CORRECTIn the labeled food shot, which exact phrase is associated with the portion description? (answer: B)
Stage 1 Summary
The planner did well by turning each answer choice into a concrete visual scene query, especially the effective soup-related query ('close-up of soup with no cheese sauce'), which matches the labeled food shots shown in the retrieved frames. The gatherer then surfaced frames containing the exact on-screen portion label text about the soup dish, including the frame that reads 'Entire Portion SOUP Only - No "Cheese" Sauce,' making the correct choice directly verifiable. This worked because the planner used an answer-choice-grounded OR strategy over distinctive food appearances, increasing the chance of retrieving the labeled comparison frame needed for the final decision.
Stage 2 Reinforcement
The success came primarily from the prompt's guidance to use answer choices wisely and convert visually different options into separate searches. In this case, each choice described a different food scene ('pasta,' 'soup,' 'salad,' 'stew,' 'rice') plus a different visible state ('extra cheese sauce,' 'no cheese sauce,' 'dressing on side,' 'no toppings'), so the planner followed: 'Use all information' and 'Use answer choices wisely. Visually different choices → search for each.' The prompt also reinforced concrete scene-level querying through the siglip instructions: 'Describe what the scene LOOKS LIKE' and 'Keep siglip queries concrete and visual.' That directly supported queries like 'close-up of soup with no cheese sauce,' which matched the retrieved labeled food shots. The OR combination was also enabled by the combining guidance: 'OR = union. Different scenes, or different queries that might each find what you need.' Finally, the OCR section mattered indirectly: 'OCR runs automatically... Always write visual queries to locate the scene, even if the answer is about on-screen text.' The planner did not try to search for the label text itself, but instead found the labeled food frames visually, allowing the answerer to read the exact phrase from the retrieved frame.

Stage 2 Reinforcement
The query succeeded because the prompt strongly pushes the planner to locate the scene rather than solve the question. Several parts of the prompt contributed directly: (1) 'Locate, don't answer' encouraged anchoring on the moment with the girl and rope instead of querying wall colors prematurely. (2) The siglip guidance — 'Describe what the scene LOOKS LIKE' and 'focus on settings, actions, spatial layout, object attributes, visual states' — supports a concrete scene query like 'smiling girl holding blue jump rope.' (3) 'Focus on the most visually distinctive feature — rare details beat generic descriptions' clearly favors the unusual combination of a smiling girl plus a blue jump rope, which is much more searchable than a generic indoor wall. (4) 'Use all information' helped extract the key visual details from the question itself: smiling girl and blue jump rope. (5) 'Use answer choices wisely' likely prevented unnecessary separate queries for each wall color, since the options describe the same scene and differ only in an attribute the answerer can inspect once the correct frames are found. (6) The OCR section also reinforces the general strategy of finding the relevant scene first and letting the answerer inspect the frame for the requested detail.

The successful strategy was using an AND between the distinctive menu scene and an OR over candidate character descriptions, which anchored retrieval to the right moment while disambiguating among the answer options.
Stage 2 Reinforcement
The success came directly from several parts of the prompt working together. First, the core instruction 'Locate, don't answer' pushed the planner to search for the moment visually instead of trying to infer the answer from the options alone. Second, the 'Use all information' and 'Use answer choices wisely' rules clearly encouraged extracting both the anchor scene from the question ('weapons menu on screen' and 'hand gesturing toward options') and the visually distinct candidate appearances from the choices, which is why the planner created one menu-scene query plus separate character queries for each option. Third, the tool guidance was used correctly: siglip handled the scene/action/layout query ('weapons menu on screen with hand gesturing toward options'), while tren handled short entity-focused character descriptions like 'soldier with face paint holding machine gun.' Fourth, the 'Combining queries' section strongly contributed: using AND to anchor the retrieval to the specific menu moment, and OR across the alternative answer-choice character appearances, is exactly the pattern the prompt recommends for a scene with multiple possible visually distinct outcomes. Finally, the prompt's emphasis on focusing on the 'most visually distinctive feature' helped because both the gesturing-hand-over-menu scene and the unusual soldier appearance with face paint and ammo belts were highly discriminative cues.

Incorrect Questions

KEEPWhen the first speaker is on screen, which detail best describes her hair? (correct: D, predicted: A)
Stage 1 Triage Reason
The planner anchored retrieval on the answer choices and a vague/likely ineffective tren query for 'first speaker,' which led to mostly interview shots of other people rather than the actual first female speaker. Better temporal/visual queries targeting the first on-screen woman and her hairstyle could have retrieved the relevant frame and likely changed the answer.
Stage 2 Diagnosis + Suggestion
Diagnosis:
The prompt overemphasizes Rule 3/4 ('Use all information' and 'Use answer choices wisely') in a way that pushed the planner to search for every hair-description option directly, instead of locating the actual target scene: the first speaker on screen. For this question, the answer choices are not different scenes; they are mutually exclusive attribute hypotheses about the same person. The examples also reinforce option-by-option querying for visually distinct choices. That led to weak queries like tren:'first speaker' (not a visible entity) plus siglip queries for each hair type, which retrieved various people from the video who happened to match those hairstyles, including an older gray-haired speaker, causing the answerer to pick A.
Suggestion:
Add a minimal clarification under 'Use answer choices wisely' (Rule 4): 'If the choices are alternative attributes of the SAME subject in the SAME scene (e.g., hair, clothing, age, color), do NOT make one query per option. Instead, query to locate the referenced subject/scene only, using role/order cues as temporal anchors when possible.'
KEEPWhile the male anchor is seated on the right side of the desk, which combination best matches his outfit? (correct: C, predicted: )
Stage 1 Triage Reason
The planner failed completely: it produced no siglip or tren queries and no combine expression, so the gatherer had no meaningful way to retrieve relevant frames. With basic queries targeting a news desk scene and the male anchor on the right, the pipeline likely could have retrieved answerable frames showing his suit, tie, and shirt.
Stage 2 Diagnosis + Suggestion
Diagnosis:
The planner produced no queries because the prompt creates a conflict for appearance-only questions like this one. Rule 3 says to use every visually searchable detail from the question and answer choices, and Rule 4 says visually different choices should be searched for each. But this question differs only by fine-grained clothing attributes on the same person in the same scene: suit color, tie color/style, shirt color. The examples emphasize scene-finding, and there is no explicit instruction for cases where the answer choices are alternative attribute combinations of a single subject. That likely made the planner hesitate between 'search each option' and 'same scene described differently,' resulting in an empty plan instead of locating the anchor-at-desk frame and using appearance cues.
Suggestion:
Add one sentence to the 'Use answer choices wisely' rule: 'If the choices are different appearance/attribute combinations of the same person or object in the same scene (e.g., clothing colors, tie style, object color), do NOT search each option separately; write queries that locate that subject and scene using stable visual anchors, and let the answerer compare the attributes in the retrieved frames.'

Second, the tool guidance was applied correctly: 'tren — Object and entity search' with 'Short noun phrases only' led to the clean anchor query 'older woman', while 'siglip — Visual similarity search' for settings/actions/layout led to 'apples being wrapped in plastic on counter,' which captures the key visual event. Third, the rule 'Use all information' encouraged extracting both the person reference ('older woman on the left') and the distinctive action/time cue ('apples being wrapped in plastic on the right side of the counter'). Fourth, the query design guidance to focus on 'the most visually distinctive feature' helped select wrapping apples in plastic, which is much more discriminative than generic kitchen activity. Fifth, the combining guidance for AND — 'Scene has multiple distinctive elements — one query each' — directly supported the effective conjunction Q1 AND Q2, which localized both the correct actor/context and the precise moment.

[7] Q: In the clip where the large dumbbell is being handled, what color combination stands out in the lifter’s clothing?
    Reinforcement: The successful plan follows several prompt elements directly. First, 'Locate, don't answer' pushed the planner to target the clip visually rather than infer the clothing from the options, which led to the strong scene query 'person lifting large dumbbell.' Second, the tool guidance was used correctly: siglip for the action/scene ('person lifting large dumbbell') and tren for specific entities and option attributes ('lifter', shirt colors, shorts colors). Third, 'Use all information' and 'Use answer choices wisely' clearly helped: the planner extracted the distinctive event from the question and translated the answer choices into visual clothing attributes, which are highly searchable. Fourth, the query design guidance to focus on the most visually distinctive feature encouraged anchoring on the rare event of handling a large dumbbell rather than generic gym footage. Fifth, the combining guidance also mattered: intersecting the scene anchor with the person query and then using OR across answer-choice-specific clothing combinations is exactly the kind of structured narrowing the prompt recommends.

[8] Q: Which pair of wall patterns is visible on the walls of the large building with towering columns and tall windows?
    Reinforcement: The success came from several prompt elements working together. First, the core instruction 'Locate, don't answer' pushed the planner to search for the scene containing the evidence rather than infer the answer from the wording. Second, the 'Use all information' and 'Use answer choices wisely' rules directly encouraged extracting both the anchor scene from the question ('large building with towering columns and tall windows') and the visually distinct wall-pattern alternatives from the choices. Third, the tool guidance for siglip was important: 'Describe what the scene LOOKS LIKE' and 'focus on the most visually distinctive feature — rare details beat generic descriptions.' That led to concrete visual pattern queries like 'sunflower motif and smaller circles beneath' instead of abstract paraphrases. Fourth, the 'Combining queries' section clearly supported the winning structure: an AND anchor for the building scene plus an OR over the candidate pattern scenes, exactly matching 'Scene has multiple distinctive elements' and 'Visually different choices → search for each.' Finally, the instruction to write reasoning first likely helped the planner identify that the key retrieval problem was to localize the building and then discriminate among multiple visually different wall motifs.

[9] Q: In the moment where he pours the sauce-like ingredient into the skillet, what color is that ingredient?
    Reinforcement: The main driver was the prompt's repeated instruction to locate the relevant frames rather than answer the question. In this case, that pushed the planner away from querying candidate sauce colors or ingredient identities and toward the visually decisive event: the pouring moment. Several prompt elements supported this: (1) 'Locate, don't answer' in Rules, which encourages retrieving the scene where the evidence is visible; (2) the siglip guidance to describe what the scene looks like using actions and visual states, which fits 'person pouring liquid into skillet' exactly; (3) 'focus on the most visually distinctive feature' and 'rare details beat generic descriptions,' which made the pouring action into a skillet the best anchor; (4) 'Keep siglip queries concrete and visual. Avoid abstract or narrative language,' which favors a direct action description instead of speculative ingredient wording; and (5) 'Use answer choices wisely... Same scene described differently -> one query, let the answerer decide,' which is especially important here because all options are just different color descriptions of the same poured substance. The planner correctly recognized that the answer choices did not require separate searches, only retrieval of the exact moment where color is visible.

[10] Q: During the nearly upside-down moment, which word can be clearly made out on the parachute?
    Reinforcement: The query succeeded because several parts of the prompt pushed the planner toward locating the visual scene rather than trying to answer the OCR-style content directly. Most important were: (1) the OCR guidance: 'OCR runs automatically... Always write visual queries to locate the scene, even if the answer is about on-screen text'; this encouraged searching for the parachute state instead of the candidate words. (2) The tool guidance for siglip: 'Describe what the scene LOOKS LIKE' and use it for 'settings, actions, spatial layout, object attributes, visual states'; this directly supports a query like 'parachute upside down with visible text,' centered on the distinctive visual condition. (3) The rule 'Locate, don't answer' prevented choice-word search and kept focus on retrieving the right frames. (4) The rule 'Use all information' helped incorporate the key scene cue from the question: the 'nearly upside-down moment.' (5) The instruction to focus on 'the most visually distinctive feature — rare details beat generic descriptions' explains why the planner used the unusual inverted-parachute state rather than generic paramotor/paragliding queries.

[11] Q: When the weapons menu is on screen and a hand is gesturing toward the listed options, what is visible on the left side of the screen?
    Reinforcement: The success came directly from several parts of the prompt working together. First, the core instruction 'Locate, don't answer' pushed the planner to search for the moment visually instead of trying to infer the answer from the options alone. Second, the 'Use all information' and 'Use answer choices wisely' rules clearly encouraged extracting both the anchor scene from the question ('weapons menu on screen' and 'hand gesturing toward options') and the visually distinct candidate appearances from the choices, which is why the planner created one menu-scene query plus separate character queries for each option. Third, the tool guidance was used correctly: siglip handled the scene/action/layout query ('weapons menu on screen with hand gesturing toward options'), while tren handled short entity-focused character descriptions like 'soldier with face paint holding machine gun.' Fourth, the 'Combining queries' section strongly contributed: using AND to anchor the retrieval to the specific menu moment, and OR across the alternative answer-choice character appearances, is exactly the pattern the prompt recommends for a scene with multiple possible visually distinct outcomes. Finally, the prompt's emphasis on focusing on the 'most visually distinctive feature' helped because both the gesturing-hand-over-menu scene and the unusual soldier appearance with face paint and ammo belts were highly discriminative cues.
Stage 3 LLM Output
The failures share one clear pattern: the planner over-applies “use answer choices” to attribute-only questions where all options describe the same subject in the same scene. In those cases, searching each option pulls in wrong matches and can even cause the model to produce no plan because the prompt gives conflicting signals. The success cases strongly validate the current core behavior: retrieval-first planning, the siglip/tren tool split, focusing on distinctive visual anchors, and using option-by-option queries only when choices imply genuinely different scenes or visually distinct outcomes. So the best fix is a minimal clarification to Rule 4, plus a small tightening of Rule 3 so “use all information” does not imply “always query every option.”

<revised_prompt>
You are a search planner for a video question-answering system. Given a question and answer choices, write queries for specific search tools that LOCATE the relevant frames. A separate answerer model will look at those frames and determine the correct answer — you do NOT answer the question yourself.

## Tools

**siglip** — Visual similarity search.
  - Describe what the scene LOOKS LIKE: settings, actions, spatial layout, object attributes, visual states.
  - Cannot read text. Never include on-screen text in siglip queries.
  - Bad: "sign reading Exit Here" → Good: "hallway with illuminated signs"
  - Bad: "someone is happy" → Good: "person smiling and clapping"

**tren** — Object and entity search.
  - Short noun phrases only. ONE entity per query.
  - Good at finding specific objects or people by appearance.
  - Bad: "person picking up a red mug from the table" → Good: "red mug"
  - Bad: "cat and dog" → Good: two separate queries, "cat" and "dog"

For both tools, focus on the most visually distinctive feature — rare details beat generic descriptions.

## Query design

- Break complex scenes into separate queries across tools. 
- Keep siglip queries concrete and visual. Avoid abstract or narrative language.
- Keep tren queries to short noun phrases. ONE entity per query.

## Combining queries (1-5 queries per plan)

- **AND** = intersection. Scene has multiple distinctive elements — one query each. Never AND queries that describe the same thing differently.
- **OR** = union. Different scenes, or different queries that might each find what you need.

## OCR

OCR runs automatically on every question — you do NOT need to handle it. Always write visual queries to locate the scene, even if the answer is about on-screen text or subtitles. The answerer model can read text directly from the frames you find.

## Rules

1. **Locate, don't answer.** Find the scene; the answerer decides what's happening.
2. **Always output at least one query.** Every question has a visual scene to find.
3. **Use all information.** Extract every visually searchable detail from the question and answer choices, but only turn details into queries if they help locate the target frames.
4. **Use answer choices wisely.** Visually different choices that imply different scenes or different visible outcomes → search for each. If the choices are alternative attributes of the SAME subject/object in the SAME scene (e.g., hair, clothing, age, color), do NOT make one query per option; query to locate that subject/scene and let the answerer compare the attributes.
5. **Right tool:** siglip for scenes, actions, layout, visual states. tren for specific objects or people. Use both when you need both.

## Question

{question}

Options:
{options}

Video duration: {duration}s encoded at {fps} fps.

You MUST first write 1-3 sentences of reasoning before the JSON block. Think about: what must be visually true about the frames that contain the answer? What is the most distinctive element to search for? Do the answer choices point to different scenes or the same scene? Never output the JSON block without reasoning first.

Then output a JSON block:
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "siglip", "query": "...", "id": "Q1"}}], "combine": "Q1"}}
```

Fields per query: "tool", "query", "id" (Q1, Q2, ...).

Examples:

---

Question: What does the woman in the red dress do after picking up the book from the table?
Options: A) places it on the shelf B) hands it to the man in glasses C) sits down on the couch and reads D) puts it in her bag E) walks out of the room

The question mentions a woman in a red dress, a book, and a table. The choices describe different actions after picking up the book — each would look different visually. I'll find the woman, the book, and search for the distinct scenes from each choice.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "woman in red dress", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "book", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person placing book on shelf", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person handing book to someone", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person sitting on couch reading", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "(Q1 AND Q2) AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

---

Question: What color is the vehicle that the man in the construction vest walks toward after crossing the street?
Options: A) red B) blue C) white D) black E) yellow

The question mentions a man in a construction vest and crossing a street. The choices are all vehicle colors — visually distinct. I'll find the man and search for each colored vehicle near a street.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "man in construction vest", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person crossing street toward vehicle", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "red vehicle", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "blue vehicle", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "white vehicle", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "Q1 AND Q2 AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

---

Question: In which room does the child first play with the wooden blocks?
Options: A) the kitchen B) the living room with the blue rug C) the bedroom D) the hallway E) the backyard

The question mentions a child and wooden blocks. The choices are different rooms, each visually distinct. I'll find the child and the blocks, and search for each room.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "child", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "wooden blocks", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "child playing in kitchen", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "living room with blue rug", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "child playing in bedroom", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "(Q1 AND Q2) AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

---

Question: What is shown on the display screen when the man in the blue jacket is standing at the podium?
Options: A) a bar chart B) a photo of the team C) the company logo D) a world map

The question asks about what appears on a display during a specific scene. OCR handles text automatically, so I need to find the scene visually — the man in the blue jacket at the podium with a display screen.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "man in blue jacket", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person standing at podium with display screen", "id": "Q2"}}], "combine": "Q1 AND Q2"}}
```

---

Question: What is the name of the restaurant shown on the sign outside the building?
Options: A) Mario's B) The Golden Fork C) Sushi Palace D) Burger Barn E) Cafe Luna

The question asks about text on a sign outside a building. I need to find the building exterior with the sign — the answerer will read the text.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "siglip", "query": "building exterior with restaurant sign", "id": "Q1"}}], "combine": "Q1"}}
```

Correct Questions

CORRECTWhile the Samsung.com page is visible in the lower-right browser, which trio is part of the company links shown there? (answer: E)
Stage 1 Summary
The planner did well by anchoring retrieval with Q1, which targets the distinctive layout of the tablet screen with a Samsung.com browser visible in the lower-right; this pulled frames showing the exact multi-window browser scene needed for the question. The option-specific visual queries then worked as verification cues, and the retrieved frames are relevant because they include the Samsung perks/company-links page where names like SiriusXM, Audible, and Webex are visible. The successful strategy was a two-step visual intersection: first find the right scene context, then discriminate among answer choices by matching the company-link text/logos shown in that browser pane.
Stage 2 Reinforcement
The success came from several prompt elements working together. First, the OCR guidance was crucial: 'OCR runs automatically... Always write visual queries to locate the scene, even if the answer is about on-screen text or subtitles.' This pushed the planner to avoid trying to answer from the company names directly and instead anchor on the visible scene layout. Second, the right-tool guidance for siglip — 'Describe what the scene LOOKS LIKE' and 'siglip for scenes, actions, layout, visual states' — directly supported Q1, which used the distinctive multi-window tablet layout with the Samsung.com browser in the lower-right corner. Third, the rule 'Use all information' helped incorporate the key spatial cue 'lower-right browser,' which made retrieval precise. Fourth, 'Use answer choices wisely' contributed by turning the different answer options into alternative visually checkable outcomes, since the options implied different visible sets of company names/logos in the browser pane. Fifth, the combining guidance for AND/OR was used effectively: Q1 anchored the target scene, while '(Q2 OR Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)' acted as discriminative verification among alternative visible company-link sets. Incorrect Questions

KEEPWhen the green convertible is first shown, how many passengers are in it? (correct: C, predicted: B)
Stage 1 Triage Reason
The planner anchored retrieval on a 'green convertible,' but the relevant first appearance seems to be a yellow/orange open car and the actual green car shown later is not a convertible. Better queries targeting the first shown open-top greenish car and passenger counting at first appearance likely would have retrieved the correct frame and changed the answer.
Stage 2 Diagnosis + Suggestion
Diagnosis:
The prompt’s Rule 4 says not to make one query per option when the choices are alternative attributes of the same subject in the same scene, but the provided examples repeatedly do exactly that for count/color/location alternatives. That contradiction likely taught the planner to generate answer-choice-specific queries like 'green convertible with two passengers' and '...with three passengers.' For this question, passenger count is the attribute to be determined after locating the first appearance of the same car, not a good retrieval cue. Those count-specific siglip queries biased retrieval toward ambiguous or later frames and did not emphasize the key temporal requirement 'first shown.'
Suggestion:
Make a minimal edit in the 'Use answer choices wisely' rule to explicitly include counts and temporal qualifiers: 'If the choices are alternative attributes of the SAME subject/object in the SAME scene (e.g., hair, clothing, age, color, number/count of people or objects), do NOT make one query per option; query only to locate the subject/scene. If the question asks about first/last/before/after, include queries that localize that event rather than the answer choices.' This directly strengthens Rule 4 and would discourage option-specific passenger-count queries here.
KEEPWhen the clip shows stations tailored to individual performers, which of these names appears in that same artist-focused set? (correct: A, predicted: E)
Stage 1 Triage Reason
The planner queries are poorly grounded in the actual visual evidence: asking tren to detect celebrity names is unlikely to retrieve readable on-screen station text, and the siglip query is too generic. Better OCR/text-focused visual queries like 'radio station list with artist names' or specific station-name text retrieval would likely have surfaced the relevant artist-focused set and the correct name.
Stage 2 Diagnosis + Suggestion
Diagnosis:
The prompt's 'Use answer choices wisely' rule tells the planner to search each option when choices imply different visible outcomes/scenes, and the examples reinforce per-option querying even for text-dependent questions. Here, however, the names are not visual objects or people in the video scene—they are on-screen text inside a UI/list. Because OCR is automatic, the planner should have located the specific artist-focused station screen, not issued tren queries for celebrity names. The 'OCR runs automatically' section says text need not be handled, but it does not explicitly forbid turning text answer choices into tren queries, so the planner followed the stronger pattern from the answer-choice rule/examples and retrieved irrelevant frames.
Suggestion:
Add one sentence to the OCR section (or Rule 4): 'If the answer choices are words/names that would appear only as on-screen text (e.g., menu items, station names, labels, subtitles), do NOT make one query per option and do NOT use tren for those names; instead query only for the surrounding visual UI/scene that contains the text.'
DISCARDWhat happens immediately after the surfer rides the wave until it collapses? (correct: A, predicted: C)
Stage 1 Triage Reason
The planner included a direct query for the correct post-event scene ('surfer ascending smaller wave and gliding toward shore'), and the retrieved frames actually show that sequence. The wrong answer is more likely due to answerer misinterpretation of the retrieved evidence rather than poor query planning.

Fifth, the combining guidance "AND = intersection" and "OR = union" directly led to the effective structure: scene anchor AND any candidate label, which narrowed retrieval to the correct menu while allowing any listed perk label to surface matching frames. Finally, the instruction to focus on the most visually distinctive feature helped because "soldier on the left" is a highly discriminative layout cue for this menu.

[8] Q: When the wording on the later item can be made out, which two-word phrase is actually visible?
    Reinforcement: The success came mainly from the OCR guidance plus the 'locate, don't answer' framing. The prompt explicitly says OCR runs automatically and that the planner should write visual queries to locate frames even when the answer is about on-screen text. That pushed the planner toward a broad visual retrieval query aimed at legible text rather than trying to infer the answer from the options. The requirement to focus on what must be visually true about the answer frames also helped: the crucial property was not a specific object brand or product type, but that a later item appears with wording large enough to read. The siglip tool guidance also contributed, since 'display screen with readable text' is a scene-level visual description appropriate for locating frames with prominent text.

[10] Q: In the center visualization, what color is used as an accent for the motor depiction?
    Reinforcement: The success came from several prompt elements working together: (1) 'Locate, don't answer' pushed the planner to search for the scene containing the visualization rather than guessing a color from the choices. (2) The siglip guidance — 'Describe what the scene LOOKS LIKE' and use it for 'scenes, actions, layout, visual states' — supported a visual query about a motor depiction in the center of a display. (3) 'Focus on the most visually distinctive feature — rare details beat generic descriptions' encouraged selecting the unusual target object ('motor' / center visualization) instead of broad car-interior queries. (4) 'Use answer choices wisely' was especially important: because the options were alternative colors of the same object in the same scene, the planner correctly did not create one query per color choice, and instead located the relevant subject/scene so the answerer could compare the attribute. (5) 'Always output at least one query' plus the 1-5 query guidance allowed a minimal single-query plan when one distinctive locator was enough.

[11] Q: When the surfer is upright on the wave and trying to stay balanced, what else is visible farther back in the water?
    Reinforcement: The success came from several prompt elements working together. First, the core rule 'Locate, don't answer' pushed the planner to target frames showing the surfer upright and balancing rather than trying to infer the answer in planning. Second, the reasoning instruction 'what must be visually true about the frames that contain the answer?' encouraged anchoring on the exact moment in the question, which produced Q1: 'surfer upright on wave trying to balance.' Third, the guidance 'Use answer choices wisely' specifically says that visually different choices implying different scenes or visible outcomes should be searched separately; that directly motivated one background query per option (ripples, surfers, rocky shoreline, rescue craft, large wave). Fourth, the siglip guidance to describe what the scene looks like and to keep queries concrete and visual fit this question perfectly, since all discriminative evidence was scene-level background imagery. Fifth, the combining guidance for AND/OR was crucial: using AND to require the anchor scene plus OR across alternative background possibilities created an effective two-stage retrieval strategy. Finally, the instruction to 'extract every visually searchable detail from the question and answer choices' helped the planner turn the phrase 'farther back in the water' into specific background-focused searches rather than generic surfing queries.

[12] Q: In the moment when only the original two boxes are visible side by side, how does the left-box character’s body look relative to his head?
    Reinforcement: The success came mainly from the prompt's repeated emphasis on locating the scene rather than answering the question. In particular: (1) the core instruction 'write queries for specific search tools that LOCATE the relevant frames' pushed the planner to target the moment where 'only the original two boxes are visible side by side'; (2) Rule 1, 'Locate, don't answer,' discouraged guessing the body proportion directly and instead encouraged finding the exact visual setup; (3) the siglip guidance to describe what the scene LOOKS LIKE and to focus on settings, actions, spatial layout, and visual states strongly supported the query's use of overall composition ('two boxes side by side') rather than identity labels or abstract attributes; (4) the guidance to focus on the most visually distinctive feature led the planner to use the highly discriminative layout cue of two boxes side by side; (5) Rule 4 about answer choices was important because all options were alternative attributes of the same subject in the same scene, so the planner correctly did not generate one query per answer choice and instead queried the shared scene; and (6) the instruction to use all information, but only turn details into queries if they help locate the target frames, helped the planner ignore unnecessary proportional descriptors and rely on the unique moment description.

[13] Q: When the brown liquid is added into the blender and later the finished smoothie is shown in the mason jar, which pair correctly identifies the tool used to add the liquid and the accessory in the drink?
    Reinforcement: The main driver of success was the prompt's emphasis on locating frames rather than answering: 'Locate, don't answer' and 'Always output at least one query.' That pushed the planner to target the two evidentiary moments in the video instead of trying to infer the answer from the options alone. The 'Use all information' rule also helped: the question contains two temporally separate visual events—brown liquid added to the blender, and later the finished smoothie in a mason jar—and the planner extracted both. The 'Use answer choices wisely' guidance was important because the options describe visibly different tools/accessories, encouraging searches around the contrasting scenes rather than a single generic smoothie shot. The tool guidance also contributed: siglip was used for scene/action states ('liquid being added to blender', 'mason jar with smoothie'), while tren was used for candidate objects ('squeeze bottle', 'paper straw'). Finally, the combining guidance was especially effective: using AND across queries forced retrieval to cover both required moments, which is exactly why the answerer saw frames containing both the liquid-addition action and the served drink accessory even though the object guesses were imperfect.
Stage 3 LLM Output
The failures share one main pattern: the planner is overusing answer-choice-specific queries in cases where the choices are just attributes of the same scene, especially counts and text labels. In both failures, existing guidance already pointed the right way, but it was weaker than contradictory patterns in the prompt/examples, so the model followed the more salient option-by-option behavior. The success analyses validate keeping the core structure: strong siglip/tren tool split, “locate, don’t answer,” concrete visual scene descriptions, distinctive anchors, and AND/OR composition. So the best fix is a minimal tightening of Rule 4 and OCR guidance to explicitly exclude counts and text-only answer choices, while preserving everything else.

<revised_prompt>
You are a search planner for a video question-answering system. Given a question and answer choices, write queries for specific search tools that LOCATE the relevant frames. A separate answerer model will look at those frames and determine the correct answer — you do NOT answer the question yourself.

## Tools

**siglip** — Visual similarity search.
  - Describe what the scene LOOKS LIKE: settings, actions, spatial layout, object attributes, visual states.
  - Cannot read text. Never include on-screen text in siglip queries.
  - Bad: "sign reading Exit Here" → Good: "hallway with illuminated signs"
  - Bad: "someone is happy" → Good: "person smiling and clapping"

**tren** — Object and entity search.
  - Short noun phrases only. ONE entity per query.
  - Good at finding specific objects or people by appearance.
  - Bad: "person picking up a red mug from the table" → Good: "red mug"
  - Bad: "cat and dog" → Good: two separate queries, "cat" and "dog"

For both tools, focus on the most visually distinctive feature — rare details beat generic descriptions.

## Query design

- Break complex scenes into separate queries across tools.
- Keep siglip queries concrete and visual. Avoid abstract or narrative language.
- Keep tren queries to short noun phrases. ONE entity per query.

## Combining queries (1-5 queries per plan)

- **AND** = intersection. Scene has multiple distinctive elements — one query each. Never AND queries that describe the same thing differently.
- **OR** = union. Different scenes, or different queries that might each find what you need.

## OCR

OCR runs automatically on every question — you do NOT need to handle it. Always write visual queries to locate the scene, even if the answer is about on-screen text or subtitles. The answerer model can read text directly from the frames you find. If answer choices are words/names that would appear only as on-screen text (e.g., menu items, station names, labels, subtitles), do NOT make one query per option and do NOT use tren for those names; query only for the surrounding visual UI/scene that contains the text.

## Rules

1. **Locate, don't answer.** Find the scene; the answerer decides what's happening.
2. **Always output at least one query.** Every question has a visual scene to find.
3. **Use all information.** Extract every visually searchable detail from the question and answer choices, but only turn details into queries if they help locate the target frames.
4. **Use answer choices wisely.** Visually different choices that imply different scenes or different visible outcomes → search for each. If the choices are alternative attributes of the SAME subject/object in the SAME scene (e.g., hair, clothing, age, color, number/count of people or objects), do NOT make one query per option; query to locate that subject/scene and let the answerer compare the attributes. If the question asks about first/last/before/after, prioritize queries that localize that event, not the answer choices.
5. **Right tool:** siglip for scenes, actions, layout, visual states. tren for specific objects or people. Use both when you need both.

## Question

{question}

Options:
{options}

Video duration: {duration}s encoded at {fps} fps.

You MUST first write 1-3 sentences of reasoning before the JSON block. Think about: what must be visually true about the frames that contain the answer? What is the most distinctive element to search for? Do the answer choices point to different scenes or the same scene? Never output the JSON block without reasoning first.

Then output a JSON block:
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "siglip", "query": "...", "id": "Q1"}}], "combine": "Q1"}}
```

Fields per query: "tool", "query", "id" (Q1, Q2, ...).

Examples:

---

Question: What does the woman in the red dress do after picking up the book from the table?
Options: A) places it on the shelf B) hands it to the man in glasses C) sits down on the couch and reads D) puts it in her bag E) walks out of the room

The question mentions a woman in a red dress, a book, and a table. The choices describe different actions after picking up the book — each would look different visually. I'll find the woman, the book, and search for the distinct scenes from each choice.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "woman in red dress", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "book", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person placing book on shelf", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person handing book to someone", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person sitting on couch reading", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "(Q1 AND Q2) AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

---

Question: What color is the vehicle that the man in the construction vest walks toward after crossing the street?
Options: A) red B) blue C) white D) black E) yellow

The question mentions a man in a construction vest and crossing a street. The choices are all vehicle colors — visually distinct. I'll find the man and search for each colored vehicle near a street.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "man in construction vest", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person crossing street toward vehicle", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "red vehicle", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "blue vehicle", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "white vehicle", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "Q1 AND Q2 AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

---

Question: In which room does the child first play with the wooden blocks?
Options: A) the kitchen B) the living room with the blue rug C) the bedroom D) the hallway E) the backyard

The question mentions a child and wooden blocks. The choices are different rooms, each visually distinct. I'll find the child and the blocks, and search for each room.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "child", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "wooden blocks", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "child playing in kitchen", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "living room with blue rug", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "child playing in bedroom", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "(Q1 AND Q2) AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

---

Question: What is shown on the display screen when the man in the blue jacket is standing at the podium?
Options: A) a bar chart B) a photo of the team C) the company logo D) a world map

The question asks about what appears on a display during a specific scene. OCR handles text automatically, so I need to find the scene visually — the man in the blue jacket at the podium with a display screen.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "man in blue jacket", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person standing at podium with display screen", "id": "Q2"}}], "combine": "Q1 AND Q2"}}
```

---

Question: What is the name of the restaurant shown on the sign outside the building?
Options: A) Mario's B) The Golden Fork C) Sushi Palace D) Burger Barn E) Cafe Luna

The question asks about text on a sign outside a building. I need to find the building exterior with the sign — the answerer will read the text.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "siglip", "query": "building exterior with restaurant sign", "id": "Q1"}}], "combine": "Q1"}}
```

Correct Questions

CORRECTWhen Q, R, and S are on screen and singing, which letter is the pastel green one? (answer: C)
Stage 1 Summary
The planner used a simple but effective siglip query, "three letters singing together on screen," which matched the distinctive visual pattern needed for the question: multiple alphabet characters appearing in a musical/singing scene. The retrieved frames include the key shot where Q, R, and S are on screen together, letting the answerer identify that S is the pastel green letter. This worked because the planner focused on the overall scene composition rather than overconstraining with specific letters or colors, making retrieval robust.
Stage 2 Reinforcement
The success came from several prompt elements working together. Most importantly, the prompt says 'Locate, don't answer. Find the scene; the answerer decides what's happening,' which pushed the planner to retrieve the singing scene instead of trying to reason directly about which option was green. The 'siglip' guidance also helped: 'Describe what the scene LOOKS LIKE: settings, actions, spatial layout, object attributes, visual states' and 'Keep siglip queries concrete and visual. Avoid abstract or narrative language.' That supports a query like 'three letters singing together on screen,' which is a concrete visual scene. The prompt's instruction to focus on 'the most visually distinctive feature — rare details beat generic descriptions' also mattered, because the distinctive event was the trio of letters singing together, not the individual answer-choice letters. In addition, the answer-choice guidance was important: 'If the choices are alternative attributes of the SAME subject/object in the SAME scene ... do NOT make one query per option; query to locate that subject/scene and let the answerer compare the attributes.' Here, the choices were candidate letter identities for the same on-screen group, so a single scene-locating query was the right strategy. Finally, 'Break complex scenes into separate queries across tools' but only when needed, plus 'Always output at least one query,' allowed the planner to stay minimal and use one strong siglip query instead of unnecessary tren queries.
CORRECTWhen the close-up treetops are shown, what contrasts with the vibrant foliage? (answer: C)
Stage 1 Summary
The planner used a highly targeted visual-similarity query—"close-up of treetops with vibrant green foliage"—that directly matched the distinctive scene the question refers to. This pulled frames showing the bright green canopy along with the nearby tree trunks, which are clearly dark brown and therefore provide the needed contrast with the foliage. The strategy worked because it focused on the exact visual moment in the question rather than broad mountain or road scenery, giving the answerer the relevant evidence to choose C.
Stage 2 Reinforcement
The success came from several prompt elements working together. First, the core instruction to 'LOCATE the relevant frames' and 'you do NOT answer the question yourself' pushed the planner to target the exact visual moment mentioned in the question rather than trying to reason over the bark options. Second, the siglip tool guidance was directly relevant: 'Describe what the scene LOOKS LIKE' and 'focus on the most visually distinctive feature — rare details beat generic descriptions.' The phrase 'close-up of treetops with vibrant green foliage' is a concrete visual scene description centered on the most distinctive cue in the question. Third, the query design guidance to keep siglip queries 'concrete and visual' and avoid abstract language helped produce a clean scene-level query. Fourth, Rule 4 about answer choices was important: because the options are alternative attributes of the same trees in the same scene, the planner correctly did not create one query per bark option and instead localized the shared scene so the answerer could compare attributes. Finally, the 1-5 query guidance encouraged a minimal plan, and since one distinctive scene was enough, the planner used a single high-precision query.
CORRECTLooking across the people on stage while the speaker is at the podium, whose robe includes blue along the sides? (answer: A)
Stage 1 Summary
The planner did well by pairing a broad scene query—"stage with people standing and one person at podium"—with appearance-focused robe queries, especially "robe with blue stripe," which directly targets the distinguishing visual detail in the answer choices. The retrieved frames are relevant because they consistently show the podium scene and the people positioned around it, making it easy to compare robes and notice that the person all the way on the left has blue panels along the sides. The successful strategy was to anchor retrieval on the event context first, then narrow with a discriminative clothing attribute so the answerer could identify the correct individual by position and robe color.
Stage 2 Reinforcement
The success came from several parts of the prompt working together. First, the core instruction 'Locate, don't answer' pushed the planner to search for the relevant podium scene rather than trying to reason out the robe owner from the choices alone. Second, the tool guidance 'siglip for scenes, actions, layout, visual states' led to the strong anchor query 'stage with people standing and one person at podium,' which matches the event context in the question. Third, the guidance to use 'the most visually distinctive feature' and that 'rare details beat generic descriptions' clearly supported the tren query 'robe with blue stripe,' which isolated the discriminative attribute needed to solve the question. Fourth, the rule 'Use all information' encouraged extracting both the stage/podium context and the robe-color detail from the question and choices. Fifth, Rule 4 on answer choices was especially important: because the options are alternative attributes/identities within the same scene, the planner correctly did not generate one query per option, and instead queried the shared scene plus the distinguishing visual attribute. Finally, the recommendation to 'use both when you need both' helped produce a complementary plan: broad scene localization with siglip and targeted clothing/entity retrieval with tren.

Stage 2 Reinforcement
The query succeeded because the prompt strongly encourages scene-localizing visual descriptions rather than answering directly. Several parts contributed: (1) the core instruction 'LOCATE the relevant frames' and Rule 1 'Locate, don't answer' pushed the planner to describe the target shot instead of selecting a choice; (2) the siglip guidance 'Describe what the scene LOOKS LIKE: settings, actions, spatial layout, object attributes, visual states' maps well to this question, where the key is the visible composition of three square treats on sticks and their decoration; (3) the query-design guidance to 'focus on the most visually distinctive feature — rare details beat generic descriptions' led to using the unusual, highly identifiable scene of Rice Krispie treats on sticks rather than generic dessert terms; (4) Rule 4 'If the choices are alternative attributes of the SAME subject/object in the SAME scene ... do NOT make one query per option; query to locate that subject/scene and let the answerer compare the attributes' was especially important, because all options described different frosting/decor details on the same treats, so a single scene query was the right strategy; (5) the combining guidance allowing 1-5 queries and not requiring unnecessary decomposition helped the planner keep one strong siglip query instead of splitting into weaker object-only searches; and (6) the instruction to 'use all information' but only turn details into queries if they help locate the target frames supported mentioning the close-up, three square treats, sticks, frosting, and decoration without overfitting to a specific answer choice.
CORRECTWhen the person recording focuses on the car’s rear, what distinctive feature is clearly visible? (answer: E)

Those queries retrieved highly relevant rear-view frames showing the back bumper, both tailpipes, and visible exhaust clouds from each side, so the answerer could directly verify that dual exhaust pipes are present and both are producing exhaust. Stage 2 Reinforcement
The success came from several prompt elements working together. First, the core instruction 'Locate, don't answer' pushed the planner to target the exact visual evidence at the car's rear rather than infer the answer. Second, the guidance under 'Use answer choices wisely' was especially important: the choices describe different visible outcomes in the same general scene, and the planner appropriately turned those alternatives into separate visual searches because they imply different visible exhaust configurations and emission patterns. Third, the siglip tool description strongly shaped the good queries: 'Describe what the scene LOOKS LIKE' and 'focus on the most visually distinctive feature' led the planner to use concrete visual phrases such as dual exhaust pipes, one side emitting smoke, both sides emitting smoke, and while moving/stopped. Fourth, the 'Break complex scenes into separate queries across tools' and 'OR = union. Different scenes, or different queries that might each find what you need' guidance supported the parallel coverage of confusable alternatives. Finally, the prompt's emphasis on concrete visual states helped the planner encode the crucial distinctions here: single vs dual pipes, left/one side vs both sides, and moving vs stopped.

That retrieved the key frames where only the two original Yu-Gi-Oh boxes are visible, making the left-box character clearly inspectable and showing he has a very large, bulky body with a comparatively small head. The success came from several prompt elements working together: (1) the core instruction 'Locate, don't answer' pushed the planner to find the relevant moment rather than reason about body proportions directly; (2) the OCR guidance and general visual-search framing encouraged querying the scene layout instead of character text/names; (3) the siglip tool description specifically says to describe what the scene looks like using settings, actions, and spatial layout, which maps directly to 'two boxes side by side on screen'; (4) the query-design guidance to keep siglip queries concrete and visual discouraged abstract phrasing like 'left character proportions'; (5) the rule 'Use answer choices wisely' was important because the options were all alternative attributes of the same subject in the same scene, so the planner correctly did not make one query per option and instead searched only for the shared target scene; (6) the instruction to focus on the most visually distinctive feature led to choosing the rare scene configuration 'only the original two boxes visible side by side,' which is exactly what localized the answerable frames.
CORRECTIn the same barred room, where does the visible illumination come from? (answer: A)

The retrieved frames are relevant because they consistently show the barred enclosure and arched passage lit from overhead, while not showing wall lamps, candles, a bright rear opening, or daylight from a side corridor. Stage 2 Reinforcement
The success came from several prompt elements working together. First, the 'Use answer choices wisely' rule explicitly says that when choices imply different visible outcomes or scenes, the planner should search for each; here the choices were competing visual sources of illumination, so making one siglip query per option was exactly aligned with that instruction. Second, the tool guidance for siglip strongly fit this task: 'Describe what the scene LOOKS LIKE' and 'focus on the most visually distinctive feature' encouraged scene-level visual phrasing such as 'barred room with light source above' rather than abstract reasoning about lighting. Third, the prompt's emphasis on 'Locate, don't answer' pushed the planner to retrieve frames showing the barred room and its illumination pattern instead of trying to infer the answer directly. Fourth, the combining guidance for OR — 'Different scenes, or different queries that might each find what you need' — directly supported the successful union of the five alternative illumination hypotheses. Finally, the rule to 'use all information' helped retain the anchoring detail 'same barred room,' which made each query more targeted and prevented retrieval from drifting to unrelated lit environments.

Incorrect Questions

KEEPWhich baby is associated with a construction worker hat, and what shoe color does that baby have? (correct: E, predicted: D)
Stage 1 Triage Reason
The planner used only a single broad query for 'baby wearing construction worker hat,' which did not target the key comparative scene with multiple babies and their shoe colors. Better queries like 'three babies standing together hats shoes' or explicit object queries for the construction hat and shoe colors could have retrieved the lineup frame more reliably and supported the correct answer.
Stage 2 Diagnosis + Suggestion
Diagnosis:
The prompt pushed the planner to 'locate the scene' with the single most distinctive element, and Rule 4 says not to make one query per option when the choices are alternative attributes of the same subject/object. For this question, that caused the planner to stop at 'baby wearing construction worker hat' and ignore the crucial relational information needed to answer: identifying that baby's position among multiple babies and its shoe color. In a multi-instance comparison scene, merely locating the target baby is insufficient if retrieved frames may show the wrong moment or incomplete group context. The prompt lacks an explicit instruction that for questions asking which item/person in a lineup has some attribute, the planner should also retrieve the full comparison scene (e.g., all babies together) and any secondary attribute needed for disambiguation.
Suggestion:
Add a minimal clarification to Rule 4 in 'Use answer choices wisely': after the sentence about not making one query per option for same-scene attributes, add: 'However, if the question requires identifying one subject among multiple similar instances in the same frame/group (e.g., leftmost/middle/rightmost, nth from left) or linking that subject to another visible attribute (e.g., shoe color), include a query for the full group/comparison scene and, if helpful, a query for the target-defining attribute/object.'
DISCARDWhich pairing of visible head or face details is correct for the tower scene and the sci-fi scene? (correct: D, predicted: B)
Stage 1 Triage Reason
The planner covered all answer choices with sensible visual queries, and the retrieved frames include the relevant evidence: a Robot Chicken scene where the chicken appears without eyewear and a sci-fi scene showing the person behind the robot in a black hood/patterned head covering. The miss is mainly downstream—either the gatherer/answerer failed to pair the correct sci-fi frame with the right option or the answerer ignored the black-hood evidence and chose uncovered hair.
KEEPIn the moment where Matsuki Suzuki is shown from the front speaking during the interview, what is he wearing around his neck? (correct: D, predicted: E)
Stage 1 Triage Reason
The planner query was too generic ('person speaking during interview from front') and failed to target the specific person or the neck area, so retrieval returned multiple unrelated interviewees. Queries mentioning 'Matsuki Suzuki', 'front interview close-up', and 'neckwear/bolo tie around neck' likely would have retrieved the correct shot and changed the answer.
Stage 2 Diagnosis + Suggestion
Diagnosis:
The prompt strongly discourages making one query per answer choice when the choices are alternative attributes of the same subject/object in the same scene (Rule 4), and the examples reinforce scene-localization over attribute-localization. For this question, the answer depends on a very small visual attribute on the same person in the same interview shot: the exact neckwear. That led the planner to issue only a generic scene query ('person speaking during interview from front'), which retrieved many front-facing interview frames but did not prioritize close/clear views of the neck area. The prompt lacks an explicit exception for fine-grained visible attributes/accessories like neckwear, so the planner under-searched the discriminative feature.
Suggestion:
Make a minimal edit to Rule 4 in 'Use answer choices wisely' by adding an exception for small, fine-grained visible attributes/accessories. For example: 'If the choices are alternative attributes of the SAME subject/object in the SAME scene, do NOT make one query per option; query to locate that subject/scene and let the answerer compare the attributes. However, if the question hinges on a small visible detail or accessory (e.g., neckwear, jewelry, logo, pattern, hairstyle detail), include an additional query that explicitly targets a clear/close front view of that region or item.'
DISCARDWhen the gentleman is visible inside the Rolls-Royce, what is he holding? (correct: C, predicted: E)
Stage 1 Triage Reason
The planner already included a direct query for a man holding a blue ribbon inside the car, and the retrieved frames clearly contain the relevant yellow Rolls-Royce scene with a visible blue first-place ribbon. This looks like an answerer mistake rather than a retrieval/planning failure.
KEEPWhen the right-side bay window is visible, which nearby object could be described as adding another distinct splash of color? (correct: B, predicted: C)
Stage 1 Triage Reason
The planner anchored retrieval on the literal phrase 'right-side bay window,' which is a poor visual query for this scene and likely missed the relevant exterior frames where the garage-side bay/window area and nearby green gate are visible together. Better scene-level queries like 'house exterior driveway garage right side' plus the candidate objects would more reliably retrieve the decisive frame and could change the answer.
Stage 2 Diagnosis + Suggestion
Diagnosis:
The prompt pushed the planner to overuse answer choices as separate visual searches even when they are just alternative nearby objects in the same scene. In this question, the key task is to localize the scene where the right-side bay window is visible and inspect what object is nearby in that scene. But Rule 3 ('Use all information') plus Rule 4 ('Use answer choices wisely') is underspecified for object-identity alternatives, so the planner turned every option into a tren query. That caused retrieval of frames containing distractor objects like the orange extension cord in unrelated contexts, and the answerer then chose based on those retrieved frames instead of the bay-window scene. The failure is especially tied to Rule 4, which forbids per-option queries for attributes of the same object, but does not also forbid per-option queries when the choices are different candidate objects that should be compared within one already-localized scene.
Suggestion:
Make a minimal addition to Rule 4 in 'Use answer choices wisely': after the sentence about attributes of the same subject/object, add: 'Likewise, if the choices are different candidate objects/items that are all supposed to be identified within the same localized scene (e.g., which nearby object, which item on the table, which vehicle beside the person), do NOT make one query per option; query only for the anchor scene/object and let the answerer compare the candidates in the retrieved frames.'
DISCARDIn that same stage lineup, which person is specifically identifiable as very young? (correct: A, predicted: E)
Stage 1 Triage Reason
The planner already queried all referenced lineup people, and the retrieved frames clearly include the stage lineup with the far-left blue-and-black robed figure visible. The mistake is in answering/grounding the attribute 'very young,' not in retrieval.
DISCARDIn the backdrop of small sponsor squares behind the man in black, which pair of names can both be seen? (correct: D, predicted: B)
Stage 1 Triage Reason
The planner already targeted the key scene ('man in black' with 'sponsor panels behind person'), and the retrieved frames include the press-conference shot where the sponsor grid is visible. The failure is mainly the answerer misreading the visible logos—Audi and Santander can be seen—rather than a retrieval/planning issue.
KEEPWhich rooftop feature is specifically noted both in the side view with the balcony and in the frontal view with the lone palm tree? (correct: D, predicted: E)
Stage 1 Triage Reason
The planner used only one vague visual-similarity query and never searched for the key cross-view rooftop feature itself. Better queries targeting rooftop equipment like 'air-conditioning units on roof', 'HVAC units', and both 'side view balcony' plus 'frontal view palm tree' would likely have retrieved more diagnostic frames and avoided the water-tank confusion.
Stage 2 Diagnosis + Suggestion
Diagnosis:
The prompt overemphasizes 'Locate, don't answer' and tells the planner to use answer choices only when they imply different scenes or different visible outcomes. In this question, the choices are alternative objects/features in the SAME rooftop scene, so the planner followed Rule 4 by searching only for the scene ('rooftop with balcony and lone palm tree') and ignored the candidate rooftop features. That was suboptimal because the target depends on comparing which feature is visible in both referenced views; a scene-only query retrieved broad building shots where multiple rooftop elements were present, leading the answerer to latch onto the wrong one (water tanks).
Suggestion:
Add a narrow exception to Rule 4 / 'Use answer choices wisely': when the question asks which object/feature is present, shared across views, or otherwise requires identifying a specific visible item within the same scene, include separate queries for the most visually distinctive candidate features from the answer choices (using tren for object nouns and/or siglip for distinctive layouts), not just the scene anchor. For example: 'If answer choices are alternative visible objects/features in the same scene, and the task is to identify which one appears, is shared, or is specifically noted, generate feature-level queries for distinctive options in addition to the scene query.'
KEEPDuring the moment when the third elephant moves toward the smaller elephant on the left, what is the other elephant doing? (correct: E, predicted: B)
Stage 1 Triage Reason
The planner used only one vague visual query focused on the 'third elephant' and did not request the key relation needed to answer what the other elephant was doing. Better queries targeting both elephants' simultaneous motion (e.g., the smaller elephant on the left and the other elephant moving left too) could have retrieved clearer frames showing answer E.
Stage 2 Diagnosis + Suggestion
Diagnosis:
The prompt over-emphasizes 'locate the scene' and 'prioritize queries that localize that event' (Rules 1 and 4), but it gives no explicit instruction for relational multi-subject questions where the answer depends on comparing another actor's motion during the same moment. That led the planner to generate only one event-localizing query — 'third elephant moving toward smaller elephant on left' — which retrieved frames centered on the trigger event but did not ensure the 'other elephant' was also clearly represented or that enough herd context was visible to compare directions. Because the choices are all alternative motions of the SAME other elephant in the SAME scene, Rule 4 discouraged per-option queries, but the prompt never compensates by telling the planner to add a second query for the referenced counterpart entity or for a wider contextual scene.
Suggestion:
Add one sentence to Rule 4 (Use answer choices wisely): 'If the question asks what another person/object is doing during a referenced event (e.g., "the other", "another", "left/right one", ordinal references), include an additional query that explicitly captures the full multi-entity scene or the counterpart entity so the retrieved frames show both subjects at once.'
Stage 3: Merge
Merge Input (all suggestions + reinforcements)
