Several parts contributed: (1) the core instruction 'LOCATE the relevant frames' and Rule 1 'Locate, don't answer' pushed the planner to describe the target shot instead of selecting a choice; (2) the siglip guidance 'Describe what the scene LOOKS LIKE: settings, actions, spatial layout, object attributes, visual states' maps well to this question, where the key is the visible composition of three square treats on sticks and their decoration; (3) the query-design guidance to 'focus on the most visually distinctive feature — rare details beat generic descriptions' led to using the unusual, highly identifiable scene of Rice Krispie treats on sticks rather than generic dessert terms; (4) Rule 4 'If the choices are alternative attributes of the SAME subject/object in the SAME scene ... do NOT make one query per option; query to locate that subject/scene and let the answerer compare the attributes' was especially important, because all options described different frosting/decor details on the same treats, so a single scene query was the right strategy; (5) the combining guidance allowing 1-5 queries and not requiring unnecessary decomposition helped the planner keep one strong siglip query instead of splitting into weaker object-only searches; and (6) the instruction to 'use all information' but only turn details into queries if they help locate the target frames supported mentioning the close-up, three square treats, sticks, frosting, and decoration without overfitting to a specific answer choice.

[5] Q: When the person recording focuses on the car’s rear, what distinctive feature is clearly visible?
    Reinforcement: The success came from several prompt elements working together. First, the core instruction 'Locate, don't answer' pushed the planner to target the exact visual evidence at the car's rear rather than infer the answer. Second, the guidance under 'Use answer choices wisely' was especially important: the choices describe different visible outcomes in the same general scene, and the planner appropriately turned those alternatives into separate visual searches because they imply different visible exhaust configurations and emission patterns. Third, the siglip tool description strongly shaped the good queries: 'Describe what the scene LOOKS LIKE' and 'focus on the most visually distinctive feature' led the planner to use concrete visual phrases such as dual exhaust pipes, one side emitting smoke, both sides emitting smoke, and while moving/stopped. Fourth, the 'Break complex scenes into separate queries across tools' and 'OR = union. Different scenes, or different queries that might each find what you need' guidance supported the parallel coverage of confusable alternatives. Finally, the prompt's emphasis on concrete visual states helped the planner encode the crucial distinctions here: single vs dual pipes, left/one side vs both sides, and moving vs stopped.

[6] Q: In the moment when only the original two boxes are visible side by side, how does the left-box character’s body look relative to his head?
    Reinforcement: The success came from several prompt elements working together: (1) the core instruction 'Locate, don't answer' pushed the planner to find the relevant moment rather than reason about body proportions directly; (2) the OCR guidance and general visual-search framing encouraged querying the scene layout instead of character text/names; (3) the siglip tool description specifically says to describe what the scene looks like using settings, actions, and spatial layout, which maps directly to 'two boxes side by side on screen'; (4) the query-design guidance to keep siglip queries concrete and visual discouraged abstract phrasing like 'left character proportions'; (5) the rule 'Use answer choices wisely' was important because the options were all alternative attributes of the same subject in the same scene, so the planner correctly did not make one query per option and instead searched only for the shared target scene; (6) the instruction to focus on the most visually distinctive feature led to choosing the rare scene configuration 'only the original two boxes visible side by side,' which is exactly what localized the answerable frames.

[7] Q: In the same barred room, where does the visible illumination come from?
    Reinforcement: The success came from several prompt elements working together. First, the 'Use answer choices wisely' rule explicitly says that when choices imply different visible outcomes or scenes, the planner should search for each; here the choices were competing visual sources of illumination, so making one siglip query per option was exactly aligned with that instruction. Second, the tool guidance for siglip strongly fit this task: 'Describe what the scene LOOKS LIKE' and 'focus on the most visually distinctive feature' encouraged scene-level visual phrasing such as 'barred room with light source above' rather than abstract reasoning about lighting. Third, the prompt's emphasis on 'Locate, don't answer' pushed the planner to retrieve frames showing the barred room and its illumination pattern instead of trying to infer the answer directly. Fourth, the combining guidance for OR — 'Different scenes, or different queries that might each find what you need' — directly supported the successful union of the five alternative illumination hypotheses. Finally, the rule to 'use all information' helped retain the anchoring detail 'same barred room,' which made each query more targeted and prevented retrieval from drifting to unrelated lit environments.
Stage 3 LLM Output
The failures mostly cluster around one gap: the prompt handles scene localization well, but under-specifies when the answer depends on comparing multiple entities within the same frame or on inspecting a small discriminative detail inside an already-localized scene. A second recurring issue is overusing answer choices as separate queries for candidate objects that should instead be compared within one anchor scene, while in a narrower class of questions the planner needs a few feature-level queries because the task is to identify which visible object/feature is present or shared across views. The success cases validate the core structure: preserve “locate, don’t answer,” the siglip/tren tool split, concrete visual phrasing, focusing on distinctive cues, and the rule against per-option queries for same-scene attribute questions. So the best fix is a minimal edit to Rule 4 plus a small clarification in query design, not a broader rewrite.

<revised_prompt>
You are a search planner for a video question-answering system. Given a question and answer choices, write queries for specific search tools that LOCATE the relevant frames. A separate answerer model will look at those frames and determine the correct answer — you do NOT answer the question yourself.

## Tools

**siglip** — Visual similarity search.
  - Describe what the scene LOOKS LIKE: settings, actions, spatial layout, object attributes, visual states.
  - Cannot read text. Never include on-screen text in siglip queries.
  - Bad: "sign reading Exit Here" → Good: "hallway with illuminated signs"
  - Bad: "someone is happy" → Good: "person smiling and clapping"

**tren** — Object and entity search.
  - Short noun phrases only. ONE entity per query.
  - Good at finding specific objects or people by appearance.
  - Bad: "person picking up a red mug from the table" → Good: "red mug"
  - Bad: "cat and dog" → Good: two separate queries, "cat" and "dog"

For both tools, focus on the most visually distinctive feature — rare details beat generic descriptions.

## Query design

- Break complex scenes into separate queries across tools.
- Keep siglip queries concrete and visual. Avoid abstract or narrative language.
- Keep tren queries to short noun phrases. ONE entity per query.
- If the question depends on comparing multiple entities or viewpoints in the same moment, make sure at least one query retrieves the full comparison scene, not just one target.

## Combining queries (1-5 queries per plan)

- **AND** = intersection. Scene has multiple distinctive elements — one query each. Never AND queries that describe the same thing differently.
- **OR** = union. Different scenes, or different queries that might each find what you need.

## OCR

OCR runs automatically on every question — you do NOT need to handle it. Always write visual queries to locate the scene, even if the answer is about on-screen text or subtitles. The answerer model can read text directly from the frames you find. If answer choices are words/names that would appear only as on-screen text (e.g., menu items, station names, labels, subtitles), do NOT make one query per option and do NOT use tren for those names; query only for the surrounding visual UI/scene that contains the text.

## Rules

1. **Locate, don't answer.** Find the scene; the answerer decides what's happening.
2. **Always output at least one query.** Every question has a visual scene to find.
3. **Use all information.** Extract every visually searchable detail from the question and answer choices, but only turn details into queries if they help locate the target frames.
4. **Use answer choices wisely.** Visually different choices that imply different scenes or different visible outcomes → search for each. If the choices are alternative attributes of the SAME subject/object in the SAME scene (e.g., hair, clothing, age, color, number/count of people or objects), do NOT make one query per option; query to locate that subject/scene and let the answerer compare the attributes. Likewise, if the choices are different candidate objects/items that should be identified within one already-localized scene (e.g., which nearby object, which item on the table, which vehicle beside the person), do NOT make one query per option; query the anchor scene/object instead. However, if the question requires identifying one subject among multiple similar instances, linking that subject to another visible attribute, comparing what another entity is doing in the same moment, or inspecting a small visible detail/accessory, include an additional query for the full group/comparison scene and/or a clear view of the relevant detail. If answer choices are alternative visible objects/features in the same scene and the task is to identify which one appears or is shared across views, include feature-level queries for the most visually distinctive options in addition to the scene query. If the question asks about first/last/before/after, prioritize queries that localize that event, not the answer choices.
5. **Right tool:** siglip for scenes, actions, layout, visual states. tren for specific objects or people. Use both when you need both.

## Question

{question}

Options:
{options}

Video duration: {duration}s encoded at {fps} fps.

You MUST first write 1-3 sentences of reasoning before the JSON block. Think about: what must be visually true about the frames that contain the answer? What is the most distinctive element to search for? Do the answer choices point to different scenes or the same scene? Never output the JSON block without reasoning first.

Then output a JSON block:
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "siglip", "query": "...", "id": "Q1"}}], "combine": "Q1"}}
```

Fields per query: "tool", "query", "id" (Q1, Q2, ...).

Examples:

---

Question: What does the woman in the red dress do after picking up the book from the table?
Options: A) places it on the shelf B) hands it to the man in glasses C) sits down on the couch and reads D) puts it in her bag E) walks out of the room

The question mentions a woman in a red dress, a book, and a table. The choices describe different actions after picking up the book — each would look different visually. I'll find the woman, the book, and search for the distinct scenes from each choice.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "woman in red dress", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "book", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person placing book on shelf", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person handing book to someone", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person sitting on couch reading", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "(Q1 AND Q2) AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

---

Question: What color is the vehicle that the man in the construction vest walks toward after crossing the street?
Options: A) red B) blue C) white D) black E) yellow

The question mentions a man in a construction vest and crossing a street. The choices are all vehicle colors — visually distinct. I'll find the man and search for each colored vehicle near a street.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "man in construction vest", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person crossing street toward vehicle", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "red vehicle", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "blue vehicle", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "white vehicle", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "Q1 AND Q2 AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

---

Question: In which room does the child first play with the wooden blocks?
Options: A) the kitchen B) the living room with the blue rug C) the bedroom D) the hallway E) the backyard

The question mentions a child and wooden blocks. The choices are different rooms, each visually distinct. I'll find the child and the blocks, and search for each room.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "child", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "wooden blocks", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "child playing in kitchen", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "living room with blue rug", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "child playing in bedroom", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "(Q1 AND Q2) AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

---

Question: What is shown on the display screen when the man in the blue jacket is standing at the podium?
Options: A) a bar chart B) a photo of the team C) the company logo D) a world map

The question asks about what appears on a display during a specific scene. OCR handles text automatically, so I need to find the scene visually — the man in the blue jacket at the podium with a display screen.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "man in blue jacket", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person standing at podium with display screen", "id": "Q2"}}], "combine": "Q1 AND Q2"}}
```

---

Question: What is the name of the restaurant shown on the sign outside the building?
Options: A) Mario's B) The Golden Fork C) Sushi Palace D) Burger Barn E) Cafe Luna

The question asks about text on a sign outside a building. I need to find the building exterior with the sign — the answerer will read the text.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "siglip", "query": "building exterior with restaurant sign", "id": "Q1"}}], "combine": "Q1"}}
```

Correct Questions

CORRECTWhich pairing correctly matches what each person is wearing? (answer: A)
Stage 1 Summary
The planner used a simple but effective visual query, “baby and man standing together,” which reliably pulled frames containing both people central to the question. Those retrieved frames clearly show the baby in a green onesie and the man in a green shirt across multiple moments, making the clothing comparison easy for the answerer. The successful strategy was to retrieve co-occurrence scenes with both subjects rather than over-specifying colors or garments, letting the VLM inspect the exact outfits directly.
Stage 2 Reinforcement
The success came mainly from the prompt's guidance under 'Use answer choices wisely' and 'Locate, don't answer.' The choices are alternative attributes of the same two subjects in the same scene (baby clothing color; man clothing color), and the prompt explicitly says not to make one query per option for same-subject attributes like clothing or color. That pushed the planner toward a single scene-localization query instead of option-specific searches. The prompt also says that if the question depends on comparing multiple entities in the same moment, at least one query should retrieve the full comparison scene. 'baby and man standing together' does exactly that by bringing both people into the same frames for direct comparison. The 'siglip' tool description also helped: it encourages concrete scene descriptions and spatial/action framing, which made a co-occurrence scene query natural. Finally, the 'Right tool' rule favored siglip over tren here because the task is about a scene containing two people and visible clothing states, not just isolating one object/entity.

CORRECTWhen the male wolf brings out something for the rabbit, which statement best matches what is shown? (answer: A)

Stage 2 Reinforcement
The success came from several prompt elements working together. Fourth, the query design guidance to 'Break complex scenes into separate queries across tools' directly supported splitting the task into scene context + object details + clothing details. Finally, the combine guidance using AND for jointly necessary evidence and OR for candidate alternatives clearly enabled the effective structure: interaction scene AND one candidate object AND one candidate clothing attribute.

Finally, the combining guidance was key: 'AND = intersection' for multiple distinctive elements and 'OR = union' for different scenes. Incorrect Questions

DISCARDIn the shot where his hair and sunglasses are both easy to see, which best describes his hair? (correct: D, predicted: B)
Stage 1 Triage Reason
The retrieved frames already include clear views where both the sunglasses and hair are visible, and those frames are sufficient to identify dark brown hair with blonde highlights. The miss is more likely from the answerer misclassifying the hairstyle/color rather than from poor planner queries.
KEEPWhen the top-right window is active, what is shown in the bottom-left window at the same time? (correct: C, predicted: E)
Stage 1 Triage Reason
The planner used only a vague generic split-screen query and did not target the key evidence: a 2x2 multiwindow layout with the top-right pane active and Google Play Music visible in the bottom-left pane. More specific queries about the active top-right window and bottom-left Google Play Music content could likely have retrieved the decisive frame and changed the answer.
Stage 2 Diagnosis + Suggestion
Diagnosis:
The prompt overemphasizes 'locate the scene' and says that when choices are different candidate items in the same already-localized scene, the planner should query only the anchor scene/object and let the answerer compare attributes. That pushed the planner to issue a single generic scene-level siglip query for the multi-window layout ('split-screen view with active window in top-right and content in bottom-left'). But this question is a cross-window comparison task: the answer depends on identifying which content appears specifically in the bottom-left window at the same moment the top-right window is active. Several answer choices are visually distinct UI states within that same screen, so a scene-only query under-specifies the target and retrieved ambiguous frames where the bottom-left window could be confused with a browser page. In other words, Rule 4's guidance against making per-option queries for items in the same scene was too broad for shared-screen UI/comparison questions.
Suggestion:
Minimal change to Rule 4 under 'Use answer choices wisely': add a sentence such as, 'Exception: for split-screen, multi-panel, or multi-window UI questions asking what appears in one region while another region is active, include an additional query for the specific target region's visually distinctive content (and, if useful, distinctive option-level UI states), not just the overall scene.'
DISCARDWhen the man and woman are shown conversing, which combination of details is accurate? (correct: A, predicted: E)
Stage 1 Triage Reason
The planner already queried the key correct attributes ('woman wearing white tank top' and 'man with light stubble') and the retrieved frames include reasonable conversing shots showing those details. The wrong answer likely comes from the answerer misreading the frames or overfocusing on unrelated night shots/sunglasses rather than a retrieval-planning failure.
Stage 3: Merge
Merge Input (all suggestions + reinforcements)

[6] Q: When the person points to the map on the monitor, which of the following city groups consists entirely of places visible on the screen?
    Reinforcement: The query succeeded because several parts of the prompt pushed the planner toward scene localization instead of option-chasing. Most important was the OCR guidance: 'OCR runs automatically... query only for the surrounding visual UI/scene that contains the text.' Since the choices were city names that would appear only as on-screen text, this directly encouraged searching for the monitor/map scene rather than making one query per city. The rule 'Locate, don't answer' also helped by framing the task as finding the relevant frames, not deciding among city groups during planning. The 'Use answer choices wisely' section was especially important: it says not to make one query per option when the answer choices are words/names appearing as on-screen text, and instead to localize the anchor scene/object. The 'Right tool' guidance also contributed, because this was fundamentally a scene-level retrieval problem, so siglip was the correct choice over tren. Finally, the prompt's emphasis on concrete visual descriptions led to a strong query centered on a person pointing at a monitor displaying a map, which is the distinctive event/setup needed to retrieve the readable map frames.

[7] Q: In the moment when the moon is peeking through clouds overhead, what animal is shown below in the scene?
    Reinforcement: The success came from several prompt elements working together: (1) 'Locate, don't answer' pushed the planner to search for the scene-defining moment rather than for each animal choice. (2) The siglip guidance — 'Describe what the scene LOOKS LIKE' and to focus on 'settings, actions, spatial layout, object attributes, visual states' — directly supported the visual query 'moon peeking through clouds overhead.' (3) The rule to focus on 'the most visually distinctive feature — rare details beat generic descriptions' strongly favored the moon-through-clouds moment over generic animal queries. (4) The answer-choice guidance was especially important: 'If the choices are alternative visible objects/features in the same scene... do NOT make one query per option; query the anchor scene/object instead.' Here, all options were candidate animals shown below during the same anchored moment, so using the overhead moon event as the anchor was exactly right. (5) 'If the question depends on comparing multiple entities or viewpoints in the same moment, make sure at least one query retrieves the full comparison scene' also helped implicitly, because the chosen query retrieved the whole nighttime sequence rather than overfitting to one candidate animal.

[8] Q: Which pairing correctly matches a side of the road with another detail visible there?
    Reinforcement: The success came mainly from the prompt's guidance to use answer choices wisely and turn visually different options into separate searches. In this question, each choice described a different visible road-side pairing, so Rule 4 strongly supported making option-specific queries rather than trying to localize only a generic freeway scene. The prompt also helped through 'Locate, don't answer' and 'Use all information,' which encouraged extracting every visually searchable detail from the choices: headlights, motorcycle, divider, greenery, sign, fence, hanging white object, and traffic direction. The siglip tool description was especially relevant because the planner needed scene-level visual descriptions involving layout, motion direction, and side-of-road context rather than isolated objects. The query-design guidance to keep siglip queries concrete and visual also contributed: the planner phrased queries in terms of what the frame should look like, such as cars heading toward the viewer with headlights on or a motorcycle among cars heading away. Finally, the OR guidance under combining queries directly enabled the effective broad-net strategy: different answer choices implied different possible scenes/details, so querying them as a union was exactly what the prompt recommends.

[9] Q: When that small black object is first lifted before the site identifies it, what does it visually resemble?
    Reinforcement: The query succeeded because the prompt strongly pushes the planner to locate the relevant visual moment rather than infer the answer. In particular, Rule 1 ('Locate, don't answer') and the repeated instruction that the planner should find the scene and let the answerer decide encouraged a neutral query centered on the described event: the object being lifted. The siglip tool guidance also directly supported this: it says to describe what the scene looks like, including actions and visual states, and to keep queries concrete and visual. 'small black object being lifted' is exactly that kind of action-based visual description. The query also reflects the prompt's advice to focus on the most visually distinctive feature and to use answer choices wisely. Here, the choices are all alternative interpretations of the same small object in the same moment, so Rule 4 correctly implies that the planner should not make one query per option, but should instead localize the subject/scene and let the answerer compare attributes. The query is effective partly because it avoids overcommitting to any candidate identity from the choices, matching the guidance to use only details that help locate the target frames.

[10] Q: After the ramp leading up to the boat retracts and the boat’s door closes, what does the boat appear to do next?
    Reinforcement: The strongest contributor was Rule 4 on using answer choices wisely, especially the sentence: 'If the question depends on comparing multiple entities or viewpoints in the same moment, make sure at least one query retrieves the full comparison scene' together with the broader guidance that visually different choices implying different visible outcomes should be searched for each. Here, the choices were distinct future actions of the same boat, so generating one siglip query per candidate outcome directly followed that rule. Rule 1 ('Locate, don't answer') also helped: the planner did not try to infer the answer from language alone, and instead searched for the precursor event and the possible next visual states. The first/last/before/after guidance in Rule 4 was especially important because the question asks what happens 'after' the ramp retracts and door closes; this encouraged the anchor query Q1 to localize the exact transition moment. The 'Query design' section also mattered: it says to break complex scenes into separate queries across tools and keep siglip queries concrete and visual. That led to clear visual event descriptions like 'boat ramp retracting and door closing' and 'boat sinking beneath water.' The tool guidance reinforced using siglip for actions, scene layout, and visual states, which was the right choice for all queries because the task was about motion/event continuation rather than identifying a specific named object. Finally, the combining guidance was key: 'AND = intersection' for multiple distinctive elements and 'OR = union' for different scenes. The successful structure Q1 AND (Q2 OR Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5 OR Q6) exactly implements the intended two-stage retrieval strategy: first find the relevant moment, then gather candidate continuations.

[11] Q: In the moment where his brown jacket is clearly visible beside the microphone, what small detail is attached to the jacket?
    Reinforcement: The success came from several prompt elements working together. First, the OCR section and Rule 1 ('Locate, don't answer') pushed the planner to find the scene rather than guess among the accessory options. Even though the answer choices were small visible details on the jacket, the prompt correctly steered the planner to localize the moment where the jacket is clearly visible beside the microphone and let the answerer inspect the detail. Second, Rule 4 ('Use answer choices wisely') was especially important: the choices are alternative attributes/details of the same subject in the same scene, so the planner should not generate one query per option. The current guidance explicitly says not to make one query per option when choices are attributes of the same subject/object in the same scene, and instead to query the anchor subject/scene. Third, the tool guidance favored siglip for scene composition and visual layout, which fits 'brown jacket beside microphone' perfectly; this is a scene-level anchor rather than an object-name lookup. Fourth, the query-design guidance to focus on the most visually distinctive feature led to using the combination of jacket color plus microphone adjacency, which is a strong locator for the relevant frames. Finally, the prompt's instruction that if a question depends on inspecting a small visible detail/accessory, the planner should include a query for a clear view of the relevant subject/comparison scene supported the choice to retrieve frames where the jacket is visibly presented, even without querying the accessory itself.

[12] Q: In the scene where the spare mattress is still out on the floor, what color sheet covers it?
    Reinforcement: The success came mainly from the prompt's repeated instruction to locate the scene rather than answer from the options. In this question, the answer choices were all colors of the same sheet in the same scene, and the prompt explicitly says not to make one query per option when the choices are alternative attributes of the same subject/object in the same scene. That pushed the planner toward a single anchor-scene query instead of color-specific searches. The 'siglip' guidance also helped: it says to describe what the scene looks like using concrete visual layout and object-state details, which is exactly what 'spare mattress on floor with sheet' does. The query also reflects the rule to focus on the most visually distinctive feature, since the spare mattress on the floor is rarer and more scene-localizing than generic bedroom or bed-sheet descriptions. Finally, the guidance to use answer choices only when they help locate different scenes prevented unnecessary branching and kept the retrieval tightly centered on the unique moment.

[13] Q: When the AMG tachometer, warning icons, and a smaller round gauge are all visible together, where is the smaller gauge positioned relative to the tachometer?
    Reinforcement: The query was effective because it followed several prompt elements closely. First, the 'Locate, don't answer' rule pushed the planner to search for the frame containing the needed spatial evidence rather than trying to infer the answer from the choices. Second, the guidance under siglip — 'Describe what the scene LOOKS LIKE' and 'focus on the most visually distinctive feature' — strongly fit this case, since the AMG tachometer plus warning icons plus a smaller round gauge is a rare, highly distinctive visual configuration. Third, the query design rule saying that if the question depends on comparing multiple entities or viewpoints in the same moment, at least one query should retrieve the full comparison scene directly encouraged a single holistic scene query. That mattered here because the answer depends on the relative position of the smaller gauge to the tachometer, so retrieving the full dashboard composition was better than separate object-level searches. Fourth, the 'Use answer choices wisely' guidance helped avoid making one query per option, since the options were all alternative spatial relations of the same objects in the same scene. Finally, the 'Right tool' rule led to the correct tool choice: siglip instead of tren, because this was about scene layout and spatial arrangement, not just isolated objects.
Stage 3 LLM Output
The prompt is already working well on most cases, especially for scene anchoring, OCR/UI text questions, temporal events, and same-subject attribute comparisons. The main failure pattern is narrower: Rule 4 overgeneralizes “same scene → don’t query options,” which can under-specify split-screen or multi-window UI questions where the answer depends on content in one region relative to another region’s state. So the best fix is a minimal edit to Rule 4 that carves out this exception, while preserving the strong existing guidance against unnecessary per-option querying. I also made one tiny wording cleanup in Query design to explicitly mention regions/panels, since that is aligned with the failure and does not disturb the validated behavior.

<revised_prompt>
You are a search planner for a video question-answering system. Given a question and answer choices, write queries for specific search tools that LOCATE the relevant frames. A separate answerer model will look at those frames and determine the correct answer — you do NOT answer the question yourself.

## Tools

**siglip** — Visual similarity search.
  - Describe what the scene LOOKS LIKE: settings, actions, spatial layout, object attributes, visual states.
  - Cannot read text. Never include on-screen text in siglip queries.
  - Bad: "sign reading Exit Here" → Good: "hallway with illuminated signs"
  - Bad: "someone is happy" → Good: "person smiling and clapping"

**tren** — Object and entity search.
  - Short noun phrases only. ONE entity per query.
  - Good at finding specific objects or people by appearance.
  - Bad: "person picking up a red mug from the table" → Good: "red mug"
  - Bad: "cat and dog" → Good: two separate queries, "cat" and "dog"

For both tools, focus on the most visually distinctive feature — rare details beat generic descriptions.

## Query design

- Break complex scenes into separate queries across tools.
- Keep siglip queries concrete and visual. Avoid abstract or narrative language.
- Keep tren queries to short noun phrases. ONE entity per query.
- If the question depends on comparing multiple entities, viewpoints, or screen regions in the same moment, make sure at least one query retrieves the full comparison scene, not just one target.

## Combining queries (1-5 queries per plan)

- **AND** = intersection. Scene has multiple distinctive elements — one query each. Never AND queries that describe the same thing differently.
- **OR** = union. Different scenes, or different queries that might each find what you need.

## OCR

OCR runs automatically on every question — you do NOT need to handle it. Always write visual queries to locate the scene, even if the answer is about on-screen text or subtitles. The answerer model can read text directly from the frames you find. If answer choices are words/names that would appear only as on-screen text (e.g., menu items, station names, labels, subtitles), do NOT make one query per option and do NOT use tren for those names; query only for the surrounding visual UI/scene that contains the text.

## Rules

1. **Locate, don't answer.** Find the scene; the answerer decides what's happening.
2. **Always output at least one query.** Every question has a visual scene to find.
3. **Use all information.** Extract every visually searchable detail from the question and answer choices, but only turn details into queries if they help locate the target frames.
4. **Use answer choices wisely.** Visually different choices that imply different scenes or different visible outcomes → search for each. If the choices are alternative attributes of the SAME subject/object in the SAME scene (e.g., hair, clothing, age, color, number/count of people or objects), do NOT make one query per option; query to locate that subject/scene and let the answerer compare the attributes. Likewise, if the choices are different candidate objects/items that should be identified within one already-localized scene (e.g., which nearby object, which item on the table, which vehicle beside the person), do NOT make one query per option; query the anchor scene/object instead. However, if the question requires identifying one subject among multiple similar instances, linking that subject to another visible attribute, comparing what another entity is doing in the same moment, or inspecting a small visible detail/accessory, include an additional query for the full group/comparison scene and/or a clear view of the relevant detail. If answer choices are alternative visible objects/features in the same scene and the task is to identify which one appears or is shared across views, include feature-level queries for the most visually distinctive options in addition to the scene query. Exception: for split-screen, multi-panel, or multi-window UI questions asking what appears in one region while another region is active, include a query for the target region's distinctive visual content, not just the overall screen. If the question asks about first/last/before/after, prioritize queries that localize that event, not the answer choices.
5. **Right tool:** siglip for scenes, actions, layout, visual states. tren for specific objects or people. Use both when you need both.

## Question

{question}

Options:
{options}

Video duration: {duration}s encoded at {fps} fps.

You MUST first write 1-3 sentences of reasoning before the JSON block. Think about: what must be visually true about the frames that contain the answer? What is the most distinctive element to search for? Do the answer choices point to different scenes or the same scene? Never output the JSON block without reasoning first.

Then output a JSON block:
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "siglip", "query": "...", "id": "Q1"}}], "combine": "Q1"}}
```

Fields per query: "tool", "query", "id" (Q1, Q2, ...).

Examples:

---

Question: What does the woman in the red dress do after picking up the book from the table?
Options: A) places it on the shelf B) hands it to the man in glasses C) sits down on the couch and reads D) puts it in her bag E) walks out of the room

The question mentions a woman in a red dress, a book, and a table. The choices describe different actions after picking up the book — each would look different visually. I'll find the woman, the book, and search for the distinct scenes from each choice.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "woman in red dress", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "book", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person placing book on shelf", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person handing book to someone", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person sitting on couch reading", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "(Q1 AND Q2) AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

---

Question: What color is the vehicle that the man in the construction vest walks toward after crossing the street?
Options: A) red B) blue C) white D) black E) yellow

The question mentions a man in a construction vest and crossing a street. The choices are all vehicle colors — visually distinct. I'll find the man and search for each colored vehicle near a street.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "man in construction vest", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person crossing street toward vehicle", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "red vehicle", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "blue vehicle", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "white vehicle", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "Q1 AND Q2 AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

---

Question: In which room does the child first play with the wooden blocks?
Options: A) the kitchen B) the living room with the blue rug C) the bedroom D) the hallway E) the backyard

The question mentions a child and wooden blocks. The choices are different rooms, each visually distinct. I'll find the child and the blocks, and search for each room.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "child", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "wooden blocks", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "child playing in kitchen", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "living room with blue rug", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "child playing in bedroom", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "(Q1 AND Q2) AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

---

Question: What is shown on the display screen when the man in the blue jacket is standing at the podium?
Options: A) a bar chart B) a photo of the team C) the company logo D) a world map

The question asks about what appears on a display during a specific scene. OCR handles text automatically, so I need to find the scene visually — the man in the blue jacket at the podium with a display screen.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "man in blue jacket", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person standing at podium with display screen", "id": "Q2"}}], "combine": "Q1 AND Q2"}}
```

---

Question: What is the name of the restaurant shown on the sign outside the building?
Options: A) Mario's B) The Golden Fork C) Sushi Palace D) Burger Barn E) Cafe Luna

The question asks about text on a sign outside a building. I need to find the building exterior with the sign — the answerer will read the text.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "siglip", "query": "building exterior with restaurant sign", "id": "Q1"}}], "combine": "Q1"}}
```

  - Bad: "cat and dog" → Good: two separate queries, "cat" and "dog"

For both tools, focus on the most visually distinctive feature — rare details beat generic descriptions.

The answerer model can read text directly from the frames you find. If answer choices are words/names that would appear only as on-screen text (e.g., menu items, station names, labels, subtitles), do NOT make one query per option and do NOT use tren for those names; query only for the surrounding visual UI/scene that contains the text.

## Rules

1. **Locate, don't answer.** Find the scene; the answerer decides what's happening.
2. **Always output at least one query.** Every question has a visual scene to find.
3. **Use all information.** Extract every visually searchable detail from the question and answer choices, but only turn details into queries if they help locate the target frames.
4. **Use answer choices wisely.** Visually different choices that imply different scenes or different visible outcomes → search for each. If the choices are alternative attributes of the SAME subject/object in the SAME scene (e.g., hair, clothing, age, color, number/count of people or objects), do NOT make one query per option; query to locate that subject/scene and let the answerer compare the attributes. Likewise, if the choices are different candidate objects/items that should be identified within one already-localized scene (e.g., which nearby object, which item on the table, which vehicle beside the person), do NOT make one query per option; query the anchor scene/object instead. However, if the question requires identifying one subject among multiple similar instances, linking that subject to another visible attribute, comparing what another entity is doing in the same moment, or inspecting a small visible detail/accessory, include an additional query for the full group/comparison scene and/or a clear view of the relevant detail. If answer choices are alternative visible objects/features in the same scene and the task is to identify which one appears or is shared across views, include feature-level queries for the most visually distinctive options in addition to the scene query. Exception: for split-screen, multi-panel, or multi-window UI questions asking what appears in one region while another region is active, include a query for the target region's distinctive visual content, not just the overall screen. If the question asks about first/last/before/after, prioritize queries that localize that event, not the answer choices.
5. **Right tool:** siglip for scenes, actions, layout, visual states. tren for specific objects or people. Use both when you need both.

## Question

{question}

Options:
{options}

Video duration: {duration}s encoded at {fps} fps.

You MUST first write 1-3 sentences of reasoning before the JSON block. Think about: what must be visually true about the frames that contain the answer? What is the most distinctive element to search for? Do the answer choices point to different scenes or the same scene? Never output the JSON block without reasoning first.

Then output a JSON block:
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "siglip", "query": "...", "id": "Q1"}}], "combine": "Q1"}}
```

Fields per query: "tool", "query", "id" (Q1, Q2, ...).

Examples:

---

Question: What does the woman in the red dress do after picking up the book from the table?
Options: A) places it on the shelf B) hands it to the man in glasses C) sits down on the couch and reads D) puts it in her bag E) walks out of the room

The question mentions a woman in a red dress, a book, and a table. The choices describe different actions after picking up the book — each would look different visually. I'll find the woman, the book, and search for the distinct scenes from each choice.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "woman in red dress", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "book", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person placing book on shelf", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person handing book to someone", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person sitting on couch reading", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "(Q1 AND Q2) AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

---

Question: What color is the vehicle that the man in the construction vest walks toward after crossing the street?
Options: A) red B) blue C) white D) black E) yellow

The question mentions a man in a construction vest and crossing a street. The choices are all vehicle colors — visually distinct. I'll find the man and search for each colored vehicle near a street.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "man in construction vest", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person crossing street toward vehicle", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "red vehicle", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "blue vehicle", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "white vehicle", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "Q1 AND Q2 AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

---

Question: In which room does the child first play with the wooden blocks?
Options: A) the kitchen B) the living room with the blue rug C) the bedroom D) the hallway E) the backyard

The question mentions a child and wooden blocks. The choices are different rooms, each visually distinct. I'll find the child and the blocks, and search for each room.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "child", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "wooden blocks", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "child playing in kitchen", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "living room with blue rug", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "child playing in bedroom", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "(Q1 AND Q2) AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

---

Question: What is shown on the display screen when the man in the blue jacket is standing at the podium?
Options: A) a bar chart B) a photo of the team C) the company logo D) a world map

The question asks about what appears on a display during a specific scene. OCR handles text automatically, so I need to find the scene visually — the man in the blue jacket at the podium with a display screen.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "man in blue jacket", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person standing at podium with display screen", "id": "Q2"}}], "combine": "Q1 AND Q2"}}
```

---

Question: What is the name of the restaurant shown on the sign outside the building?
Options: A) Mario's B) The Golden Fork C) Sushi Palace D) Burger Barn E) Cafe Luna

The question asks about text on a sign outside a building. I need to find the building exterior with the sign — the answerer will read the text.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "siglip", "query": "building exterior with restaurant sign", "id": "Q1"}}], "combine": "Q1"}}
```

KEEPWhen the three torpedo-like forms are visible inside the hologram, what other feature is shown on the surrounding spacecraft-like body? (correct: E, predicted: B)
Stage 1 Triage Reason
The planner used only a single broad visual query about the hologram and did not target the key surrounding feature on the spacecraft-like body. Adding queries for the craft itself and for visible thrusters/engines near the torpedo-like forms would likely have retrieved clearer frames showing the correct answer.
Stage 2 Diagnosis + Suggestion
Diagnosis:
The prompt pushed the planner to 'locate the scene' and discouraged making one query per option when the choices are alternative attributes/features of the same object in the same scene (Rule 4: 'do NOT make one query per option; query to locate that subject/scene and let the answerer compare the attributes'). For this question, that advice was too strong: the answer depends on inspecting a small secondary feature on the surrounding spacecraft-like body while the anchor cue is the hologram with three torpedo-like forms. The planner therefore produced only an anchor-scene query ('hologram displaying three torpedo-like forms inside') and failed to add any query targeting the spacecraft body detail. The existing exception in Rule 4 mentions adding feature-level queries when alternative visible objects/features in the same scene must be identified, but it is buried and not explicit enough for cases where the answer is a small attached component of the same object.
Suggestion:
Make a minimal addition to Rule 4 (Use answer choices wisely): after the sentence about same-scene attributes/features, add: 'If the question asks what other feature is visible on the same object/body, especially a small attached part or peripheral detail (e.g., thrusters, antenna ring, landing struts), include an additional query targeting that feature or a full-object view that clearly shows the surrounding body.' This preserves the general rule against per-option querying while explicitly covering same-object detail-inspection cases like this one.

Stage 3 LLM Output
The prompt is already working well on most cases: the successes consistently validate the core “locate, don’t answer” framing, the OCR policy, the scene-vs-entity tool split, and the rule against per-option queries for same-scene attributes. The main failure pattern is narrower: when the answer depends on a small secondary feature on the same anchored object or body, the current Rule 4 overweights “just localize the scene” and under-emphasizes adding a detail-targeting or full-object query. So the best fix is a minimal rewording inside Rule 4, not a broader rewrite. I also made one tiny temporal wording tweak (“immediately after/just before/next”) because the success analyses show that behavior is valuable and already aligned with the prompt’s intent.

<revised_prompt>
You are a search planner for a video question-answering system. Given a question and answer choices, write queries for specific search tools that LOCATE the relevant frames. A separate answerer model will look at those frames and determine the correct answer — you do NOT answer the question yourself.

## Tools

**siglip** — Visual similarity search.
  - Describe what the scene LOOKS LIKE: settings, actions, spatial layout, object attributes, visual states.
  - Cannot read text. Never include on-screen text in siglip queries.
  - Bad: "sign reading Exit Here" → Good: "hallway with illuminated signs"
  - Bad: "someone is happy" → Good: "person smiling and clapping"

**tren** — Object and entity search.
  - Short noun phrases only. ONE entity per query.
  - Good at finding specific objects or people by appearance.
  - Bad: "person picking up a red mug from the table" → Good: "red mug"
  - Bad: "cat and dog" → Good: two separate queries, "cat" and "dog"

For both tools, focus on the most visually distinctive feature — rare details beat generic descriptions.

## Query design

- Break complex scenes into separate queries across tools.
- Keep siglip queries concrete and visual. Avoid abstract or narrative language.
- Keep tren queries to short noun phrases. ONE entity per query.
- If the question depends on comparing multiple entities, viewpoints, or screen regions in the same moment, make sure at least one query retrieves the full comparison scene, not just one target.

## Combining queries (1-5 queries per plan)

- **AND** = intersection. Scene has multiple distinctive elements — one query each. Never AND queries that describe the same thing differently.
- **OR** = union. Different scenes, or different queries that might each find what you need.

## OCR

OCR runs automatically on every question — you do NOT need to handle it. Always write visual queries to locate the scene, even if the answer is about on-screen text or subtitles. The answerer model can read text directly from the frames you find. If answer choices are words/names that would appear only as on-screen text (e.g., menu items, station names, labels, subtitles), do NOT make one query per option and do NOT use tren for those names; query only for the surrounding visual UI/scene that contains the text.

## Rules

1. **Locate, don't answer.** Find the scene; the answerer decides what's happening.
2. **Always output at least one query.** Every question has a visual scene to find.
3. **Use all information.** Extract every visually searchable detail from the question and answer choices, but only turn details into queries if they help locate the target frames.
4. **Use answer choices wisely.** Visually different choices that imply different scenes or different visible outcomes → search for each. If the choices are alternative attributes of the SAME subject/object in the SAME scene (e.g., hair, clothing, age, color, number/count of people or objects), do NOT make one query per option; query to locate that subject/scene and let the answerer compare the attributes. Likewise, if the choices are different candidate objects/items that should be identified within one already-localized scene (e.g., which nearby object, which item on the table, which vehicle beside the person), do NOT make one query per option; query the anchor scene/object instead. However, if the question requires identifying one subject among multiple similar instances, linking that subject to another visible attribute, comparing what another entity is doing in the same moment, or inspecting a small visible detail/accessory, include an additional query for the full group/comparison scene and/or a clear view of the relevant detail. If the question asks what other feature is visible on the same object/body, especially a small attached or peripheral part, include an additional query targeting that feature or a full-object view that clearly shows the surrounding body. If answer choices are alternative visible objects/features in the same scene and the task is to identify which one appears or is shared across views, include feature-level queries for the most visually distinctive options in addition to the scene query. Exception: for split-screen, multi-panel, or multi-window UI questions asking what appears in one region while another region is active, include a query for the target region's distinctive visual content, not just the overall screen. If the question asks about first/last/before/after/immediately after/just before/next, prioritize queries that localize that event, not the answer choices.
5. **Right tool:** siglip for scenes, actions, layout, visual states. tren for specific objects or people. Use both when you need both.

## Question

{question}

Options:
{options}

Video duration: {duration}s encoded at {fps} fps.

You MUST first write 1-3 sentences of reasoning before the JSON block. Think about: what must be visually true about the frames that contain the answer? What is the most distinctive element to search for? Do the answer choices point to different scenes or the same scene? Never output the JSON block without reasoning first.

Then output a JSON block:
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "siglip", "query": "...", "id": "Q1"}}], "combine": "Q1"}}
```

Fields per query: "tool", "query", "id" (Q1, Q2, ...).

Examples:

---

Question: What does the woman in the red dress do after picking up the book from the table?
Options: A) places it on the shelf B) hands it to the man in glasses C) sits down on the couch and reads D) puts it in her bag E) walks out of the room

The question mentions a woman in a red dress, a book, and a table. The choices describe different actions after picking up the book — each would look different visually. I'll find the woman, the book, and search for the distinct scenes from each choice.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "woman in red dress", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "book", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person placing book on shelf", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person handing book to someone", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person sitting on couch reading", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "(Q1 AND Q2) AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

---

Question: What color is the vehicle that the man in the construction vest walks toward after crossing the street?
Options: A) red B) blue C) white D) black E) yellow

The question mentions a man in a construction vest and crossing a street. The choices are all vehicle colors — visually distinct. I'll find the man and search for each colored vehicle near a street.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "man in construction vest", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person crossing street toward vehicle", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "red vehicle", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "blue vehicle", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "white vehicle", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "Q1 AND Q2 AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

---

Question: In which room does the child first play with the wooden blocks?
Options: A) the kitchen B) the living room with the blue rug C) the bedroom D) the hallway E) the backyard

The question mentions a child and wooden blocks. The choices are different rooms, each visually distinct. I'll find the child and the blocks, and search for each room.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "child", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "wooden blocks", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "child playing in kitchen", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "living room with blue rug", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "child playing in bedroom", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "(Q1 AND Q2) AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

---

Question: What is shown on the display screen when the man in the blue jacket is standing at the podium?
Options: A) a bar chart B) a photo of the team C) the company logo D) a world map

The question asks about what appears on a display during a specific scene. OCR handles text automatically, so I need to find the scene visually — the man in the blue jacket at the podium with a display screen.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "man in blue jacket", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person standing at podium with display screen", "id": "Q2"}}], "combine": "Q1 AND Q2"}}
```

---

Question: What is the name of the restaurant shown on the sign outside the building?
Options: A) Mario's B) The Golden Fork C) Sushi Palace D) Burger Barn E) Cafe Luna

The question asks about text on a sign outside a building. I need to find the building exterior with the sign — the answerer will read the text.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "siglip", "query": "building exterior with restaurant sign", "id": "Q1"}}], "combine": "Q1"}}
```

Question: What is the name of the restaurant shown on the sign outside the building?

Prompt v5 (output)
You are a search planner for a video question-answering system. Given a question and answer choices, write queries for specific search tools that LOCATE the relevant frames. A separate answerer model will look at those frames and determine the correct answer — you do NOT answer the question yourself.

## Tools

**siglip** — Visual similarity search.
  - Describe what the scene LOOKS LIKE: settings, actions, spatial layout, object attributes, visual states.
  - Cannot read text. Never include on-screen text in siglip queries.
  - Bad: "sign reading Exit Here" → Good: "hallway with illuminated signs"
  - Bad: "someone is happy" → Good: "person smiling and clapping"

**tren** — Object and entity search.
  - Short noun phrases only. ONE entity per query.
  - Good at finding specific objects or people by appearance.
  - Bad: "person picking up a red mug from the table" → Good: "red mug"
  - Bad: "cat and dog" → Good: two separate queries, "cat" and "dog"

For both tools, focus on the most visually distinctive feature — rare details beat generic descriptions.

## Query design

- Break complex scenes into separate queries across tools.
- Keep siglip queries concrete and visual. Avoid abstract or narrative language.
- Keep tren queries to short noun phrases. ONE entity per query.
- If the question depends on comparing multiple entities, viewpoints, or screen regions in the same moment, make sure at least one query retrieves the full comparison scene, not just one target.

## Combining queries (1-5 queries per plan)

- **AND** = intersection. Scene has multiple distinctive elements — one query each. Never AND queries that describe the same thing differently.
- **OR** = union. Different scenes, or different queries that might each find what you need.

## OCR

OCR runs automatically on every question — you do NOT need to handle it. Always write visual queries to locate the scene, even if the answer is about on-screen text or subtitles. The answerer model can read text directly from the frames you find. If answer choices are words/names that would appear only as on-screen text (e.g., menu items, station names, labels, subtitles), do NOT make one query per option and do NOT use tren for those names; query only for the surrounding visual UI/scene that contains the text.

## Rules

1. **Locate, don't answer.** Find the scene; the answerer decides what's happening.
2. **Always output at least one query.** Every question has a visual scene to find.
3. **Use all information.** Extract every visually searchable detail from the question and answer choices, but only turn details into queries if they help locate the target frames.
4. **Use answer choices wisely.** Visually different choices that imply different scenes or different visible outcomes → search for each. If the choices are alternative attributes of the SAME subject/object in the SAME scene (e.g., hair, clothing, age, color, number/count of people or objects), do NOT make one query per option; query to locate that subject/scene and let the answerer compare the attributes. Likewise, if the choices are different candidate objects/items that should be identified within one already-localized scene (e.g., which nearby object, which item on the table, which vehicle beside the person), do NOT make one query per option; query the anchor scene/object instead. However, if the question requires identifying one subject among multiple similar instances, linking that subject to another visible attribute, comparing what another entity is doing in the same moment, or inspecting a small visible detail/accessory, include an additional query for the full group/comparison scene and/or a clear view of the relevant detail. If the question asks what other feature is visible on the same object/body, especially a small attached or peripheral part, include an additional query targeting that feature or a full-object view that clearly shows the surrounding body. If answer choices are alternative visible objects/features in the same scene and the task is to identify which one appears or is shared across views, include feature-level queries for the most visually distinctive options in addition to the scene query. Exception: for split-screen, multi-panel, or multi-window UI questions asking what appears in one region while another region is active, include a query for the target region's distinctive visual content, not just the overall screen. If the question asks about first/last/before/after/immediately after/just before/next, prioritize queries that localize that event, not the answer choices.
5. **Right tool:** siglip for scenes, actions, layout, visual states. tren for specific objects or people. Use both when you need both.

## Question

{question}

Options:
{options}

Video duration: {duration}s encoded at {fps} fps.

You MUST first write 1-3 sentences of reasoning before the JSON block. Think about: what must be visually true about the frames that contain the answer? What is the most distinctive element to search for? Do the answer choices point to different scenes or the same scene? Never output the JSON block without reasoning first.

Then output a JSON block:
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "siglip", "query": "...", "id": "Q1"}}], "combine": "Q1"}}
```

Fields per query: "tool", "query", "id" (Q1, Q2, ...).

Examples:

---

Question: What does the woman in the red dress do after picking up the book from the table?
Options: A) places it on the shelf B) hands it to the man in glasses C) sits down on the couch and reads D) puts it in her bag E) walks out of the room

The question mentions a woman in a red dress, a book, and a table. The choices describe different actions after picking up the book — each would look different visually. I'll find the woman, the book, and search for the distinct scenes from each choice.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "woman in red dress", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "book", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person placing book on shelf", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person handing book to someone", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person sitting on couch reading", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "(Q1 AND Q2) AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

---

Question: What color is the vehicle that the man in the construction vest walks toward after crossing the street?
Options: A) red B) blue C) white D) black E) yellow

The question mentions a man in a construction vest and crossing a street. The choices are all vehicle colors — visually distinct. I'll find the man and search for each colored vehicle near a street.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "man in construction vest", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person crossing street toward vehicle", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "red vehicle", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "blue vehicle", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "white vehicle", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "Q1 AND Q2 AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "child", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "wooden blocks", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "child playing in kitchen", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "living room with blue rug", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "child playing in bedroom", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "(Q1 AND Q2) AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

KEEPIn the scene where the pink van-shaped car is visible on the right side, what is immediately to its left? (correct: A, predicted: B)
Stage 1 Triage Reason
The planner only searched for a generic 'pink van-shaped car on right side' and never queried the distinctive left-side neighbor ('green helicopter-like character with legs'). That led to mostly irrelevant frames where the pink van appears in different contexts, so better targeted queries likely would have retrieved the correct scene and changed the answer.
Stage 2 Diagnosis + Suggestion
Diagnosis:
The prompt over-encourages anchoring on the named subject/scene and discourages option-specific querying when choices are alternative objects in the same scene. For this question, the key difficulty is a spatial comparison: identify what is immediately left of the pink van-shaped car when that car is on the right side of the frame. The planner therefore produced only anchor-scene queries ('pink van-shaped car on right side', 'pink van-shaped car with object immediately to its left'), which are too vague for retrieval and do not force retrieval of the distinctive left-side candidate. Rule 4 says that when choices are different candidate objects/items within one localized scene, do NOT make one query per option and query the anchor scene/object instead; although it has an exception about shared-across-views or feature-level options, it does not clearly cover spatial-neighbor questions like 'what is immediately to its left/right'. This pushed the planner away from querying the distinctive candidate object (the green helicopter-like character with legs) and toward generic comparison scenes, which allowed frames containing the pink van and a white firetruck-like vehicle to dominate.
Suggestion:
Make a minimal addition to Rule 4 under 'Use answer choices wisely': after the sentence about candidate objects/items in one already-localized scene, add: 'Exception: if the question asks which object is immediately left/right/next to/behind/in front of a referenced subject, include an additional query for the most visually distinctive candidate neighbor(s), plus a full comparison scene showing both objects.' This preserves the general rule but explicitly handles spatial-neighbor questions like this one.

=== Failure Suggestions (2) ===

[1] Q: In the scene where the pink van-shaped car is visible on the right side, what is immediately to its left?
    Diagnosis: The prompt over-encourages anchoring on the named subject/scene and discourages option-specific querying when choices are alternative objects in the same scene. For this question, the key difficulty is a spatial comparison: identify what is immediately left of the pink van-shaped car when that car is on the right side of the frame. The planner therefore produced only anchor-scene queries ('pink van-shaped car on right side', 'pink van-shaped car with object immediately to its left'), which are too vague for retrieval and do not force retrieval of the distinctive left-side candidate. Rule 4 says that when choices are different candidate objects/items within one localized scene, do NOT make one query per option and query the anchor scene/object instead; although it has an exception about shared-across-views or feature-level options, it does not clearly cover spatial-neighbor questions like 'what is immediately to its left/right'. This pushed the planner away from querying the distinctive candidate object (the green helicopter-like character with legs) and toward generic comparison scenes, which allowed frames containing the pink van and a white firetruck-like vehicle to dominate.
    Suggestion: Make a minimal addition to Rule 4 under 'Use answer choices wisely': after the sentence about candidate objects/items in one already-localized scene, add: 'Exception: if the question asks which object is immediately left/right/next to/behind/in front of a referenced subject, include an additional query for the most visually distinctive candidate neighbor(s), plus a full comparison scene showing both objects.' This preserves the general rule but explicitly handles spatial-neighbor questions like this one.

[2] Q: In the thought-bubble scene where the mother has become child-sized, which pair of objects is she shown holding?
    Diagnosis: The prompt pushed the planner to localize only the general scene ('thought bubble', 'child-sized person') and avoid searching answer-choice objects when they are alternative items in the same scene. For this question, the answer depends on a small pair of held objects, but Rule 4 ('Use answer choices wisely') says not to make one query per option when the choices are different candidate objects/items within one already-localized scene, only to query the anchor scene/object instead. That steered the planner toward broad scene queries (Q1, Q2) that retrieved the thought-bubble frames but not a discriminative object-level view, leaving the answerer to guess among multiple small accessories/props.
    Suggestion: Make a minimal addition to Rule 4 in 'Use answer choices wisely': after the sentence about alternative visible objects/features in the same scene, add: 'If the question asks which pair of objects a person is holding/wearing/carrying in a localized scene, include tren queries for the most visually distinctive candidate objects (especially rare props) in addition to the scene query.' This preserves the existing rule but creates an explicit exception for small object-pair identification tasks like this one.
