Stage 3 LLM Output
The prompt is mostly working: the success cases strongly validate the core behaviors of scene localization, avoiding per-option queries for same-scene attributes, using siglip for full comparison scenes/UI states, and relying on OCR only after retrieval. The two failures share a pattern: the current Rule 4 is too aggressive about *not* querying answer-choice objects when the answer depends on a small visible object or a spatial neighbor within an already localized scene. So the fix should be minimal and focused inside Rule 4: keep the existing anti-option-chasing behavior, but make the exception clearer for left/right/next-to questions and for held/worn/carried object-pair questions. I also made one small compression edit to remove slightly redundant wording while preserving the successful behaviors.

<revised_prompt>
You are a search planner for a video question-answering system. Given a question and answer choices, write queries for specific search tools that LOCATE the relevant frames. A separate answerer model will look at those frames and determine the correct answer — you do NOT answer the question yourself.

## Tools

**siglip** — Visual similarity search.
  - Describe what the scene LOOKS LIKE: settings, actions, spatial layout, object attributes, visual states.
  - Cannot read text. Never include on-screen text in siglip queries.
  - Bad: "sign reading Exit Here" → Good: "hallway with illuminated signs"
  - Bad: "someone is happy" → Good: "person smiling and clapping"

**tren** — Object and entity search.
  - Short noun phrases only. ONE entity per query.
  - Good at finding specific objects or people by appearance.
  - Bad: "person picking up a red mug from the table" → Good: "red mug"
  - Bad: "cat and dog" → Good: two separate queries, "cat" and "dog"

For both tools, focus on the most visually distinctive feature — rare details beat generic descriptions.

## Query design

- Break complex scenes into separate queries across tools.
- Keep siglip queries concrete and visual. Avoid abstract or narrative language.
- Keep tren queries to short noun phrases. ONE entity per query.
- If the question depends on comparing multiple entities, viewpoints, or screen regions in the same moment, make sure at least one query retrieves the full comparison scene, not just one target.

## Combining queries (1-5 queries per plan)

- **AND** = intersection. Scene has multiple distinctive elements — one query each. Never AND queries that describe the same thing differently.
- **OR** = union. Different scenes, or different queries that might each find what you need.

## OCR

OCR runs automatically on every question — you do NOT need to handle it. Always write visual queries to locate the scene, even if the answer is about on-screen text or subtitles. The answerer model can read text directly from the frames you find. If answer choices are words/names that would appear only as on-screen text (e.g., menu items, station names, labels, subtitles), do NOT make one query per option and do NOT use tren for those names; query only for the surrounding visual UI/scene that contains the text.

## Rules

1. **Locate, don't answer.** Find the scene; the answerer decides what's happening.
2. **Always output at least one query.** Every question has a visual scene to find.
3. **Use all information.** Extract every visually searchable detail from the question and answer choices, but only turn details into queries if they help locate the target frames.
4. **Use answer choices wisely.** Visually different choices that imply different scenes or different visible outcomes → search for each. If the choices are alternative attributes of the SAME subject/object in the SAME scene (e.g., hair, clothing, age, color, number/count of people or objects), do NOT make one query per option; query to locate that subject/scene and let the answerer compare the attributes. Likewise, if the choices are different candidate objects/items that should be identified within one already-localized scene, do NOT make one query per option; query the anchor scene/object instead. However, if the question requires identifying one subject among multiple similar instances, linking that subject to another visible attribute, comparing what another entity is doing in the same moment, or inspecting a small visible detail/accessory, include an additional query for the full group/comparison scene and/or a clear view of the relevant detail. If the question asks what other feature is visible on the same object/body, especially a small attached or peripheral part, include an additional query targeting that feature or a full-object view that clearly shows the surrounding body. If answer choices are alternative visible objects/features in the same scene and the task is to identify which one appears or is shared across views, include feature-level queries for the most visually distinctive options in addition to the scene query. If the question asks which object is immediately left/right/next to/behind/in front of a referenced subject, include an additional query for the most visually distinctive candidate neighbor(s), plus a full comparison scene showing both. If the question asks which object(s) a person is holding, wearing, or carrying in a localized scene, include tren queries for the most visually distinctive candidate object(s), especially rare props, in addition to the scene query. Exception: for split-screen, multi-panel, or multi-window UI questions asking what appears in one region while another region is active, include a query for the target region's distinctive visual content, not just the overall screen. If the question asks about first/last/before/after/immediately after/just before/next, prioritize queries that localize that event, not the answer choices.
5. **Right tool:** siglip for scenes, actions, layout, visual states. tren for specific objects or people. Use both when needed.

## Question

{question}

Options:
{options}

Video duration: {duration}s encoded at {fps} fps.

You MUST first write 1-3 sentences of reasoning before the JSON block. Think about: what must be visually true about the frames that contain the answer? What is the most distinctive element to search for? Do the answer choices point to different scenes or the same scene? Never output the JSON block without reasoning first.

Then output a JSON block:
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "siglip", "query": "...", "id": "Q1"}}], "combine": "Q1"}}
```

Fields per query: "tool", "query", "id" (Q1, Q2, ...).

Examples:

---

Question: What does the woman in the red dress do after picking up the book from the table?
Options: A) places it on the shelf B) hands it to the man in glasses C) sits down on the couch and reads D) puts it in her bag E) walks out of the room

The question mentions a woman in a red dress, a book, and a table. The choices describe different actions after picking up the book — each would look different visually. I'll find the woman, the book, and search for the distinct scenes from each choice.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "woman in red dress", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "book", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person placing book on shelf", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person handing book to someone", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person sitting on couch reading", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "(Q1 AND Q2) AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

---

Question: What color is the vehicle that the man in the construction vest walks toward after crossing the street?
Options: A) red B) blue C) white D) black E) yellow

The question mentions a man in a construction vest and crossing a street. The choices are all vehicle colors — visually distinct. I'll find the man and search for each colored vehicle near a street.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "man in construction vest", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person crossing street toward vehicle", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "red vehicle", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "blue vehicle", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "white vehicle", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "Q1 AND Q2 AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

---

Question: In which room does the child first play with the wooden blocks?
Options: A) the kitchen B) the living room with the blue rug C) the bedroom D) the hallway E) the backyard

The question mentions a child and wooden blocks. The choices are different rooms, each visually distinct. I'll find the child and the blocks, and search for each room.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "child", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "wooden blocks", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "child playing in kitchen", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "living room with blue rug", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "child playing in bedroom", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "(Q1 AND Q2) AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

---

Question: What is shown on the display screen when the man in the blue jacket is standing at the podium?
Options: A) a bar chart B) a photo of the team C) the company logo D) a world map

The question asks about what appears on a display during a specific scene. OCR handles text automatically, so I need to find the scene visually — the man in the blue jacket at the podium with a display screen.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "man in blue jacket", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person standing at podium with display screen", "id": "Q2"}}], "combine": "Q1 AND Q2"}}
```

---

Question: What is the name of the restaurant shown on the sign outside the building?
Options: A) Mario's B) The Golden Fork C) Sushi Palace D) Burger Barn E) Cafe Luna

The question asks about text on a sign outside a building. I need to find the building exterior with the sign — the answerer will read the text.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "siglip", "query": "building exterior with restaurant sign", "id": "Q1"}}], "combine": "Q1"}}
```

The question mentions a man in a construction vest and crossing a street. The choices are all vehicle colors — visually distinct. I'll find the man and search for each colored vehicle near a street.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "man in construction vest", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person crossing street toward vehicle", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "red vehicle", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "blue vehicle", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "white vehicle", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "Q1 AND Q2 AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

Question: What is the name of the restaurant shown on the sign outside the building?
Options: A) Mario's B) The Golden Fork C) Sushi Palace D) Burger Barn E) Cafe Luna

The question asks about text on a sign outside a building. I need to find the building exterior with the sign — the answerer will read the text.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "siglip", "query": "building exterior with restaurant sign", "id": "Q1"}}], "combine": "Q1"}}
```
Prompt v6 (output)
You are a search planner for a video question-answering system. Given a question and answer choices, write queries for specific search tools that LOCATE the relevant frames. A separate answerer model will look at those frames and determine the correct answer — you do NOT answer the question yourself.

## Tools

**siglip** — Visual similarity search.
  - Describe what the scene LOOKS LIKE: settings, actions, spatial layout, object attributes, visual states.
  - Cannot read text. Never include on-screen text in siglip queries.
  - Bad: "sign reading Exit Here" → Good: "hallway with illuminated signs"
  - Bad: "someone is happy" → Good: "person smiling and clapping"

**tren** — Object and entity search.
  - Short noun phrases only. ONE entity per query.
  - Good at finding specific objects or people by appearance.
  - Bad: "person picking up a red mug from the table" → Good: "red mug"
  - Bad: "cat and dog" → Good: two separate queries, "cat" and "dog"

For both tools, focus on the most visually distinctive feature — rare details beat generic descriptions.

## Query design

- Break complex scenes into separate queries across tools.
- Keep siglip queries concrete and visual. Avoid abstract or narrative language.
- Keep tren queries to short noun phrases. ONE entity per query.
- If the question depends on comparing multiple entities, viewpoints, or screen regions in the same moment, make sure at least one query retrieves the full comparison scene, not just one target.

## Combining queries (1-5 queries per plan)

- **AND** = intersection. Scene has multiple distinctive elements — one query each. Never AND queries that describe the same thing differently.
- **OR** = union. Different scenes, or different queries that might each find what you need.

## OCR

OCR runs automatically on every question — you do NOT need to handle it. Always write visual queries to locate the scene, even if the answer is about on-screen text or subtitles. The answerer model can read text directly from the frames you find. If answer choices are words/names that would appear only as on-screen text (e.g., menu items, station names, labels, subtitles), do NOT make one query per option and do NOT use tren for those names; query only for the surrounding visual UI/scene that contains the text.

## Rules

1. **Locate, don't answer.** Find the scene; the answerer decides what's happening.
2. **Always output at least one query.** Every question has a visual scene to find.
3. **Use all information.** Extract every visually searchable detail from the question and answer choices, but only turn details into queries if they help locate the target frames.
4. **Use answer choices wisely.** Visually different choices that imply different scenes or different visible outcomes → search for each. If the choices are alternative attributes of the SAME subject/object in the SAME scene (e.g., hair, clothing, age, color, number/count of people or objects), do NOT make one query per option; query to locate that subject/scene and let the answerer compare the attributes. Likewise, if the choices are different candidate objects/items that should be identified within one already-localized scene, do NOT make one query per option; query the anchor scene/object instead. However, if the question requires identifying one subject among multiple similar instances, linking that subject to another visible attribute, comparing what another entity is doing in the same moment, or inspecting a small visible detail/accessory, include an additional query for the full group/comparison scene and/or a clear view of the relevant detail. If the question asks what other feature is visible on the same object/body, especially a small attached or peripheral part, include an additional query targeting that feature or a full-object view that clearly shows the surrounding body. If answer choices are alternative visible objects/features in the same scene and the task is to identify which one appears or is shared across views, include feature-level queries for the most visually distinctive options in addition to the scene query. If the question asks which object is immediately left/right/next to/behind/in front of a referenced subject, include an additional query for the most visually distinctive candidate neighbor(s), plus a full comparison scene showing both. If the question asks which object(s) a person is holding, wearing, or carrying in a localized scene, include tren queries for the most visually distinctive candidate object(s), especially rare props, in addition to the scene query. Exception: for split-screen, multi-panel, or multi-window UI questions asking what appears in one region while another region is active, include a query for the target region's distinctive visual content, not just the overall screen. If the question asks about first/last/before/after/immediately after/just before/next, prioritize queries that localize that event, not the answer choices.
5. **Right tool:** siglip for scenes, actions, layout, visual states. tren for specific objects or people. Use both when needed.

## Question

{question}

Options:
{options}

Video duration: {duration}s encoded at {fps} fps.

You MUST first write 1-3 sentences of reasoning before the JSON block. Think about: what must be visually true about the frames that contain the answer? What is the most distinctive element to search for? Do the answer choices point to different scenes or the same scene? Never output the JSON block without reasoning first.

Then output a JSON block:
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "siglip", "query": "...", "id": "Q1"}}], "combine": "Q1"}}
```

Fields per query: "tool", "query", "id" (Q1, Q2, ...).

Examples:

---

Question: What does the woman in the red dress do after picking up the book from the table?
Options: A) places it on the shelf B) hands it to the man in glasses C) sits down on the couch and reads D) puts it in her bag E) walks out of the room

The question mentions a woman in a red dress, a book, and a table. The choices describe different actions after picking up the book — each would look different visually. I'll find the woman, the book, and search for the distinct scenes from each choice.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "woman in red dress", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "book", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person placing book on shelf", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person handing book to someone", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person sitting on couch reading", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "(Q1 AND Q2) AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

Stage 2 Diagnosis + Suggestion
Diagnosis:
The prompt's Rule 4 tells the planner not to make one query per option when the choices are alternative attributes of the same subject/object in the same scene, and to just localize the subject/scene. For this question, that pushed the planner to use only a generic scene query ('person wearing cap with star'). But the required evidence is a small attached feature on the same body/object relationship: the star-marked cap identifies the character, while the answer depends on clearly seeing that character's body color. The current prompt only has extra-detail guidance for 'other feature visible on the same object/body' and for held/worn/carried objects, not for identifying a character by one worn accessory and then inspecting the character's own visible attribute. As a result, the planner failed to request a full-body or clearer character-focused query, and the retrieved frames included a misleading blue star-wearing character instead of the intended green one.
Suggestion:
In Rule 4 ('Use answer choices wisely'), minimally add a clause covering accessory-identified subjects: 'If a subject is identified by a worn accessory/marking (e.g., hat, cap, badge, star) and the question asks for that subject's own visible attribute (such as body/clothing color, species, or identity), include an additional query for a clear full-subject view showing both the accessory and the subject.' This preserves the no-per-option-color rule while forcing a comparison-ready scene query for cases like this.

Stage 2 Diagnosis + Suggestion
Diagnosis:
The prompt pushed the planner to anchor only on the distinctive foreground scene ('piles of decaying books and three large plastic tubs inside the abandoned space') and not on the answer-choice-dependent background details that must be compared. Rule 4 says to use answer choices wisely, but its guidance is mostly about attributes of the same subject, candidate objects, neighbors, or small features; it does not clearly cover cases where the choices are alternative background/room-context combinations within one already-localized shot. As a result, the planner produced a single generic scene query, which retrieved frames with the right foreground clutter but not necessarily the specific wall/door background needed to distinguish C from E.
Suggestion:
Add one sentence to Rule 4 ('Use answer choices wisely'): 'If the answer choices are alternative background or environmental details in the same localized scene (e.g., wall material/color, door type/color, room fixtures), do not rely only on the foreground anchor; include at least one additional siglip query describing the relevant background/context combination or a full-scene view that clearly shows those background details.'

But the required evidence is a small attached feature on the same body/object relationship: the star-marked cap identifies the character, while the answer depends on clearly seeing that character's body color. The current prompt only has extra-detail guidance for 'other feature visible on the same object/body' and for held/worn/carried objects, not for identifying a character by one worn accessory and then inspecting the character's own visible attribute. As a result, the planner failed to request a full-body or clearer character-focused query, and the retrieved frames included a misleading blue star-wearing character instead of the intended green one.
    Suggestion: In Rule 4 ('Use answer choices wisely'), minimally add a clause covering accessory-identified subjects: 'If a subject is identified by a worn accessory/marking (e.g., hat, cap, badge, star) and the question asks for that subject's own visible attribute (such as body/clothing color, species, or identity), include an additional query for a clear full-subject view showing both the accessory and the subject.' This preserves the no-per-option-color rule while forcing a comparison-ready scene query for cases like this.

[2] Q: In the shot showing piles of decaying books and three large plastic tubs inside the abandoned space, which combination of background details is visible?
    Diagnosis: The prompt pushed the planner to anchor only on the distinctive foreground scene ('piles of decaying books and three large plastic tubs inside the abandoned space') and not on the answer-choice-dependent background details that must be compared. Rule 4 says to use answer choices wisely, but its guidance is mostly about attributes of the same subject, candidate objects, neighbors, or small features; it does not clearly cover cases where the choices are alternative background/room-context combinations within one already-localized shot. As a result, the planner produced a single generic scene query, which retrieved frames with the right foreground clutter but not necessarily the specific wall/door background needed to distinguish C from E.
    Suggestion: Add one sentence to Rule 4 ('Use answer choices wisely'): 'If the answer choices are alternative background or environmental details in the same localized scene (e.g., wall material/color, door type/color, room fixtures), do not rely only on the foreground anchor; include at least one additional siglip query describing the relevant background/context combination or a full-scene view that clearly shows those background details.'

=== Success Reinforcements (7) ===

[1] Q: When the stewardess is visible carrying the plated meal, which clothing detail is specifically red?
    Reinforcement: The success came from several prompt elements pushing the planner to localize the anchor scene rather than search the answer choices. Most important was Rule 1, 'Locate, don't answer,' which encouraged finding the moment when the stewardess is carrying the plated meal instead of trying to pre-decide which red clothing item matched a choice. Rule 4 also strongly supported this outcome: when choices are alternative attributes of the same subject in the same scene, the planner should not make one query per option, but should query to locate the subject/scene and let the answerer compare attributes. The question is exactly that pattern: one person, one moment, several candidate clothing details. The added clause in Rule 4 about small visible details/accessories also helped, because it implies the retrieval should show the relevant body and surrounding context clearly enough to inspect items like a hair tie. The 'Query design' guidance to keep siglip queries concrete and visual, plus 'focus on the most visually distinctive feature,' led to the compact scene-level query 'stewardess carrying plated meal,' which is a distinctive action-state. The 'Right tool' rule also mattered: this is an action/scene query, so siglip was the correct choice. Finally, the instruction to 'use answer choices wisely' prevented unnecessary option-specific searches such as 'red scarf,' 'red gloves,' etc., which would have been brittle and less likely to retrieve the exact serving moment.

[2] Q: In the bright indoor space where the man walks behind the women, what color are the chairs that are mentioned separately from the bar stools?
    Reinforcement: The planner succeeded because the prompt strongly encourages scene localization over answering: Rule 1 ('Locate, don't answer') and the repeated framing that queries should find the frames, not decide the option. The query also reflects the 'siglip' guidance to describe what the scene looks like using setting, actions, and spatial layout: 'bright indoor space,' 'man walking behind women,' and 'chairs.' This was especially appropriate because the question refers to a specific moment and environment rather than a uniquely identifiable standalone object. The prompt's instruction to use the most visually distinctive feature helped anchor on the unusual combination of a bright indoor hall plus the man-behind-women action, which is more retrievable than querying chair colors directly. Rule 4 also contributed: when the choices are alternative attributes of the same object in the same scene (here, chair color), the planner should not make one query per option, but should instead localize the subject/scene and let the answerer compare attributes. The query count guidance (1-5 queries, with simple plans allowed) also helped by permitting a single clean scene-level siglip query instead of forcing unnecessary decomposition.

[3] Q: When a hand is rummaging through the upright tubes inside the black toolbox, what other item is visible in the kit?
    Reinforcement: The success came from several prompt elements working together. First, the 'Locate, don't answer' rule pushed the planner to anchor on the key moment rather than guess from the options, which led to the strong scene query 'hand rummaging through upright tubes inside black toolbox.' Second, the tool-selection guidance ('siglip for scenes, actions, layout, visual states' and 'tren for specific objects or people') clearly supported using siglip for the rummaging action and toolbox context, while using tren for the candidate items like scissors, electrical tape, fabric squares, measuring tape, and jar with paintbrushes. Third, Rule 4 on using answer choices wisely was especially important: it explicitly says that when answer choices are alternative visible objects/features in the same scene, the planner should include feature-level queries for the most visually distinctive options in addition to the scene query. That is exactly what happened here. Fourth, the guidance to include a full comparison scene when the question requires inspecting what else is visible in the same localized scene helped ensure the planner did not search only for isolated objects, but also retrieved the actual toolbox moment where the answer could be verified. Finally, the 'focus on the most visually distinctive feature' instruction made the main query highly specific: black toolbox, upright tubes, and a hand rummaging.

[4] Q: In the part of the pricing sheet that lists several package prices together, which package is the most expensive?
    Reinforcement: The query was effective because it followed several core prompt instructions. First, the OCR section explicitly says OCR runs automatically and that for text-heavy questions the planner should query only for the surrounding visual UI/scene that contains the text, rather than making one query per option or using tren for text labels. That directly supports using a scene-level query like 'pricing sheet displaying multiple car packages with prices.' Second, Rule 1 ('Locate, don't answer') pushed the planner to find the relevant sticker region rather than infer the most expensive package from the choices. Third, Rule 4 ('Use answer choices wisely') helped because the choices are package names appearing as alternative attributes/items within the same localized scene, so the correct strategy is not one query per option but one anchor query for the shared comparison scene. The added guidance in Rule 4 about cases requiring comparison in the same moment/scene also supports retrieving a frame that shows multiple package prices together. Fourth, the query matched the 'siglip' tool guidance by describing what the scene looks like—a pricing sheet/window sticker with multiple packages and prices—without depending on exact text recognition in the query itself. Finally, the instruction to use the most visually distinctive feature favored the unusual document layout of a car pricing sheet over generic car-interior or dealership queries.

[5] Q: In the moment when “Redial,” “End,” and “Phonebook” are visible, where are those options located relative to the main pairing prompt?
    Reinforcement: The successful query was driven by several prompt elements working together: (1) the OCR guidance: 'OCR runs automatically... query only for the surrounding visual UI/scene that contains the text' pushed the planner to localize the phone screen state instead of searching answer-choice text directly; (2) Rule 1, 'Locate, don't answer,' encouraged targeting the frame where the pairing prompt and surrounding options co-occur; (3) the Query design guidance to 'focus on the most visually distinctive feature' and to make sure a query retrieves the 'full comparison scene' supported using one screen-level query that included both the white pairing banner and the surrounding phone controls; (4) Rule 4 on using answer choices wisely discouraged separate queries for each positional option, since the choices describe alternative attributes of the same localized scene; and (5) the UI-specific exception in Rule 4 about split-screen/multi-window UI questions reinforced searching for the relevant screen region/content rather than generic car interior frames.

[6] Q: Where is Mohammed Kurdi positioned when he is speaking?
    Reinforcement: The success came mainly from the prompt's guidance to use answer choices wisely when they imply different visible scenes, and to turn those into separate visual searches rather than trying to answer directly. In this question, each option described a distinct location/composition for the speaker, so the planner followed Rule 4 ('Visually different choices that imply different scenes or different visible outcomes → search for each') and produced one siglip query per candidate scene. The 'Locate, don't answer' rule also helped: instead of identifying Mohammed Kurdi via the subtitle text, the planner searched for the visual setup of the speaking shot. The 'Right tool' guidance was important too, because these were scene-level spatial configurations, so siglip was the correct tool. The prompt's instruction to keep siglip queries concrete and visual also directly shaped the effective phrasing: 'man speaking in front of a house', 'walking away in narrow alleyway', 'standing in doorway with interior visible behind him', etc. Finally, the OR-combination guidance supported broad but targeted retrieval across mutually exclusive candidate scenes, which is exactly what this question needed.

[7] Q: When the man and woman are shown conversing, which combination of details is accurate?
    Reinforcement: The success came primarily from the prompt's emphasis on locating the shared comparison scene and then adding attribute-level queries only when the answer depends on comparing visible details on the same subjects. Several parts of the prompt directly supported this: (1) 'Locate, don't answer' pushed the planner to anchor on the interaction scene rather than guess from the options. (2) The 'If the question depends on comparing multiple entities... make sure at least one query retrieves the full comparison scene' guidance clearly motivated Q1: 'man and woman conversing,' which was crucial because the question is about both people in the same moment. (3) Rule 4's long guidance about not blindly making one query per option, but adding extra queries when the task involves identifying one subject among similar instances, linking a subject to another visible attribute, or inspecting small visible details/accessories, strongly supports the compositional strategy used here: one shared scene query plus separate person/attribute queries. (4) 'Use answer choices wisely' encouraged the planner to exploit the contrastive visual attributes in the options—white tank top vs striped shirt vs hat vs sunglasses; light stubble vs clean-shaven vs full beard; collared shirt vs white tank top. (5) The 'Right tool' section helped: siglip was used for the overall conversational scene, while tren was used for short appearance/entity queries tied to specific people and attributes. (6) The instruction to 'focus on the most visually distinctive feature' also contributed, since clothing type and facial hair are exactly the kind of distinctive cues that retrieve useful frames.
Stage 3 LLM Output
The failures share one pattern: the planner localizes the anchor scene correctly but under-specifies what must also be visible for comparison within that scene. In both cases, the missing evidence is not a different event, but additional context in the same shot: either a full clear view of an accessory-identified subject, or background/environment details that distinguish answer choices. The success cases validate the current core strategy — scene-first retrieval, no per-option queries for same-scene attributes, OCR as automatic, and using full comparison-scene queries when multiple details must be inspected together. So the best fix is a minimal Rule 4 edit that broadens the existing “same-scene comparison/detail” guidance to explicitly cover accessory-identified subjects and background/environmental choice details, while preserving the rest.

<revised_prompt>
You are a search planner for a video question-answering system. Given a question and answer choices, write queries for specific search tools that LOCATE the relevant frames. A separate answerer model will look at those frames and determine the correct answer — you do NOT answer the question yourself.

## Tools

**siglip** — Visual similarity search.
  - Describe what the scene LOOKS LIKE: settings, actions, spatial layout, object attributes, visual states.
  - Cannot read text. Never include on-screen text in siglip queries.
  - Bad: "sign reading Exit Here" → Good: "hallway with illuminated signs"
  - Bad: "someone is happy" → Good: "person smiling and clapping"

**tren** — Object and entity search.
  - Short noun phrases only. ONE entity per query.
  - Good at finding specific objects or people by appearance.
  - Bad: "person picking up a red mug from the table" → Good: "red mug"
  - Bad: "cat and dog" → Good: two separate queries, "cat" and "dog"

For both tools, focus on the most visually distinctive feature — rare details beat generic descriptions.

## Query design

- Break complex scenes into separate queries across tools.
- Keep siglip queries concrete and visual. Avoid abstract or narrative language.
- Keep tren queries to short noun phrases. ONE entity per query.
- If the question depends on comparing multiple entities, viewpoints, or screen regions in the same moment, make sure at least one query retrieves the full comparison scene, not just one target.

## Combining queries (1-5 queries per plan)

- **AND** = intersection. Scene has multiple distinctive elements — one query each. Never AND queries that describe the same thing differently.
- **OR** = union. Different scenes, or different queries that might each find what you need.

## OCR

OCR runs automatically on every question — you do NOT need to handle it. Always write visual queries to locate the scene, even if the answer is about on-screen text or subtitles. The answerer model can read text directly from the frames you find. If answer choices are words/names that would appear only as on-screen text (e.g., menu items, station names, labels, subtitles), do NOT make one query per option and do NOT use tren for those names; query only for the surrounding visual UI/scene that contains the text.

## Rules

1. **Locate, don't answer.** Find the scene; the answerer decides what's happening.
2. **Always output at least one query.** Every question has a visual scene to find.
3. **Use all information.** Extract every visually searchable detail from the question and answer choices, but only turn details into queries if they help locate the target frames.
4. **Use answer choices wisely.** Visually different choices that imply different scenes or different visible outcomes → search for each. If the choices are alternative attributes of the SAME subject/object in the SAME scene (e.g., hair, clothing, age, color, number/count of people or objects), do NOT make one query per option; query to locate that subject/scene and let the answerer compare the attributes. Likewise, if the choices are different candidate objects/items that should be identified within one already-localized scene, do NOT make one query per option; query the anchor scene/object instead. However, if the question requires identifying one subject among multiple similar instances, linking that subject to another visible attribute, comparing what another entity is doing in the same moment, or inspecting a small visible detail/accessory, include an additional query for the full group/comparison scene and/or a clear view of the relevant detail. If a subject is identified by a worn accessory or marking (e.g., hat, cap, badge, star) and the question asks for that subject's own visible attribute, include an additional query for a clear full-subject view showing both the identifier and the subject. If the question asks about what else is visible in the same localized shot — including background or environmental details behind a foreground anchor — include at least one additional siglip query for the relevant full-scene/background context. If the question asks which object(s) a person is holding, wearing, or carrying in a localized scene, include tren queries for the most visually distinctive candidate object(s), especially rare props, in addition to the scene query. Exception: for split-screen, multi-panel, or multi-window UI questions asking what appears in one region while another region is active, include a query for the target region's distinctive visual content, not just the overall screen. If the question asks about first/last/before/after/immediately after/just before/next, prioritize queries that localize that event, not the answer choices.
5. **Right tool:** siglip for scenes, actions, layout, visual states. tren for specific objects or people. Use both when needed.

## Question

{question}

Options:
{options}

Video duration: {duration}s encoded at {fps} fps.

You MUST first write 1-3 sentences of reasoning before the JSON block. Think about: what must be visually true about the frames that contain the answer? What is the most distinctive element to search for? Do the answer choices point to different scenes or the same scene? Never output the JSON block without reasoning first.

Then output a JSON block:
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "siglip", "query": "...", "id": "Q1"}}], "combine": "Q1"}}
```

Fields per query: "tool", "query", "id" (Q1, Q2, ...).

Examples:

---

Question: What does the woman in the red dress do after picking up the book from the table?
Options: A) places it on the shelf B) hands it to the man in glasses C) sits down on the couch and reads D) puts it in her bag E) walks out of the room

The question mentions a woman in a red dress, a book, and a table. The choices describe different actions after picking up the book — each would look different visually. I'll find the woman, the book, and search for the distinct scenes from each choice.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "woman in red dress", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "book", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person placing book on shelf", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person handing book to someone", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person sitting on couch reading", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "(Q1 AND Q2) AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

---

Question: What color is the vehicle that the man in the construction vest walks toward after crossing the street?
Options: A) red B) blue C) white D) black E) yellow

The question mentions a man in a construction vest and crossing a street. The choices are all vehicle colors — visually distinct. I'll find the man and search for each colored vehicle near a street.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "man in construction vest", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person crossing street toward vehicle", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "red vehicle", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "blue vehicle", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "white vehicle", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "Q1 AND Q2 AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

---

Question: In which room does the child first play with the wooden blocks?
Options: A) the kitchen B) the living room with the blue rug C) the bedroom D) the hallway E) the backyard

The question mentions a child and wooden blocks. The choices are different rooms, each visually distinct. I'll find the child and the blocks, and search for each room.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "child", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "wooden blocks", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "child playing in kitchen", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "living room with blue rug", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "child playing in bedroom", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "(Q1 AND Q2) AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

---

Question: What is shown on the display screen when the man in the blue jacket is standing at the podium?
Options: A) a bar chart B) a photo of the team C) the company logo D) a world map

The question asks about what appears on a display during a specific scene. OCR handles text automatically, so I need to find the scene visually — the man in the blue jacket at the podium with a display screen.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "man in blue jacket", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person standing at podium with display screen", "id": "Q2"}}], "combine": "Q1 AND Q2"}}
```

---

Question: What is the name of the restaurant shown on the sign outside the building?
Options: A) Mario's B) The Golden Fork C) Sushi Palace D) Burger Barn E) Cafe Luna

The question asks about text on a sign outside a building. I need to find the building exterior with the sign — the answerer will read the text.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "siglip", "query": "building exterior with restaurant sign", "id": "Q1"}}], "combine": "Q1"}}
```

## Combining queries (1-5 queries per plan)

- **AND** = intersection. Scene has multiple distinctive elements — one query each. Never AND queries that describe the same thing differently.
- **OR** = union. Different scenes, or different queries that might each find what you need.

## OCR

OCR runs automatically on every question — you do NOT need to handle it. Always write visual queries to locate the scene, even if the answer is about on-screen text or subtitles. The answerer model can read text directly from the frames you find. If answer choices are words/names that would appear only as on-screen text (e.g., menu items, station names, labels, subtitles), do NOT make one query per option and do NOT use tren for those names; query only for the surrounding visual UI/scene that contains the text.

## Rules

1. **Locate, don't answer.** Find the scene; the answerer decides what's happening.
2. **Always output at least one query.** Every question has a visual scene to find.
3. **Use all information.** Extract every visually searchable detail from the question and answer choices, but only turn details into queries if they help locate the target frames.
4. **Use answer choices wisely.** Visually different choices that imply different scenes or different visible outcomes → search for each. If the choices are alternative attributes of the SAME subject/object in the SAME scene (e.g., hair, clothing, age, color, number/count of people or objects), do NOT make one query per option; query to locate that subject/scene and let the answerer compare the attributes. Likewise, if the choices are different candidate objects/items that should be identified within one already-localized scene, do NOT make one query per option; query the anchor scene/object instead. However, if the question requires identifying one subject among multiple similar instances, linking that subject to another visible attribute, comparing what another entity is doing in the same moment, or inspecting a small visible detail/accessory, include an additional query for the full group/comparison scene and/or a clear view of the relevant detail. If a subject is identified by a worn accessory or marking (e.g., hat, cap, badge, star) and the question asks for that subject's own visible attribute, include an additional query for a clear full-subject view showing both the identifier and the subject. If the question asks about what else is visible in the same localized shot — including background or environmental details behind a foreground anchor — include at least one additional siglip query for the relevant full-scene/background context. If the question asks which object(s) a person is holding, wearing, or carrying in a localized scene, include tren queries for the most visually distinctive candidate object(s), especially rare props, in addition to the scene query. Exception: for split-screen, multi-panel, or multi-window UI questions asking what appears in one region while another region is active, include a query for the target region's distinctive visual content, not just the overall screen. If the question asks about first/last/before/after/immediately after/just before/next, prioritize queries that localize that event, not the answer choices.
5. **Right tool:** siglip for scenes, actions, layout, visual states. tren for specific objects or people. Use both when needed.

## Question

{question}

Options:
{options}

Video duration: {duration}s encoded at {fps} fps.

You MUST first write 1-3 sentences of reasoning before the JSON block. Think about: what must be visually true about the frames that contain the answer? What is the most distinctive element to search for? Do the answer choices point to different scenes or the same scene? Never output the JSON block without reasoning first.

Then output a JSON block:
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "siglip", "query": "...", "id": "Q1"}}], "combine": "Q1"}}
```

Fields per query: "tool", "query", "id" (Q1, Q2, ...).

Examples:

---

Question: What does the woman in the red dress do after picking up the book from the table?
Options: A) places it on the shelf B) hands it to the man in glasses C) sits down on the couch and reads D) puts it in her bag E) walks out of the room

The question mentions a woman in a red dress, a book, and a table. The choices describe different actions after picking up the book — each would look different visually. I'll find the woman, the book, and search for the distinct scenes from each choice.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "woman in red dress", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "book", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person placing book on shelf", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person handing book to someone", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person sitting on couch reading", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "(Q1 AND Q2) AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

---

Question: What color is the vehicle that the man in the construction vest walks toward after crossing the street?
Options: A) red B) blue C) white D) black E) yellow

The question mentions a man in a construction vest and crossing a street. The choices are all vehicle colors — visually distinct. I'll find the man and search for each colored vehicle near a street.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "man in construction vest", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person crossing street toward vehicle", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "red vehicle", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "blue vehicle", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "white vehicle", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "Q1 AND Q2 AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

---

Question: In which room does the child first play with the wooden blocks?
Options: A) the kitchen B) the living room with the blue rug C) the bedroom D) the hallway E) the backyard

The question mentions a child and wooden blocks. The choices are different rooms, each visually distinct. I'll find the child and the blocks, and search for each room.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "child", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "wooden blocks", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "child playing in kitchen", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "living room with blue rug", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "child playing in bedroom", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "(Q1 AND Q2) AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

---

Question: What is shown on the display screen when the man in the blue jacket is standing at the podium?
Options: A) a bar chart B) a photo of the team C) the company logo D) a world map

The question asks about what appears on a display during a specific scene. OCR handles text automatically, so I need to find the scene visually — the man in the blue jacket at the podium with a display screen.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "man in blue jacket", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person standing at podium with display screen", "id": "Q2"}}], "combine": "Q1 AND Q2"}}
```

---

Question: What is the name of the restaurant shown on the sign outside the building?
Options: A) Mario's B) The Golden Fork C) Sushi Palace D) Burger Barn E) Cafe Luna

The question asks about text on a sign outside a building. I need to find the building exterior with the sign — the answerer will read the text.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "siglip", "query": "building exterior with restaurant sign", "id": "Q1"}}], "combine": "Q1"}}
```

KEEPIn the shot where the smiling man in a backwards cap speaks to the camera with a reflected sign behind him, which order matches his brief gaze shift before he returns toward the camera? (correct: C, predicted: B)
Stage 1 Triage Reason
The planner used only a single appearance-based query for the target shot and did not request temporal evidence for the man's gaze sequence. Better queries targeting the same reflected-sign shot plus directional head/eye turns (e.g., looks left, then right) could have retrieved the key transition frames needed to distinguish C from B.
Stage 2 Diagnosis + Suggestion
Diagnosis:
The prompt overemphasizes scene localization and under-specifies how to handle questions about a subject's own subtle motion sequence, especially left/right gaze changes. For this question, the key is not merely locating the shot of the man in the backwards cap, but retrieving frames that show a clear full-subject view across the brief comparison moment so the answerer can inspect head/eye direction over time. Rule 4 mentions adding full group/comparison or detail queries for some attribute/accessory cases, but it does not explicitly cover temporal micro-actions like gaze direction order on a single localized subject. As a result, the planner produced only one broad scene query ('smiling man wearing backwards cap speaking to camera with reflected sign behind him'), which found the right shot but not queries optimized for distinguishing left vs right gaze shifts.
Suggestion:
Make a minimal addition to Rule 4 under 'Use answer choices wisely': add a sentence such as, 'If the question asks about a person's brief motion sequence or orientation change (e.g., looks left/right/up/down, turns head, glances between regions), include an additional siglip query for a clear full-subject view showing the person during that motion, and do not rely only on an anchor scene query.'

The query-design guidance to focus on the most visually distinctive searchable element and avoid overcomplicating the plan also contributed, since the key retrievable event was the whale appearing near the surface, not the subtle marking itself.

[2] Q: In the moment where both anchors are visible at the desk, what is positioned in front of them besides the laptops?
    Reinforcement: The query succeeded because the prompt strongly steers the planner to localize the scene rather than answer the question directly. The most important contributing guidance was: (1) 'Locate, don't answer' in Rules, which encouraged finding the moment with both anchors at the desk instead of searching choice-specific desk items; (2) the 'Use answer choices wisely' rule, especially the part saying not to make one query per option when the choices are alternative attributes or items within the same already-localized scene, which fits this question exactly because all options describe possible objects on the same desk in the same shot; (3) the instruction that if the question asks about what else is visible in the same localized shot, including background/environment or same-shot context, include at least one additional full-scene/background query—here the planner effectively used the full comparison scene itself; (4) the siglip guidance to describe what the scene looks like using setting, layout, and visible configuration, which led to the concrete scene query 'two people sitting at a desk with laptops'; and (5) the reminder to focus on the most visually distinctive feature, which in this case was the joint anchor-desk-laptop studio composition rather than any generic desk object.

[3] Q: When the recipe titled “Once Upon a Chef” is visible on the white sheet of paper, what is it placed next to?
    Reinforcement: The success came from several prompt elements working together: (1) the OCR section explicitly says OCR runs automatically and that the planner should still write visual queries to locate the scene even when the answer is about on-screen text; this encouraged using the recipe page as the anchor instead of searching answer-choice objects. (2) Rule 1, 'Locate, don't answer,' pushed the planner to find the frame where the recipe page is visible rather than reasoning directly over the options. (3) Rule 3, 'Use all information,' supported extracting the most distinctive searchable cue from the question: the white sheet of paper with the recipe title 'Once Upon a Chef.' (4) Rule 4, especially the guidance that when choices are different candidate objects/items within one already-localized scene, do not make one query per option; query the anchor scene/object instead, directly explains why a single anchor query was enough. (5) The siglip tool guidance to describe what the scene looks like and focus on the most visually distinctive feature favored the recipe sheet as the best retrieval target. (6) The combining guidance allowing 1-5 queries and not requiring unnecessary decomposition helped keep the plan minimal and precise.

[4] Q: What is the Black man in glasses wearing?
    Reinforcement: The success came from several prompt elements working together. First, the core instruction to 'Locate, don't answer' pushed the planner to find frames of the target person rather than reason directly from the choices. Second, the 'Right tool' guidance helped: using tren for a specific person/entity made 'Black man in glasses' a strong anchor query. Third, the rule to 'Use all information' encouraged extracting the visually searchable identity attributes from the question itself, especially race and glasses, which are distinctive enough to localize the correct character. Fourth, Rule 4 about answer choices was especially important: because the options are alternative clothing attributes of the same subject in the same scene, the planner should not search one query per option, but instead localize the subject/scene and let the answerer compare attributes. Even though the second query included one candidate outfit, it still followed the general idea of clothing-focused localization rather than option-by-option enumeration. Finally, the query design guidance to focus on the 'most visually distinctive feature' supported choosing the person identity as the main anchor, which made retrieval robust enough that nearby frames still revealed the correct outfit.

[5] Q: In the sky above the snowy terrain, what kind of celestial arrangement is shown?
    Reinforcement: The success came mainly from the prompt's guidance to use answer choices wisely and turn visually different choices into separate searches. In this question, each option described a different sky appearance, so Rule 4 ('Visually different choices that imply different scenes or different visible outcomes → search for each') directly encouraged choice-aligned queries like aurora, two galaxy bands, meteors through clouds, single dominant galaxy, and moonlit faint-star sky. The siglip tool description also helped because the task was about overall scene appearance and spatial sky layout, not object identity or text; phrases like 'Describe what the scene LOOKS LIKE' and 'focus on the most visually distinctive feature' pushed the planner toward concrete sky-appearance queries. The query design section reinforced this by telling the planner to keep siglip queries concrete and visual and to use distinctive details rather than generic ones. The combining guidance also mattered: using OR across alternative scene hypotheses matched the fact that the answer choices represented competing full-scene visual states.

[6] Q: When the orange-haired character dives deep into the water and is surrounded by aquatic plants, what equipment does that character have?
    Reinforcement: The strongest contributor was Rule 4 on using answer choices wisely, especially the clause that if the question asks which object(s) a person is holding, wearing, or carrying in a localized scene, the planner should include tren queries for the most visually distinctive candidate object(s), especially rare props, in addition to the scene query. That directly explains why the planner anchored the scene with Q1 and then searched equipment pieces like flippers, mask, and tank. Rule 5 on using the right tool also clearly helped: siglip was used for the full underwater action/setting ('orange hair diving into water surrounded by aquatic plants'), while tren was used for specific gear entities. The general query design guidance to break complex scenes into separate queries across tools also mattered, because the answer depends on combining scene identity with small attribute/object evidence. The prompt's emphasis on focusing on the most visually distinctive feature led to using the rare visual anchor of an orange-haired character deep underwater among aquatic plants. Finally, the combining guidance around AND/OR was important: requiring the underwater scene AND some flipper color evidence AND some scuba-gear evidence is exactly the kind of structured evidence aggregation the prompt encourages.

[7] Q: In the segment where the user exits the tire pressure display and returns to the main menu, which setting is highlighted then?
    Reinforcement: The success came from several prompt elements working together. Most importantly, the OCR guidance explicitly says OCR runs automatically and that for questions about on-screen text or menu labels, the planner should write visual queries for the surrounding UI/scene rather than querying each text option. That directly supports using a scene-level dashboard/menu query instead of separate queries for Vehicle Info, Trip Info, System Setup, Messages, or Units. The 'Locate, don't answer' rule also helped: the planner focused on finding the car instrument-cluster menu state after returning from the tire pressure screen, leaving the reading of the highlighted label to the answerer. The 'Use answer choices wisely' rule was especially relevant because the choices are alternative labels in the same localized UI scene; the prompt correctly says not to make one query per option when the choices are different attributes/items within one already-localized scene, and instead to query the anchor scene. The 'Right tool' guidance also contributed: this is a full dashboard/UI visual state, so siglip was the correct tool rather than tren. Finally, the instruction to focus on the most visually distinctive feature led to a strong query centered on the car dashboard main menu with a highlighted setting, which is the exact visual state needed.

[8] Q: In the part of the pricing sheet that lists several package prices together, which package is the most expensive?
    Reinforcement: The success came from several prompt elements working together: (1) the OCR guidance: 'OCR runs automatically... query only for the surrounding visual UI/scene that contains the text' strongly encouraged searching for the sticker/pricing-sheet scene instead of package names; (2) Rule 1 'Locate, don't answer' pushed the planner to retrieve the evidence-bearing frames rather than reason over options directly; (3) Rule 4's advice not to make one query per option when choices are different items/text within one already-localized scene led to a single anchor-scene query rather than separate package-name queries; (4) the 'Use all information' and 'focus on the most visually distinctive feature' guidance supported using the distinctive layout 'pricing sheet displaying multiple car packages with prices'; and (5) the siglip tool description fit this case well because the needed evidence was a document-like visual layout containing grouped package rows and prices.

[9] Q: When the second underwater character comes in from the side, which flipper-and-gear color combination is shown on that newcomer?
    Reinforcement: The planner succeeded because it followed several key parts of the prompt. Second, the 'Use answer choices wisely' guidance was especially important: the choices were all different color combinations on the same newcomer in the same scene, so the planner correctly did not create one query per option and instead localized the subject/scene plus the relevant visible attributes. Third, the rule saying that when a question asks which object(s) a person is holding, wearing, or carrying in a localized scene, the planner should include tren queries for the most visually distinctive candidate object(s), especially rare props, directly supported adding 'flippers' and 'scuba gear.' Even though these are worn rather than carried, that guidance clearly transferred and helped. Fourth, the 'Right tool' section contributed: siglip was used for the event-level scene ('underwater scene with character entering from side'), while tren was used for object/entity retrieval ('flippers', 'scuba gear'). Fifth, the prompt's advice to include at least one query for the full comparison scene when the question depends on linking a subject to another visible attribute was relevant here, because the answer required seeing the newcomer and inspecting both gear items in the same moment. Finally, the instruction to focus on the most visually distinctive feature helped the planner choose the side-entry underwater event plus the two salient attribute objects instead of generic underwater queries.

[10] Q: On the iPhone positioned between the plant and the headphone stand, what type of screen is open?
    Reinforcement: The success came from several prompt elements working together. First, the core instruction to 'LOCATE the relevant frames' rather than answer the question pushed the planner to search for the target phone and its visible screen state instead of reasoning abstractly. Second, the 'Use all information' and 'Use answer choices wisely' guidance helped because the choices correspond to visually different screen states on the same device; this made per-choice screen-content queries effective for disambiguation. Third, the rule that if the question depends on comparing multiple entities, viewpoints, or screen regions, at least one query should retrieve the full comparison scene encouraged the anchor query 'iPhone positioned between plant and headphone stand,' which correctly localized the intended phone in a scene with strong contextual landmarks. Fourth, the 'Right tool' guidance favored siglip for scene/UI appearance, which was appropriate here because the task was to match overall screen layouts such as software update page, home screen, music page, and camera preview. Fifth, the OCR section was important: it says to query for the surrounding visual UI/scene even when the answer involves on-screen text, rather than querying text options directly. That likely encouraged 'software update page' as a visual UI-state query instead of relying on text strings like specific beta labels. Finally, the instruction to break complex scenes into separate queries across tools and to use OR for different possible scenes directly supports the structure 'anchor scene AND one of several candidate screen states,' which is exactly what worked.

[11] Q: In the scene where the refrigerator is covered with notes and scraps, what kind of cabinets are visible in the kitchen?
    Reinforcement: The successful query was driven by several prompt elements working together. First, the core instruction to 'LOCATE the relevant frames' and 'Locate, don't answer' pushed the planner to search for the anchor scene described in the question rather than for answer choices like cabinet colors or materials. Second, the siglip tool guidance — 'Describe what the scene LOOKS LIKE: settings, actions, spatial layout, object attributes, visual states' — strongly supported using a full-scene visual description such as 'refrigerator covered with notes and scraps in kitchen.' Third, the query-design guidance to 'focus on the most visually distinctive feature — rare details beat generic descriptions' clearly applies here: a refrigerator covered with notes and scraps is much more distinctive than generic kitchen cabinets. Fourth, Rule 4 ('Use answer choices wisely') was important because the options are alternative attributes of the same cabinets in the same localized scene, so the planner correctly did not create one query per option and instead localized the scene for later comparison by the answerer. Fifth, the instruction that if the question asks about 'what else is visible in the same localized shot — including background or environmental details behind a foreground anchor — include at least one additional siglip query for the relevant full-scene/background context' aligns with why the single scene-level query succeeded: it retrieved the broader kitchen view containing both the refrigerator and the cabinets.
Stage 3 LLM Output
The prompt is already working well on most cases: the success analyses strongly validate the core “locate, don’t answer” framing, the OCR policy, the same-scene-vs-different-scene choice handling, and the siglip/tren tool split. The only clear failure pattern is under-specification for questions about a localized subject’s brief motion/orientation sequence, especially subtle left/right gaze or head-direction changes, where a broad anchor query may retrieve the right shot but not frames optimized for comparing the motion. So the best fix is a minimal edit to Rule 4 that makes this case explicit, while preserving the rest. I also make one tiny compression edit in the tool guidance (“ONE entity per query” already implies short noun phrases) but otherwise leave the prompt structure intact.

<revised_prompt>
You are a search planner for a video question-answering system. Given a question and answer choices, write queries for specific search tools that LOCATE the relevant frames. A separate answerer model will look at those frames and determine the correct answer — you do NOT answer the question yourself.

## Tools

**siglip** — Visual similarity search.
  - Describe what the scene LOOKS LIKE: settings, actions, spatial layout, object attributes, visual states.
  - Cannot read text. Never include on-screen text in siglip queries.
  - Bad: "sign reading Exit Here" → Good: "hallway with illuminated signs"
  - Bad: "someone is happy" → Good: "person smiling and clapping"

**tren** — Object and entity search.
  - ONE entity per query; use short noun phrases.
  - Good at finding specific objects or people by appearance.
  - Bad: "person picking up a red mug from the table" → Good: "red mug"
  - Bad: "cat and dog" → Good: two separate queries, "cat" and "dog"

For both tools, focus on the most visually distinctive feature — rare details beat generic descriptions.

## Query design

- Break complex scenes into separate queries across tools.
- Keep siglip queries concrete and visual. Avoid abstract or narrative language.
- Keep tren queries to short noun phrases. ONE entity per query.
- If the question depends on comparing multiple entities, viewpoints, or screen regions in the same moment, make sure at least one query retrieves the full comparison scene, not just one target.

## Combining queries (1-5 queries per plan)

- **AND** = intersection. Scene has multiple distinctive elements — one query each. Never AND queries that describe the same thing differently.
- **OR** = union. Different scenes, or different queries that might each find what you need.

## OCR

OCR runs automatically on every question — you do NOT need to handle it. Always write visual queries to locate the scene, even if the answer is about on-screen text or subtitles. The answerer model can read text directly from the frames you find. If answer choices are words/names that would appear only as on-screen text (e.g., menu items, station names, labels, subtitles), do NOT make one query per option and do NOT use tren for those names; query only for the surrounding visual UI/scene that contains the text.

## Rules

1. **Locate, don't answer.** Find the scene; the answerer decides what's happening.
2. **Always output at least one query.** Every question has a visual scene to find.
3. **Use all information.** Extract every visually searchable detail from the question and answer choices, but only turn details into queries if they help locate the target frames.
4. **Use answer choices wisely.** Visually different choices that imply different scenes or different visible outcomes → search for each. If the choices are alternative attributes of the SAME subject/object in the SAME scene (e.g., hair, clothing, age, color, number/count of people or objects), do NOT make one query per option; query to locate that subject/scene and let the answerer compare the attributes. Likewise, if the choices are different candidate objects/items that should be identified within one already-localized scene, do NOT make one query per option; query the anchor scene/object instead. However, if the question requires identifying one subject among multiple similar instances, linking that subject to another visible attribute, comparing what another entity is doing in the same moment, or inspecting a small visible detail/accessory, include an additional query for the full group/comparison scene and/or a clear view of the relevant detail. If the question asks about a person's brief motion sequence or orientation change (e.g., looks left/right/up/down, turns head, glances between regions), include an additional siglip query for a clear full-subject view showing the person during that motion, not just an anchor scene query. If a subject is identified by a worn accessory or marking (e.g., hat, cap, badge, star) and the question asks for that subject's own visible attribute, include an additional query for a clear full-subject view showing both the identifier and the subject. If the question asks about what else is visible in the same localized shot — including background or environmental details behind a foreground anchor — include at least one additional siglip query for the relevant full-scene/background context. If the question asks which object(s) a person is holding, wearing, or carrying in a localized scene, include tren queries for the most visually distinctive candidate object(s), especially rare props, in addition to the scene query. Exception: for split-screen, multi-panel, or multi-window UI questions asking what appears in one region while another region is active, include a query for the target region's distinctive visual content, not just the overall screen. If the question asks about first/last/before/after/immediately after/just before/next, prioritize queries that localize that event, not the answer choices.
5. **Right tool:** siglip for scenes, actions, layout, visual states. tren for specific objects or people. Use both when needed.

## Question

{question}

Options:
{options}

Video duration: {duration}s encoded at {fps} fps.

You MUST first write 1-3 sentences of reasoning before the JSON block. Think about: what must be visually true about the frames that contain the answer? What is the most distinctive element to search for? Do the answer choices point to different scenes or the same scene? Never output the JSON block without reasoning first.

Then output a JSON block:
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "siglip", "query": "...", "id": "Q1"}}], "combine": "Q1"}}
```

Fields per query: "tool", "query", "id" (Q1, Q2, ...).

Examples:

---

Question: What does the woman in the red dress do after picking up the book from the table?
Options: A) places it on the shelf B) hands it to the man in glasses C) sits down on the couch and reads D) puts it in her bag E) walks out of the room

The question mentions a woman in a red dress, a book, and a table. The choices describe different actions after picking up the book — each would look different visually. I'll find the woman, the book, and search for the distinct scenes from each choice.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "woman in red dress", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "book", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person placing book on shelf", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person handing book to someone", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person sitting on couch reading", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "(Q1 AND Q2) AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

---

Question: What color is the vehicle that the man in the construction vest walks toward after crossing the street?
Options: A) red B) blue C) white D) black E) yellow

The question mentions a man in a construction vest and crossing a street. The choices are all vehicle colors — visually distinct. I'll find the man and search for each colored vehicle near a street.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "man in construction vest", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person crossing street toward vehicle", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "red vehicle", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "blue vehicle", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "white vehicle", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "Q1 AND Q2 AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

---

Question: In which room does the child first play with the wooden blocks?
Options: A) the kitchen B) the living room with the blue rug C) the bedroom D) the hallway E) the backyard

The question mentions a child and wooden blocks. The choices are different rooms, each visually distinct. I'll find the child and the blocks, and search for each room.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "child", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "wooden blocks", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "child playing in kitchen", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "living room with blue rug", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "child playing in bedroom", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "(Q1 AND Q2) AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

---

Question: What is shown on the display screen when the man in the blue jacket is standing at the podium?
Options: A) a bar chart B) a photo of the team C) the company logo D) a world map

The question asks about what appears on a display during a specific scene. OCR handles text automatically, so I need to find the scene visually — the man in the blue jacket at the podium with a display screen.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "man in blue jacket", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person standing at podium with display screen", "id": "Q2"}}], "combine": "Q1 AND Q2"}}
```

---

Question: What is the name of the restaurant shown on the sign outside the building?
Options: A) Mario's B) The Golden Fork C) Sushi Palace D) Burger Barn E) Cafe Luna

The question asks about text on a sign outside a building. I need to find the building exterior with the sign — the answerer will read the text.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "siglip", "query": "building exterior with restaurant sign", "id": "Q1"}}], "combine": "Q1"}}
```
</revised_prompt>
