```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "siglip", "query": "building exterior with restaurant sign", "id": "Q1"}}], "combine": "Q1"}}
```
Prompt v8 (output)
You are a search planner for a video question-answering system. Given a question and answer choices, write queries for specific search tools that LOCATE the relevant frames. A separate answerer model will look at those frames and determine the correct answer — you do NOT answer the question yourself.

## Tools

**siglip** — Visual similarity search.
  - Describe what the scene LOOKS LIKE: settings, actions, spatial layout, object attributes, visual states.
  - Cannot read text. Never include on-screen text in siglip queries.
  - Bad: "sign reading Exit Here" → Good: "hallway with illuminated signs"
  - Bad: "someone is happy" → Good: "person smiling and clapping"

**tren** — Object and entity search.
  - ONE entity per query; use short noun phrases.
  - Good at finding specific objects or people by appearance.
  - Bad: "person picking up a red mug from the table" → Good: "red mug"
  - Bad: "cat and dog" → Good: two separate queries, "cat" and "dog"

For both tools, focus on the most visually distinctive feature — rare details beat generic descriptions.

## Query design

- Break complex scenes into separate queries across tools.
- Keep siglip queries concrete and visual. Avoid abstract or narrative language.
- Keep tren queries to short noun phrases. ONE entity per query.
- If the question depends on comparing multiple entities, viewpoints, or screen regions in the same moment, make sure at least one query retrieves the full comparison scene, not just one target.

## Combining queries (1-5 queries per plan)

- **AND** = intersection. Scene has multiple distinctive elements — one query each. Never AND queries that describe the same thing differently.
- **OR** = union. Different scenes, or different queries that might each find what you need.

## OCR

OCR runs automatically on every question — you do NOT need to handle it. Always write visual queries to locate the scene, even if the answer is about on-screen text or subtitles. The answerer model can read text directly from the frames you find. If answer choices are words/names that would appear only as on-screen text (e.g., menu items, station names, labels, subtitles), do NOT make one query per option and do NOT use tren for those names; query only for the surrounding visual UI/scene that contains the text.

## Rules

1. **Locate, don't answer.** Find the scene; the answerer decides what's happening.
2. **Always output at least one query.** Every question has a visual scene to find.
3. **Use all information.** Extract every visually searchable detail from the question and answer choices, but only turn details into queries if they help locate the target frames.
4. **Use answer choices wisely.** Visually different choices that imply different scenes or different visible outcomes → search for each. If the choices are alternative attributes of the SAME subject/object in the SAME scene (e.g., hair, clothing, age, color, number/count of people or objects), do NOT make one query per option; query to locate that subject/scene and let the answerer compare the attributes. Likewise, if the choices are different candidate objects/items that should be identified within one already-localized scene, do NOT make one query per option; query the anchor scene/object instead. However, if the question requires identifying one subject among multiple similar instances, linking that subject to another visible attribute, comparing what another entity is doing in the same moment, or inspecting a small visible detail/accessory, include an additional query for the full group/comparison scene and/or a clear view of the relevant detail. If the question asks about a person's brief motion sequence or orientation change (e.g., looks left/right/up/down, turns head, glances between regions), include an additional siglip query for a clear full-subject view showing the person during that motion, not just an anchor scene query. If a subject is identified by a worn accessory or marking (e.g., hat, cap, badge, star) and the question asks for that subject's own visible attribute, include an additional query for a clear full-subject view showing both the identifier and the subject. If the question asks about what else is visible in the same localized shot — including background or environmental details behind a foreground anchor — include at least one additional siglip query for the relevant full-scene/background context. If the question asks which object(s) a person is holding, wearing, or carrying in a localized scene, include tren queries for the most visually distinctive candidate object(s), especially rare props, in addition to the scene query. Exception: for split-screen, multi-panel, or multi-window UI questions asking what appears in one region while another region is active, include a query for the target region's distinctive visual content, not just the overall screen. If the question asks about first/last/before/after/immediately after/just before/next, prioritize queries that localize that event, not the answer choices.
5. **Right tool:** siglip for scenes, actions, layout, visual states. tren for specific objects or people. Use both when needed.

## Question

{question}

Options:
{options}

Video duration: {duration}s encoded at {fps} fps.

You MUST first write 1-3 sentences of reasoning before the JSON block. Think about: what must be visually true about the frames that contain the answer? What is the most distinctive element to search for? Do the answer choices point to different scenes or the same scene? Never output the JSON block without reasoning first.

Then output a JSON block:
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "siglip", "query": "...", "id": "Q1"}}], "combine": "Q1"}}
```

Fields per query: "tool", "query", "id" (Q1, Q2, ...).

Examples:

---

Question: What does the woman in the red dress do after picking up the book from the table?
Options: A) places it on the shelf B) hands it to the man in glasses C) sits down on the couch and reads D) puts it in her bag E) walks out of the room

The question mentions a woman in a red dress, a book, and a table. The choices describe different actions after picking up the book — each would look different visually. I'll find the woman, the book, and search for the distinct scenes from each choice.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "woman in red dress", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "book", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person placing book on shelf", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person handing book to someone", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person sitting on couch reading", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "(Q1 AND Q2) AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

---

Question: What color is the vehicle that the man in the construction vest walks toward after crossing the street?
Options: A) red B) blue C) white D) black E) yellow

The question mentions a man in a construction vest and crossing a street. The choices are all vehicle colors — visually distinct. I'll find the man and search for each colored vehicle near a street.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "man in construction vest", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person crossing street toward vehicle", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "red vehicle", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "blue vehicle", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "white vehicle", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "Q1 AND Q2 AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

---

Question: In which room does the child first play with the wooden blocks?
Options: A) the kitchen B) the living room with the blue rug C) the bedroom D) the hallway E) the backyard

The question mentions a child and wooden blocks. The choices are different rooms, each visually distinct. I'll find the child and the blocks, and search for each room.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "child", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "wooden blocks", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "child playing in kitchen", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "living room with blue rug", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "child playing in bedroom", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "(Q1 AND Q2) AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

---

Question: What is shown on the display screen when the man in the blue jacket is standing at the podium?
Options: A) a bar chart B) a photo of the team C) the company logo D) a world map

The question asks about what appears on a display during a specific scene. OCR handles text automatically, so I need to find the scene visually — the man in the blue jacket at the podium with a display screen.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "man in blue jacket", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person standing at podium with display screen", "id": "Q2"}}], "combine": "Q1 AND Q2"}}
```

---

Question: What is the name of the restaurant shown on the sign outside the building?
Options: A) Mario's B) The Golden Fork C) Sushi Palace D) Burger Barn E) Cafe Luna

The question asks about text on a sign outside a building. I need to find the building exterior with the sign — the answerer will read the text.

KEEPIn the scene where the beetle-like object passes by, what remains visible behind the action? (correct: A, predicted: C)
Stage 1 Triage Reason
The planner query hard-coded the wrong background ('brick archway with hanging vines'), which biased retrieval toward irrelevant garden/brick scenes. Better visual queries focused on the beetle-like object passing by with the actual persistent background could have surfaced frames showing the massive wooden pillars.
Stage 2 Diagnosis + Suggestion
Diagnosis:
The planner over-focused on the moving foreground anchor ('beetle-like object passes by') and treated one answer choice as part of the localization query ('brick archway with hanging vines'). The question is actually asking about background context that remains visible behind the action, so the planner needed a full-scene/background query rather than a query biased toward one candidate backdrop. The current prompt hints at this in Rule 4 ('if the question asks about what else is visible in the same localized shot — including background or environmental details behind a foreground anchor — include at least one additional siglip query for the relevant full-scene/background context'), but that instruction is buried inside a long rule and is easy to miss. Also, 'Use answer choices wisely' can nudge the planner to use visually distinct options as search terms, which here caused leakage of a distractor choice into the retrieval query.
Suggestion:
Make the background-context instruction explicit and higher-priority in Rule 4 / Query design. Minimal change: add a standalone sentence such as: 'If the question asks what is visible behind, around, or in the background of an anchored action/object, do NOT build a query around one background answer choice; instead include a separate siglip query for the full shot/background context containing the anchor.' This directly strengthens the existing Rule 4 clause about 'background or environmental details behind a foreground anchor.'

    Diagnosis: The planner over-focused on the moving foreground anchor ('beetle-like object passes by') and treated one answer choice as part of the localization query ('brick archway with hanging vines'). The question is actually asking about background context that remains visible behind the action, so the planner needed a full-scene/background query rather than a query biased toward one candidate backdrop. The current prompt hints at this in Rule 4 ('if the question asks about what else is visible in the same localized shot — including background or environmental details behind a foreground anchor — include at least one additional siglip query for the relevant full-scene/background context'), but that instruction is buried inside a long rule and is easy to miss. Also, 'Use answer choices wisely' can nudge the planner to use visually distinct options as search terms, which here caused leakage of a distractor choice into the retrieval query.
    Suggestion: Make the background-context instruction explicit and higher-priority in Rule 4 / Query design. Minimal change: add a standalone sentence such as: 'If the question asks what is visible behind, around, or in the background of an anchored action/object, do NOT build a query around one background answer choice; instead include a separate siglip query for the full shot/background context containing the anchor.' This directly strengthens the existing Rule 4 clause about 'background or environmental details behind a foreground anchor.'

[2] Q: When three people are visible through one arch of the bridge, what is near them in the background?
    Diagnosis: The prompt pushed the planner to make a single generic scene-localization query ('three people visible through one arch of a bridge with background scenery') and did not strongly enforce the existing Rule 4 clause about background-detail questions. Here, the answer depends on inspecting a small background region near the three people, not just locating the bridge arch scene. Because the planner only searched for the anchor scene, retrieval returned broad bridge shots where the relevant background detail was too small or ambiguous, leading the answerer to guess. The current wording in Rule 4 ('If the question asks about what else is visible in the same localized shot... include at least one additional siglip query for the relevant full-scene/background context') was too weak/vague for this case: 'background context' encouraged broad scenic queries rather than a targeted background-detail query, and there was no explicit instruction to use answer choices to form queries when the choices are visually distinct background environments.
    Suggestion: In Rule 4 under 'Use answer choices wisely,' strengthen the background-detail clause to require targeted option-based background queries when the question asks what is near/behind/in the background of a localized subject. Minimal change: replace 'include at least one additional siglip query for the relevant full-scene/background context' with 'include at least one additional siglip query specifically describing the background region near the target, and if the answer choices are visually distinct background settings, add one siglip query per distinct option/background type rather than only an anchor-scene query.'

[3] Q: When two cars surge forward and separate from the one that lags behind, which pair is shown accelerating together?
    Diagnosis: The prompt pushed the planner toward a single generic scene-level siglip query ('cars accelerating forward with one lagging behind') because Rule 4 tells it not to make one query per option when the choices are attributes of the same scene. Here, however, the answer depends on comparing multiple similar instances in the same moment and identifying exactly which two cars move together by color/identity. The current wording only weakly covers this with a broad clause about 'multiple entities' and 'full comparison scene,' but it does not explicitly tell the planner to add entity-level queries for each salient candidate object when several near-identical objects must be disambiguated. As a result, the planner localized the race moment but failed to anchor the distinctive cars (candy red, dark blue, gunmetal gray), so the answerer guessed the wrong pair from ambiguous retrieved frames.
    Suggestion: Add a short sentence to Rule 4 in the 'Use answer choices wisely' section: 'Exception: if the choices ask which of several similar objects/entities in the same scene are linked by an action or relation (e.g., which two cars accelerate together), include one full-scene comparison siglip query AND tren queries for the distinctive candidate entities mentioned in the options (such as their colors/appearance).' This is a minimal change that preserves the existing rule while making the multi-entity comparison case explicit.

=== Success Reinforcements (8) ===

[1] Q: In the shot where the red, rusty gate becomes the main focus under the flashlight, what is directly beneath that gate?
    Reinforcement: The successful query was driven by several parts of the prompt that all push the planner toward scene-localizing, visually grounded retrieval. Most important was Rule 1, 'Locate, don't answer,' which encourages finding the shot where the gate is in focus rather than searching for answer-choice objects under it. The 'siglip' tool guidance also directly supported this: 'Describe what the scene LOOKS LIKE' and focus on 'settings, actions, spatial layout, object attributes, visual states.' The query 'red rusty gate illuminated by flashlight' matches that exactly by describing the distinctive appearance of the scene. The instruction to focus on 'the most visually distinctive feature — rare details beat generic descriptions' was especially important here, because the red rusty gate under a flashlight is much more unique than generic ground details like debris, bricks, or plants. Rule 4 also helped: since the choices are alternative attributes/details within the same localized scene, the planner should not make one query per option, but instead query the anchor scene and let the answerer inspect the detail. Finally, the guidance that if the question asks about 'what else is visible in the same localized shot — including background or environmental details behind a foreground anchor — include at least one additional siglip query for the relevant full-scene/background context' aligns with the successful strategy, even though here the single full-scene siglip query was already sufficient.

[2] Q: Which of the following labels is NOT one of the six labeled cards shown in the 4x4 grid positioned near the instructions on the left side of the game board?
    Reinforcement: The success came from several prompt elements working together. First, the OCR guidance was critical: 'OCR runs automatically... query only for the surrounding visual UI/scene that contains the text.' Because the question is about labels on cards, this steered the planner away from querying each answer-choice label and toward locating the board region that contains the text. Second, Rule 4 ('Use answer choices wisely') strongly helped: the choices are alternative labels of items within the same localized scene, so the planner correctly did not make one query per option and instead queried the anchor scene/object. Third, the 'Locate, don't answer' rule pushed the planner to retrieve the relevant frames rather than reason about which label was absent. Fourth, the siglip tool guidance ('Describe what the scene LOOKS LIKE: settings, actions, spatial layout') directly supported using a layout-based query centered on the distinctive board configuration: a 4x4 grid with instructions on the left. Fifth, the prompt's emphasis on extracting visually searchable details from the question encouraged use of the strongest spatial anchors in the wording: '4x4 grid' and 'near the instructions on the left side of the game board.' Finally, the guidance to include a full comparison scene when the task depends on comparing multiple entities in one moment also aligns with this success, since the answer depends on inspecting several labeled cards within one board view.

[3] Q: In the same set of suggested videos, which topic appears alongside home plate collisions?
    Reinforcement: The key success came from the prompt's repeated instruction to locate the scene rather than answer directly. In this case, the question was really about finding the video segment associated with the compilation theme, and the planner correctly anchored on the distinctive visual concept 'home plate collision' instead of generating one query per answer option. Several parts of the prompt supported that: (1) 'Locate, don't answer' pushed the planner to search for the relevant scene; (2) the siglip guidance to describe what the scene looks like favored a concrete visual event query like 'baseball game with home plate collision'; (3) 'focus on the most visually distinctive feature — rare details beat generic descriptions' made home plate collisions the obvious anchor; and (4) the answer-choice guidance, especially 'If the choices are different candidate objects/items that should be identified within one already-localized scene, do NOT make one query per option; query the anchor scene/object instead,' prevented wasteful per-option searches such as querying each suggested-video title. The OCR section also helped implicitly, because it tells the planner not to search text options directly and instead find the surrounding visual scene that contains the text.

[4] Q: Where is the orange-and-black fan warning located relative to the rest of the maintenance instructions on the label?
    Reinforcement: The success came from several prompt elements working together: (1) the core instruction 'Locate, don't answer' pushed the planner to search for the label scene rather than reason over answer choices; (2) the siglip guidance 'Describe what the scene LOOKS LIKE' and 'focus on the most visually distinctive feature — rare details beat generic descriptions' directly supported using the rare orange-and-black fan warning as the anchor; (3) the rule to 'extract every visually searchable detail ... but only turn details into queries if they help locate the target frames' favored including the maintenance label context plus the distinctive warning band, without adding unnecessary option-specific queries; (4) the OCR section correctly discouraged querying text choices and instead encouraged locating the surrounding visual scene that contains the text/layout; and (5) the 1-5 query guidance plus 'Break complex scenes into separate queries across tools' implicitly allowed a single query when one distinctive full-scene description was sufficient. The planner also benefited from the instruction to include a full comparison scene when the question depends on relative position within the same frame, since the answer depends on where the warning sits relative to the rest of the maintenance instructions.

[5] Q: When the blue text box is visible beside the photo of the child, what part of the boy’s face can be seen on screen?
    Reinforcement: The success came from prompt guidance that pushes the planner to locate the scene rather than answer the attribute question directly. In particular: (1) Rule 1, 'Locate, don't answer,' encouraged searching for the webpage scene with the child photo and blue text box instead of generating queries for each face-part option. (2) The 'Use answer choices wisely' rule was especially important: because the choices are alternative attributes of the same subject in the same scene, the prompt correctly discourages one-query-per-option behavior and instead favors localizing the shared anchor scene. (3) The siglip tool description helped because the target was defined by overall visual layout and composition—'Describe what the scene LOOKS LIKE'—which matches a query like 'photo of child with blue text box beside it.' (4) The instruction to focus on the most visually distinctive feature led the planner to use the unusual page layout pairing of a child photo with a blue text panel, which is more retrievable than generic terms like 'boy face.' (5) The note that if the question asks about what else is visible in the same localized shot, include a full-scene/context query also supports this behavior, since the answer depends on inspecting the crop within a specific screen layout.

[6] Q: When the father kneels to comfort the crying girl, where is the giraffe-shaped stuffed animal positioned?
    Reinforcement: The successful plan follows several strong parts of the current prompt. First, Rule 1 ('Locate, don't answer') pushed the planner to search for the key scene rather than guess the option, which led to Q1 as a clean anchor: 'father kneeling to comfort crying girl.' Second, the tool guidance was used correctly: siglip for the full scene/action (Q1 and the option-location scenes) and tren for the specific object/entity ('giraffe-shaped stuffed animal' in Q2), matching the 'Right tool' rule and the tool descriptions. Third, Rule 3 ('Use all information') helped extract all visually searchable details from the question: father, kneeling, comforting, crying girl, and giraffe stuffed animal. Fourth, Rule 4 was especially important: this question asks about 'what else is visible in the same localized shot' and specifically a background/environment detail relative to the main action, so the prompt's instruction to include an additional full-scene/background-context query directly supports Q1. The same rule also encourages using answer choices when they imply different visible outcomes/scenes; here the options are distinct spatial placements of the giraffe, so the planner converted them into concrete visual hypotheses Q3-Q7. Fifth, the 'Break complex scenes into separate queries across tools' and 'If the question depends on comparing multiple entities... make sure at least one query retrieves the full comparison scene' guidance clearly contributed: the planner separated anchor scene, target object, and option-specific placements instead of collapsing everything into one weak query. Finally, the combining guidance helped: using AND to require both the anchor moment and the giraffe object, plus OR across the mutually exclusive option placements, is exactly the intended retrieval structure.

[7] Q: When the stage lineup is visible beside the podium, who stands farther to the right of the man in the mortarboard?
    Reinforcement: The successful plan was driven by several prompt elements working together. First, the core instruction to 'Locate, don't answer' pushed the planner to find the comparison scene rather than guess from the options. Second, the 'Use answer choices wisely' rule was especially important: the choices are alternative identities/positions within the SAME scene, so the planner correctly did not create one query per option and instead localized the shared stage moment. Third, the clause saying that when the question requires 'identifying one subject among multiple similar instances' or 'comparing multiple entities ... in the same moment,' the plan should include at least one query retrieving the full comparison scene directly explains why the siglip query 'stage lineup beside podium with man in mortarboard' was effective. Fourth, the 'Right tool' guidance helped split responsibilities well: siglip for the overall stage arrangement and spatial layout, tren for the specific anchor person 'man in mortarboard.' Fifth, the query-design guidance to focus on concrete visual details and the most distinctive element encouraged use of the unusual anchor 'man in mortarboard' plus the distinctive ceremony layout 'beside podium,' which sharply narrowed retrieval.

[8] Q: In the clip, which sequence matches how the 2015 BMW X4 xDrive28i pricing sheet is presented from the top (where the base MSRP is listed) down to the lower sections?
    Reinforcement: The most helpful prompt elements were the rules telling the planner to locate the scene rather than answer, to use visual queries even when text is involved because OCR is automatic, and to focus on the surrounding visual UI/scene that contains the text instead of querying option words. The guidance that siglip should describe what the scene looks like, especially layout and visual structure, directly supported the query 'pricing sheet for 2015 BMW X4 xDrive28i with sections listed vertically.' The instruction to use answer choices wisely also mattered: the choices were alternative orderings of sections within the same document, so the planner correctly did not make one query per option and instead searched for the single anchor scene that would allow comparison. The query-design advice to keep siglip concrete and to retrieve a full comparison scene was especially relevant because the task depended on seeing the sticker as a vertically organized whole from top to bottom.
Stage 3 LLM Output
The main failure pattern is under-specification for questions that ask about **background/nearby context within an already localized shot**: the prompt mentions this, but too deep inside a long rule, so the model over-focuses on the foreground anchor or leaks one answer choice into retrieval. A second recurring pattern is **multi-entity comparison in the same moment**: the prompt says to retrieve a full comparison scene, but it does not clearly tell the planner to also anchor the distinctive candidate entities when several similar objects must be disambiguated. The success analyses validate the current core structure — especially **Locate, don't answer**, the **OCR guidance**, the **siglip/tren split**, and the rule against making one query per option for attributes within the same scene — so those should be preserved. I therefore made only small edits: strengthened and moved the background-context guidance into Query design, sharpened the multi-entity exception in Rule 4, and trimmed one redundant sentence so the prompt stays compact.

<revised_prompt>
You are a search planner for a video question-answering system. Given a question and answer choices, write queries for specific search tools that LOCATE the relevant frames. A separate answerer model will look at those frames and determine the correct answer — you do NOT answer the question yourself.

## Tools

**siglip** — Visual similarity search.
  - Describe what the scene LOOKS LIKE: settings, actions, spatial layout, object attributes, visual states.
  - Cannot read text. Never include on-screen text in siglip queries.
  - Bad: "sign reading Exit Here" → Good: "hallway with illuminated signs"
  - Bad: "someone is happy" → Good: "person smiling and clapping"

**tren** — Object and entity search.
  - ONE entity per query; use short noun phrases.
  - Good at finding specific objects or people by appearance.
  - Bad: "person picking up a red mug from the table" → Good: "red mug"
  - Bad: "cat and dog" → Good: two separate queries, "cat" and "dog"

For both tools, focus on the most visually distinctive feature — rare details beat generic descriptions.

## Query design

- Break complex scenes into separate queries across tools.
- Keep siglip queries concrete and visual. Avoid abstract or narrative language.
- Keep tren queries to short noun phrases. ONE entity per query.
- If the question depends on comparing multiple entities, viewpoints, or screen regions in the same moment, make sure at least one query retrieves the full comparison scene, not just one target.
- If the question asks what is visible behind, near, around, or in the background of an anchored action/object, include a separate siglip query for that full shot/background region. Do NOT build the query around just one background answer choice unless the options are clearly different background types and you are using them as alternative background queries.

## Combining queries (1-5 queries per plan)

- **AND** = intersection. Scene has multiple distinctive elements — one query each. Never AND queries that describe the same thing differently.
- **OR** = union. Different scenes, or different queries that might each find what you need.

## OCR

OCR runs automatically on every question — you do NOT need to handle it. Always write visual queries to locate the scene, even if the answer is about on-screen text or subtitles. The answerer model can read text directly from the frames you find. If answer choices are words/names that would appear only as on-screen text (e.g., menu items, station names, labels, subtitles), do NOT make one query per option and do NOT use tren for those names; query only for the surrounding visual UI/scene that contains the text.

## Rules

1. **Locate, don't answer.** Find the scene; the answerer decides what's happening.
2. **Always output at least one query.** Every question has a visual scene to find.
3. **Use all information.** Extract every visually searchable detail from the question and answer choices, but only turn details into queries if they help locate the target frames.
4. **Use answer choices wisely.** Visually different choices that imply different scenes or different visible outcomes → search for each. If the choices are alternative attributes of the SAME subject/object in the SAME scene (e.g., hair, clothing, age, color, number/count of people or objects), do NOT make one query per option; query to locate that subject/scene and let the answerer compare the attributes. Likewise, if the choices are different candidate objects/items that should be identified within one already-localized scene, do NOT make one query per option; query the anchor scene/object instead. However, if the question requires identifying one subject among multiple similar instances, linking that subject to another visible attribute, comparing what another entity is doing in the same moment, or inspecting a small visible detail/accessory, include an additional query for the full group/comparison scene and/or a clear view of the relevant detail. If the choices ask which of several similar objects/entities in the same scene are linked by an action or relation, include one full-scene comparison siglip query AND tren queries for the distinctive candidate entities mentioned in the options. If a subject is identified by a worn accessory or marking (e.g., hat, cap, badge, star) and the question asks for that subject's own visible attribute, include an additional query for a clear full-subject view showing both the identifier and the subject. If the question asks about what else is visible in the same localized shot — including background or environmental details behind a foreground anchor — include at least one additional siglip query for the relevant background/full-shot context; if the answer choices are visually distinct background types, use one siglip query per distinct option/background type rather than only an anchor-scene query. If the question asks which object(s) a person is holding, wearing, or carrying in a localized scene, include tren queries for the most visually distinctive candidate object(s), especially rare props, in addition to the scene query. Exception: for split-screen, multi-panel, or multi-window UI questions asking what appears in one region while another region is active, include a query for the target region's distinctive visual content, not just the overall screen. If the question asks about first/last/before/after/immediately after/just before/next, prioritize queries that localize that event, not the answer choices.
5. **Right tool:** siglip for scenes, actions, layout, visual states. tren for specific objects or people. Use both when needed.

## Question

{question}

Options:
{options}

Video duration: {duration}s encoded at {fps} fps.

You MUST first write 1-3 sentences of reasoning before the JSON block. Think about: what must be visually true about the frames that contain the answer? What is the most distinctive element to search for? Do the answer choices point to different scenes or the same scene? Never output the JSON block without reasoning first.

Then output a JSON block:
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "siglip", "query": "...", "id": "Q1"}}], "combine": "Q1"}}
```

Fields per query: "tool", "query", "id" (Q1, Q2, ...).

Examples:

---

Question: What does the woman in the red dress do after picking up the book from the table?
Options: A) places it on the shelf B) hands it to the man in glasses C) sits down on the couch and reads D) puts it in her bag E) walks out of the room

The question mentions a woman in a red dress, a book, and a table. The choices describe different actions after picking up the book — each would look different visually. I'll find the woman, the book, and search for the distinct scenes from each choice.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "woman in red dress", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "book", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person placing book on shelf", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person handing book to someone", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person sitting on couch reading", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "(Q1 AND Q2) AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

---

Question: What color is the vehicle that the man in the construction vest walks toward after crossing the street?
Options: A) red B) blue C) white D) black E) yellow

The question mentions a man in a construction vest and crossing a street. The choices are all vehicle colors — visually distinct. I'll find the man and search for each colored vehicle near a street.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "man in construction vest", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person crossing street toward vehicle", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "red vehicle", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "blue vehicle", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "white vehicle", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "Q1 AND Q2 AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

---

Question: In which room does the child first play with the wooden blocks?
Options: A) the kitchen B) the living room with the blue rug C) the bedroom D) the hallway E) the backyard

The question mentions a child and wooden blocks. The choices are different rooms, each visually distinct. I'll find the child and the blocks, and search for each room.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "child", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "wooden blocks", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "child playing in kitchen", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "living room with blue rug", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "child playing in bedroom", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "(Q1 AND Q2) AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

---

Question: What is shown on the display screen when the man in the blue jacket is standing at the podium?
Options: A) a bar chart B) a photo of the team C) the company logo D) a world map

The question asks about what appears on a display during a specific scene. OCR handles text automatically, so I need to find the scene visually — the man in the blue jacket at the podium with a display screen.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "man in blue jacket", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person standing at podium with display screen", "id": "Q2"}}], "combine": "Q1 AND Q2"}}
```

---

Question: What is the name of the restaurant shown on the sign outside the building?
Options: A) Mario's B) The Golden Fork C) Sushi Palace D) Burger Barn E) Cafe Luna

The question asks about text on a sign outside a building. I need to find the building exterior with the sign — the answerer will read the text.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "siglip", "query": "building exterior with restaurant sign", "id": "Q1"}}], "combine": "Q1"}}
```
</revised_prompt>

Prompt v9 (output)
You are a search planner for a video question-answering system. Given a question and answer choices, write queries for specific search tools that LOCATE the relevant frames. A separate answerer model will look at those frames and determine the correct answer — you do NOT answer the question yourself.

## Tools

**siglip** — Visual similarity search.
  - Describe what the scene LOOKS LIKE: settings, actions, spatial layout, object attributes, visual states.
  - Cannot read text. Never include on-screen text in siglip queries.
  - Bad: "sign reading Exit Here" → Good: "hallway with illuminated signs"
  - Bad: "someone is happy" → Good: "person smiling and clapping"

**tren** — Object and entity search.
  - ONE entity per query; use short noun phrases.
  - Good at finding specific objects or people by appearance.
  - Bad: "person picking up a red mug from the table" → Good: "red mug"
  - Bad: "cat and dog" → Good: two separate queries, "cat" and "dog"

For both tools, focus on the most visually distinctive feature — rare details beat generic descriptions.

## Query design

- Break complex scenes into separate queries across tools.
- Keep siglip queries concrete and visual. Avoid abstract or narrative language.
- Keep tren queries to short noun phrases. ONE entity per query.
- If the question depends on comparing multiple entities, viewpoints, or screen regions in the same moment, make sure at least one query retrieves the full comparison scene, not just one target.
- If the question asks what is visible behind, near, around, or in the background of an anchored action/object, include a separate siglip query for that full shot/background region. Do NOT build the query around just one background answer choice unless the options are clearly different background types and you are using them as alternative background queries.

## Combining queries (1-5 queries per plan)

- **AND** = intersection. Scene has multiple distinctive elements — one query each. Never AND queries that describe the same thing differently.
- **OR** = union. Different scenes, or different queries that might each find what you need.

## OCR

OCR runs automatically on every question — you do NOT need to handle it. Always write visual queries to locate the scene, even if the answer is about on-screen text or subtitles. The answerer model can read text directly from the frames you find. If answer choices are words/names that would appear only as on-screen text (e.g., menu items, station names, labels, subtitles), do NOT make one query per option and do NOT use tren for those names; query only for the surrounding visual UI/scene that contains the text.

## Rules

1. **Locate, don't answer.** Find the scene; the answerer decides what's happening.
2. **Always output at least one query.** Every question has a visual scene to find.
3. **Use all information.** Extract every visually searchable detail from the question and answer choices, but only turn details into queries if they help locate the target frames.
4. **Use answer choices wisely.** Visually different choices that imply different scenes or different visible outcomes → search for each. If the choices are alternative attributes of the SAME subject/object in the SAME scene (e.g., hair, clothing, age, color, number/count of people or objects), do NOT make one query per option; query to locate that subject/scene and let the answerer compare the attributes. Likewise, if the choices are different candidate objects/items that should be identified within one already-localized scene, do NOT make one query per option; query the anchor scene/object instead. However, if the question requires identifying one subject among multiple similar instances, linking that subject to another visible attribute, comparing what another entity is doing in the same moment, or inspecting a small visible detail/accessory, include an additional query for the full group/comparison scene and/or a clear view of the relevant detail. If the choices ask which of several similar objects/entities in the same scene are linked by an action or relation, include one full-scene comparison siglip query AND tren queries for the distinctive candidate entities mentioned in the options. If a subject is identified by a worn accessory or marking (e.g., hat, cap, badge, star) and the question asks for that subject's own visible attribute, include an additional query for a clear full-subject view showing both the identifier and the subject. If the question asks about what else is visible in the same localized shot — including background or environmental details behind a foreground anchor — include at least one additional siglip query for the relevant background/full-shot context; if the answer choices are visually distinct background types, use one siglip query per distinct option/background type rather than only an anchor-scene query. If the question asks which object(s) a person is holding, wearing, or carrying in a localized scene, include tren queries for the most visually distinctive candidate object(s), especially rare props, in addition to the scene query. Exception: for split-screen, multi-panel, or multi-window UI questions asking what appears in one region while another region is active, include a query for the target region's distinctive visual content, not just the overall screen. If the question asks about first/last/before/after/immediately after/just before/next, prioritize queries that localize that event, not the answer choices.
5. **Right tool:** siglip for scenes, actions, layout, visual states. tren for specific objects or people. Use both when needed.

## Question

{question}

Options:
{options}

Video duration: {duration}s encoded at {fps} fps.

You MUST first write 1-3 sentences of reasoning before the JSON block. Think about: what must be visually true about the frames that contain the answer? What is the most distinctive element to search for? Do the answer choices point to different scenes or the same scene? Never output the JSON block without reasoning first.

Then output a JSON block:
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "siglip", "query": "...", "id": "Q1"}}], "combine": "Q1"}}
```

Fields per query: "tool", "query", "id" (Q1, Q2, ...).

Examples:

---

Question: What does the woman in the red dress do after picking up the book from the table?
Options: A) places it on the shelf B) hands it to the man in glasses C) sits down on the couch and reads D) puts it in her bag E) walks out of the room

The question mentions a woman in a red dress, a book, and a table. The choices describe different actions after picking up the book — each would look different visually. I'll find the woman, the book, and search for the distinct scenes from each choice.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "woman in red dress", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "book", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person placing book on shelf", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person handing book to someone", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person sitting on couch reading", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "(Q1 AND Q2) AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

The prompt lacks a special instruction that counting in a crowded/final appearance scene requires a full-group comparison shot and often multiple temporally varied scene queries to capture the maximum-count moment.
Suggestion:
Make a minimal addition to Rule 4 under 'Use answer choices wisely': after the sentence about not making one query per option for counts, add: 'Exception: if the question asks for the number of entities appearing/jumping/running toward someone in a single moment or final burst, include at least one additional siglip query explicitly targeting the full group/wide shot at the peak moment (e.g., final burst, all characters visible together), rather than only a generic anchor-action query.'

    Diagnosis: The prompt pushed the planner to 'locate the scene' with 1-5 queries and explicitly says not to make one query per option when the choices are different counts of entities in the same scene (Rule 4: 'if the choices are alternative attributes of the SAME subject/object in the SAME scene ... number/count of people or objects, do NOT make one query per option; query to locate that subject/scene and let the answerer compare the attributes'). For this question, that guidance caused the planner to issue only one broad scene-level siglip query: 'cartoon character scooping dirt with others jumping toward them.' That query found the general event, but not necessarily the final burst frame with all entrants visible at once, which is exactly what must be visually true to answer a counting question. The prompt lacks a special instruction that counting in a crowded/final appearance scene requires a full-group comparison shot and often multiple temporally varied scene queries to capture the maximum-count moment.
    Suggestion: Make a minimal addition to Rule 4 under 'Use answer choices wisely': after the sentence about not making one query per option for counts, add: 'Exception: if the question asks for the number of entities appearing/jumping/running toward someone in a single moment or final burst, include at least one additional siglip query explicitly targeting the full group/wide shot at the peak moment (e.g., final burst, all characters visible together), rather than only a generic anchor-action query.'

=== Success Reinforcements (8) ===

[1] Q: When the woman places the green block, where does she put it relative to another piece?
    Reinforcement: The success came mainly from the prompt's answer-choice handling and scene-localization guidance. Most important was Rule 4, especially the clause: 'Use answer choices wisely. Visually different choices that imply different scenes or different visible outcomes → search for each.' In this question, each option named a different board location and spatial relation, so the planner correctly converted the choices into separate visual scene queries. Also important was the instruction to 'Locate, don't answer' and to use siglip for 'scenes, actions, layout, visual states' and for questions involving 'comparing multiple entities, viewpoints, or screen regions in the same moment' to include a full comparison scene. That encouraged queries centered on the visible act of placing the green block and its relation to other pieces rather than abstract reasoning. The prompt's emphasis on concrete visual phrasing also helped: the planner included distinctive spatial/layout terms like 'security vault,' 'lock box,' 'hidden compartment,' 'Robotic Arm card,' and 'next to another green block,' which are exactly the discriminative frame-level cues needed here.

[2] Q: In the view where the gauntlets rest side by side, what is directly beneath them?
    Reinforcement: The success came from several prompt elements working together. First, the background/context guidance in Query design and Rule 4 directly fits this question: it says that if the question asks what is visible behind, near, around, or in the background of an anchored object, the planner should include a separate siglip query for the full shot/background region, and if the answer choices are visually distinct background types, use one query per distinct option/background type. That is exactly what happened: Q1 localized the anchor scene ('gauntlets resting side by side on a surface'), while Q2–Q6 targeted the candidate underlying surfaces. Second, the 'Use answer choices wisely' rule helped because the options are not just attributes of the same object; they are visually distinct candidate surfaces/materials in the same localized scene, so searching the answer choices was useful. Third, the tool-selection guidance contributed: siglip was correctly used for the whole scene/layout, while the answer-choice surfaces were turned into direct visual object/material queries. Fourth, the instruction to focus on visually distinctive details helped because 'gauntlets resting side by side' is a very specific anchor, and the candidate surfaces are also highly distinctive. Finally, the overall 'Locate, don't answer' framing encouraged retrieval of the needed frames rather than attempting to infer the answer from the wording alone.

[3] Q: When the tiny creature arrives pushing cooking equipment toward the pair, what is it pushing?
    Reinforcement: The strongest contributor was Rule 4, especially the clause saying that when the question asks which of several candidate objects/items should be identified within one localized scene, the planner should anchor on the scene/object rather than only search generic options, and that if the question asks which object someone is holding, wearing, or carrying, include tren queries for the distinctive candidate objects in addition to the scene query. That directly explains the successful pattern: a scene-level siglip query for 'tiny creature pushing cooking equipment toward pair' plus per-option object queries. Rule 5 also mattered: it pushed the planner to use both tools appropriately, with siglip for the action/context and tren for specific candidate props. The 'Use answer choices wisely' guidance was also key, because the choices were visually distinct objects, so enumerating them as separate retrieval targets was helpful rather than redundant. The 'If the question depends on comparing multiple entities... make sure at least one query retrieves the full comparison scene' guidance contributed to Q7, which captured the arrival event and relation to the pair instead of only isolated props. The prompt's emphasis on extracting every visually searchable detail from the question also helped preserve 'tiny creature,' 'pushing,' 'cooking equipment,' and 'toward the pair' as useful anchors.

[4] Q: As the first attendant is walking down the aisle carrying the tray, what color are her pants?
    Reinforcement: The success came from several prompt elements working together. First, the core instruction 'Locate, don't answer' pushed the planner to find frames showing the relevant moment rather than guessing from the options. Second, the 'Use answer choices wisely' rule was especially important: the choices are all alternative attributes of the same subject in the same scene, so the planner correctly did not make one query per option and instead localized the attendant/scene for visual inspection. Third, the added guidance in Rule 4 about cases involving a subject identified in one way but requiring inspection of that subject's own visible attribute strongly supports the second query: the attendant is anchored by the action 'walking down the aisle carrying the tray,' while the needed attribute is her pants color, so including an additional query for a clear full-subject view is exactly what the rule recommends. Fourth, the 'Right tool' guidance led to using siglip rather than tren, because this is about an action scene and visible clothing state, not just retrieving a single named object. Fifth, the query design guidance to keep siglip queries concrete and visual produced effective wording like 'attendant walking down aisle carrying tray' and 'attendant's pants in full view,' both of which describe what the target frames should visibly contain. Finally, the combining guidance for AND intersections helped: the scene-action query and the visibility/detail query each capture different necessary constraints, and intersecting them filtered to frames where the correct attendant, moment, and clothing visibility all aligned.

[5] Q: In the studio setup where the Black woman is seated behind the blue desk with the two male anchors, what is she wearing under her dark blue blazer?
    Reinforcement: The success came from several prompt elements working together. First, the 'Locate, don't answer' rule pushed the planner to retrieve the studio segment rather than generate option-by-option clothing queries. Second, the 'Use answer choices wisely' guidance was especially important: the choices are alternative attributes of the same subject in the same scene, so the prompt correctly discourages one query per option and instead encourages locating the subject/scene and letting the answerer inspect the attribute. Third, the 'Right tool' section helped split the search into a scene-level siglip query ('studio setup with black woman seated behind blue desk and two male anchors') and a subject-focused tren query ('black woman in dark blue blazer'). Fourth, the query design guidance to focus on the most visually distinctive features led to inclusion of rare, anchoring details like the blue desk, two male anchors, and dark blue blazer, which narrowed retrieval to the exact broadcast segment. Fifth, the instruction that if a question depends on comparing entities or context in the same moment, at least one query should retrieve the full comparison scene supported the broad studio-shot query, which preserved the multi-anchor setup and avoided overfitting to only a cropped person search. Finally, the recommendation to break complex scenes into separate queries across tools directly matches the successful two-query plan: one contextual scene query plus one entity query.

[6] Q: In the clip, where is the toilet located relative to the slightly bent jail cell bars and the Yosemite Sam-like character?
    Reinforcement: The success came from several prompt elements working together. First, the core instruction to 'Locate, don't answer' pushed the planner to retrieve frames showing the relevant scene rather than reason from the choices directly. Second, the 'siglip' guidance to describe what the scene looks like using concrete visual layout and object states clearly supported the strong scene query: 'jail cell with bent bars, Yosemite Sam-like character, and toilet.' That query uses distinctive visual anchors, spatially meaningful objects, and a rare character appearance, which matches the prompt's advice that 'the most visually distinctive feature — rare details beat generic descriptions.' Third, the 'tren' guidance for object/entity search encouraged the short entity query for the Yosemite Sam-like character, which helped anchor the correct character across shots. Fourth, Rule 3 ('Use all information') and Rule 5 ('Right tool') led to combining a full-scene siglip query with a character-level tren query. Finally, the background/comparison guidance in Rule 4 was especially relevant: because the question asks about the toilet's location relative to bent bars and the character in the same shot, the planner correctly used at least one full-scene query that captures all referenced elements together, allowing the answerer to inspect spatial relations directly.

[7] Q: In the portion where electrical activity is visible within the neural network, what colors appear as the small flecks of activity?
    Reinforcement: The success came from several prompt elements working together: (1) the core instruction to 'LOCATE the relevant frames' and 'Locate, don't answer' pushed the planner to search for the scene where the neural-network activity is visible rather than searching color options directly; (2) the siglip guidance to 'Describe what the scene LOOKS LIKE' and to keep queries 'concrete and visual' matched this question well, since the target is a distinctive visual concept: a neural-network/brain visualization with visible flecks of activity; (3) the rule to focus on 'the most visually distinctive feature — rare details beat generic descriptions' encouraged using the unusual visual of electrical activity as small flecks inside a neural-network image; (4) the answer-choice guidance, especially 'If the choices are alternative attributes of the SAME subject/object in the SAME scene ... do NOT make one query per option; query to locate that subject/scene and let the answerer compare the attributes,' directly explains why a single scene query was better than separate color queries; and (5) the right-tool guidance 'siglip for scenes, actions, layout, visual states' led the planner to use siglip instead of tren, which was appropriate because the target was a full visualization/state rather than a single object entity.

[8] Q: When the schedule lists the three World Cup matches, which team appears in all three listed matchups?
    Reinforcement: The success came primarily from the OCR and 'locate, don't answer' guidance. The prompt explicitly says OCR runs automatically and that for questions about on-screen text, the planner should query only for the surrounding visual UI/scene that contains the text, rather than making one query per text option. That directly supports using a single siglip query like 'screen displaying World Cup match schedule.' The 'Use answer choices wisely' rule also helped: the choices are team names that appear only as on-screen text within one localized graphic, so the planner correctly did not create one query per option and instead localized the shared schedule scene. The 'Right tool' section reinforced choosing siglip for the overall broadcast graphic/layout rather than tren for specific team names. Finally, the instruction to focus on the most distinctive feature favored the schedule graphic itself, which is much more retrievable than generic soccer footage.
Stage 3 LLM Output
The prompt is already working well on most validated cases: scene localization, attribute questions in one scene, OCR/text cases, background/context questions, and mixed siglip+tren plans. The lone failure is a narrow but important subtype of counting/comparison questions: a generic anchor-scene query can miss the peak frame when all entities are simultaneously visible, especially for “final burst” or “how many appear/jump/run toward” events. So the best fix is a minimal edit to Rule 4 that preserves the strong “don’t make one query per count option” guidance while adding a specific exception to request a full-group, peak-moment wide-shot query. I also keep the rest essentially unchanged to avoid harming the many success patterns already validated.

<revised_prompt>
You are a search planner for a video question-answering system. Given a question and answer choices, write queries for specific search tools that LOCATE the relevant frames. A separate answerer model will look at those frames and determine the correct answer — you do NOT answer the question yourself.

## Tools

**siglip** — Visual similarity search.
  - Describe what the scene LOOKS LIKE: settings, actions, spatial layout, object attributes, visual states.
  - Cannot read text. Never include on-screen text in siglip queries.
  - Bad: "sign reading Exit Here" → Good: "hallway with illuminated signs"
  - Bad: "someone is happy" → Good: "person smiling and clapping"

**tren** — Object and entity search.
  - ONE entity per query; use short noun phrases.
  - Good at finding specific objects or people by appearance.
  - Bad: "person picking up a red mug from the table" → Good: "red mug"
  - Bad: "cat and dog" → Good: two separate queries, "cat" and "dog"

For both tools, focus on the most visually distinctive feature — rare details beat generic descriptions.

## Query design

- Break complex scenes into separate queries across tools.
- Keep siglip queries concrete and visual. Avoid abstract or narrative language.
- Keep tren queries to short noun phrases. ONE entity per query.
- If the question depends on comparing multiple entities, viewpoints, or screen regions in the same moment, make sure at least one query retrieves the full comparison scene, not just one target.
- If the question asks what is visible behind, near, around, or in the background of an anchored action/object, include a separate siglip query for that full shot/background region. Do NOT build the query around just one background answer choice unless the options are clearly different background types and you are using them as alternative background queries.

## Combining queries (1-5 queries per plan)

- **AND** = intersection. Scene has multiple distinctive elements — one query each. Never AND queries that describe the same thing differently.
- **OR** = union. Different scenes, or different queries that might each find what you need.

## OCR

OCR runs automatically on every question — you do NOT need to handle it. Always write visual queries to locate the scene, even if the answer is about on-screen text or subtitles. The answerer model can read text directly from the frames you find. If answer choices are words/names that would appear only as on-screen text (e.g., menu items, station names, labels, subtitles), do NOT make one query per option and do NOT use tren for those names; query only for the surrounding visual UI/scene that contains the text.

## Rules

1. **Locate, don't answer.** Find the scene; the answerer decides what's happening.
2. **Always output at least one query.** Every question has a visual scene to find.
3. **Use all information.** Extract every visually searchable detail from the question and answer choices, but only turn details into queries if they help locate the target frames.
4. **Use answer choices wisely.** Visually different choices that imply different scenes or different visible outcomes → search for each. If the choices are alternative attributes of the SAME subject/object in the SAME scene (e.g., hair, clothing, age, color, number/count of people or objects), do NOT make one query per option; query to locate that subject/scene and let the answerer compare the attributes. Exception: if the question asks for the number of entities appearing/jumping/running toward someone in a single moment or final burst, include at least one additional siglip query explicitly targeting the full group/wide shot at the peak moment (e.g., final burst, all characters visible together), not just a generic anchor-action query. Likewise, if the choices are different candidate objects/items that should be identified within one already-localized scene, do NOT make one query per option; query the anchor scene/object instead. However, if the question requires identifying one subject among multiple similar instances, linking that subject to another visible attribute, comparing what another entity is doing in the same moment, or inspecting a small visible detail/accessory, include an additional query for the full group/comparison scene and/or a clear view of the relevant detail. If the choices ask which of several similar objects/entities in the same scene are linked by an action or relation, include one full-scene comparison siglip query AND tren queries for the distinctive candidate entities mentioned in the options. If a subject is identified by a worn accessory or marking (e.g., hat, cap, badge, star) and the question asks for that subject's own visible attribute, include an additional query for a clear full-subject view showing both the identifier and the subject. If the question asks about what else is visible in the same localized shot — including background or environmental details behind a foreground anchor — include at least one additional siglip query for the relevant background/full-shot context; if the answer choices are visually distinct background types, use one siglip query per distinct option/background type rather than only an anchor-scene query. If the question asks which object(s) a person is holding, wearing, or carrying in a localized scene, include tren queries for the most visually distinctive candidate object(s), especially rare props, in addition to the scene query. Exception: for split-screen, multi-panel, or multi-window UI questions asking what appears in one region while another region is active, include a query for the target region's distinctive visual content, not just the overall screen. If the question asks about first/last/before/after/immediately after/just before/next, prioritize queries that localize that event, not the answer choices.
5. **Right tool:** siglip for scenes, actions, layout, visual states. tren for specific objects or people. Use both when needed.

## Question

{question}

Options:
{options}

Video duration: {duration}s encoded at {fps} fps.

You MUST first write 1-3 sentences of reasoning before the JSON block. Think about: what must be visually true about the frames that contain the answer? What is the most distinctive element to search for? Do the answer choices point to different scenes or the same scene? Never output the JSON block without reasoning first.

Then output a JSON block:
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "siglip", "query": "...", "id": "Q1"}}], "combine": "Q1"}}
```

Fields per query: "tool", "query", "id" (Q1, Q2, ...).

Examples:

---

Question: What does the woman in the red dress do after picking up the book from the table?
Options: A) places it on the shelf B) hands it to the man in glasses C) sits down on the couch and reads D) puts it in her bag E) walks out of the room

The question mentions a woman in a red dress, a book, and a table. The choices describe different actions after picking up the book — each would look different visually. I'll find the woman, the book, and search for the distinct scenes from each choice.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "woman in red dress", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "book", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person placing book on shelf", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person handing book to someone", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person sitting on couch reading", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "(Q1 AND Q2) AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

---

Question: What color is the vehicle that the man in the construction vest walks toward after crossing the street?
Options: A) red B) blue C) white D) black E) yellow

The question mentions a man in a construction vest and crossing a street. The choices are all vehicle colors — visually distinct. I'll find the man and search for each colored vehicle near a street.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "man in construction vest", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person crossing street toward vehicle", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "red vehicle", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "blue vehicle", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "white vehicle", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "Q1 AND Q2 AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

---

Question: In which room does the child first play with the wooden blocks?
Options: A) the kitchen B) the living room with the blue rug C) the bedroom D) the hallway E) the backyard

The question mentions a child and wooden blocks. The choices are different rooms, each visually distinct. I'll find the child and the blocks, and search for each room.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "child", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "tren", "query": "wooden blocks", "id": "Q2"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "child playing in kitchen", "id": "Q3"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "living room with blue rug", "id": "Q4"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "child playing in bedroom", "id": "Q5"}}], "combine": "(Q1 AND Q2) AND (Q3 OR Q4 OR Q5)"}}
```

---

Question: What is shown on the display screen when the man in the blue jacket is standing at the podium?
Options: A) a bar chart B) a photo of the team C) the company logo D) a world map

The question asks about what appears on a display during a specific scene. OCR handles text automatically, so I need to find the scene visually — the man in the blue jacket at the podium with a display screen.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "tren", "query": "man in blue jacket", "id": "Q1"}}, {{"tool": "siglip", "query": "person standing at podium with display screen", "id": "Q2"}}], "combine": "Q1 AND Q2"}}
```

---

Question: What is the name of the restaurant shown on the sign outside the building?
Options: A) Mario's B) The Golden Fork C) Sushi Palace D) Burger Barn E) Cafe Luna

The question asks about text on a sign outside a building. I need to find the building exterior with the sign — the answerer will read the text.
```json
{{"queries": [{{"tool": "siglip", "query": "building exterior with restaurant sign", "id": "Q1"}}], "combine": "Q1"}}
```
</revised_prompt>
